Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
12.1  Introduction
Pathology data are essential elements of toxicity studies. 
Pathology is a branch of medical science dedicated to evaluat-
ing all aspects of disease. Toxicology is a branch of medical 
science that investigates the impact of substances (typically 
xenobiotics) on cells, organs, tissues, whole organisms, and 
even entire ecosystems. Both pathology and toxicology place 
particular emphasis on finding causes (etiologies), defining 
effects (biochemical/molecular, functional, and structural), 
understanding mechanisms (pathogenesis), and predicting the 
evolution (progression) of changes produced by potentially 
harmful materials (“toxicants”). Toxicologic pathology is best 
comprehended as the medical specialty which unites pathol-
ogy and toxicology into a single discipline where the tools of 
pathology are used to assess the effects of xenobiotics, act-
ing alone or in combination, on cells, organs, tissues, whole 
organisms, or ecosystems. Proficiency in toxicologic pathol-
ogy requires a broad education in which knowledge of basic 
cellular and molecular processes as well as general (whole ani-
mal) anatomy and physiology in health are bolstered by com-
prehensive familiarity with clinical medicine, pharmacology, 
and toxicology as they relate to substance-induced disease 
states. This foundation necessarily includes an understand-
ing of comparative biology since the pathology evaluation for 
toxicity studies usually is performed in one or more laboratory 
animal species to support human safety and risk assessment.
Pathology endpoints used in toxicity testing are best under-
stood as screening tools to identify organ-specific or systemic 
perturbations in homeostatic or metabolic processes. The 
results of pathology testing offer insight regarding the nature, 
extent, and mechanism(s) of xenobiotic-induced pathologic 
processes. For animal toxicity studies, pathology observations 
typically reflect contributions from two pathology specialties. 
Clinical pathologists generally examine test article–related 
changes in fluids and isolated cells using samples collected 
intermittently during life and/or at necropsy. Anatomic 
pathologists usually make macroscopic (“gross”) observations 
at necropsy, interpret organ weights gathered at necropsy, and 
evaluate tissue sections using various microscopic techniques. 
Anatomic pathology and/or clinical pathology analyses often 
discover and characterize findings (or patterns of findings) that 
serve to illuminate major target organs, establish threshold 
values (e.g., no observed effect level [NOEL] or no observed 
adverse effect level [NOAEL]), and provide insight regarding 
whether or not test article–related effects should be classified 
as adverse.108,109 Interpretation of pathology data requires that 
correlations be made between related clinical pathology and 
anatomic pathology findings (and other relevant in-life data 
such as clinical observations and neurological and ophthal-
mic examination findings).114 In short, toxicologic pathology is

Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
critical to both identifying possible hazards and to the effective 
data comparison across species needed for risk assessment. 
Success in toxicologic pathology, and indeed in toxicology, 
requires that pathology findings be communicated clearly, 
accurately, and succinctly.
The current chapter is designed to provide a basic appre-
ciation for the principles and practices utilized to generate 
pathology data for toxicity studies. Concepts include (1) the 
major categories of pathology endpoints, (2) appropriate study 
designs and methods used to assess them, (3) key points to 
consider in interpreting, integrating, and communicating 
pathology data, and (4) quality assurance (QA) practices in 
toxicologic pathology. These concepts are relevant in general 
terms to the development of any product, including agricul-
tural and industrial chemicals, drugs (biologics and small mol-
ecules), engrafted (cells and organs) and implanted (medical 
devices) materials, genome-modifying treatments (gene edit-
ing and gene therapy), and physical agents (radiation), to name 
a few. Comprehensive discussion of toxicologic pathology 
principles and practices for particular organs, test ­species, or 
toxicant classes are beyond the scope of this chapter. Further 
details on these topics may be found in standard reference 
books for general and toxicologic pathology.144,156,193,294,347 
Given the plethora of abbreviations in common use for pathol-
ogy terms, a table of abbreviations is provided for the conve-
nience of readers (Table 12.1).
12.2  Study Design Considerations for 
Pathology Endpoints in Toxicity Studies
12.2.1  Overview
The designs of standard animal studies, whether or not per-
formed in compliance with Good Laboratory Practice (GLP) 
guidance, readily accommodate the acquisition of samples 
suitable for routine pathology endpoints. This section provides 
recommendations for sample collection, personnel quali-
fications, and quality control (QC) as applied to pathology 
parameters. The discussion is broken into two parts: clinical 
pathology and anatomic pathology. Requirements for clinical 
pathology analyses are presented before those for anatomic 
pathology evaluation because typical toxicity studies permit 
clinical pathology sampling intermittently throughout the 
course of a study, both during life (antemortem) and as a ter-
minal procedure (post mortem), but limit anatomic pathology 
sampling to the post mortem phase. Integration of data from 
these pathology specialties is essential in efficacy and safety 
testing, especially for detecting target organ toxicity and estab-
lishing dose–response relationships.
12.2.2  Categories of Pathology Data 
in Toxicity Studies
The majority of pathology endpoints and the procedures used 
to assess them are identical to those used in diagnostic pathol-
ogy practice in the medical and veterinary medical settings. 
The main differences between their use in the diagnostic and 
toxicologic pathology settings are that pathology samples and 
endpoints for toxicity studies may be chosen in advance and 
compared with data from concurrent control animals to opti-
mize the sensitivity and overall utility of the assessment.
12.2.2.1  Clinical Pathology
Clinical pathology analysis uses batteries of qualitative and 
quantitative tests to explore the health and state (i.e., tissue/cel-
lular integrity and/or function) of various body systems at the 
point in time at which the sample is collected. A more dynamic 
view of organ/tissue state over time may be gained by intermit-
tent sampling, including acquisition of one or more pre-study 
(“baseline”) specimens to let each animal serve as its own 
control. Such pre-study samples typically are obtained from 
non-rodents (e.g., rabbits, dogs, minipigs, nonhuman primates 
[NHPs]) due to their larger blood volume, which allows repeated 
sampling without a negative impact on the animals’ health.
Most toxicity studies evaluate hematology, clinical chem-
istry, coagulation, and urinalysis parameters as the minimal 
clinical pathology assessment.355 Microscopic (i.e., “cytologi-
cal”) evaluation of major cytoarchitectural features may be 
performed on isolated cells and other materials (e.g., bacteria, 
crystals) in blood smears, other body fluids (e.g., cerebrospinal 
or synovial fluid), bone marrow smears, tissue squash or touch 
preparations, or urine sediment. The kinds of procedures and 
values of clinical pathology endpoints vary with the species.305
12.2.2.2  Anatomic Pathology
Anatomic pathology evaluation involves qualitative or semi-
quantitative observations or quantitative measurements of cell, 
organ, or tissue structure as a means of assessing organ or sys-
tem function. In conventional animal-based efficacy and toxic-
ity studies, these endpoints are examined at necropsy (autopsy) 
or in specimens obtained at necropsy. In principle, samples of 
cells or tissues may be acquired during life, but such collec-
tions are seldom included in the study plan for routine toxicity 
studies.
The usual data types gathered during anatomic pathol-
ogy evaluation encompass qualitative descriptions of body 
and organ features as well as quantitative measurements of 
organ size (e.g., weights).62 Macroscopic (“gross”) observa-
tions encompass the color, shape, size, and sometimes smell 
of organs and tissues as assessed during the necropsy. Organ 
weights are an easy quantitative means of assessing alterations 
in organs, which may be either increased or decreased from 
normal. Microscopic (or “histopathologic”) evaluation of cell, 
organ, and tissue characteristics (e.g., appearance, organiza-
tion, and numbers) in thin tissue sections is the gold standard 
for generating pathology data in toxicity studies.
Ultrastructural evaluation (mainly transmission electron 
microscopy [TEM] but sometimes scanning electron micros-
copy [SEM]) is a special anatomic pathology approach that 
sometimes is added to the study plan for toxicity studies to 
investigate particular questions. Tissue samples for TEM and 
SEM require special processing techniques, sectioning equip-
ment (for TEM), and analytical instruments. A detailed dis-
cussion of these considerations is outside the scope of this 
chapter, but interested readers may explore these methods as

applied to biology and diagnostic pathology in standard refer-
ence texts.5,52,81,138,157,180
Other special techniques in anatomic pathology may be 
employed during toxicity studies to address specific issues. 
Examples include confocal microscopy,88,265 morphometry 
(also called “histomorphometry”),136,166 and stereology.40,231 
Confocal microscopy and stereology are means for explor-
ing three-dimensional tissue structures using a series of two-
dimensional optical or physical sections. These two methods 
require considerable attention to detail in specimen handling, 
processing, and analysis. Morphometry may be performed at 
the macroscopic or microscopic level to measure the areal or 
linear dimensions of structures and is amenable to use with rou-
tinely harvested and processed samples. However, morphom-
etry does require a high degree of homology across specimens 
and does not provide the systematic uniform random sampling 
that is the main advantage of unbiased stereological analysis.
12.2.2.3  Molecular Pathology
Special procedures to permit in situ evaluation of molecular 
endpoints may be used for both clinical pathology (cytological 
preparations) and anatomic pathology (histopathological and 
if warranted TEM preparations). The advantage of such tech-
niques is that alterations in molecular expression may be visu-
alized in specific cell populations, organ domains, or tissue 
components to anatomically localize the signal. These end-
points are utilized in animal studies to confirm efficacy (i.e., 
pharmacodynamics [PD] biomarkers), evaluate cell numbers 
and tissue features (e.g., cell type-specific markers), explore 
tissue reactions (e.g., numbers and types of infiltrating or resi-
dent inflammatory cells), and probe disease mechanisms (e.g., 
biomarkers for cell death or proliferation, “–omics” signatures 
in toxic conditions [see Chapter 7]). Molecular pathology 
methods are included in the study protocol on a case-by-case 
basis to answer specific questions.
Common molecular pathology methods used in animal tox-
icity studies may be performed utilizing routinely processed 
samples.273 The main methods used in conventional animal 
toxicity studies are immunohistochemistry (IHC), in situ 
hybridization (ISH, including fluorescent ISH [FISH]), and 
enzyme histochemistry (EHC). These techniques measure the 
presence of a protein or sometimes another molecule (IHC), 
the presence of mRNA (ISH) or chromosomal DNA (FISH) 
sequences, and the presence of functional protein (EHC). 
Principal considerations for proper specimen acquisition, 
handling, and processing to support the application of these 
three workhorse methods in routine animal toxicity studies are 
introduced below. Detailed coverage of the fundamental prin-
ciples for these procedures is outside the scope of this chapter 
but may be gleaned from standard reference texts.29,47,67,167,207,
278,307,343,364
Additional molecular pathology techniques may be per-
formed if warranted, although these methods typically are 
confined to investigational studies rather than routine efficacy 
or toxicity assays. For example, some molecular methods per-
mit amplification of rare molecules within a tissue section 
(e.g., in situ polymerase chain reaction [PCR]) or a precision-
isolated cell field (e.g., laser capture microdissection [LCM]). 
Others permit the analysis of molecules (e.g., matrix-assisted 
laser desorption/ionization [MALDI] mass spectrometry) or 
their kinetics (e.g., laser scanning cytometry [LSC]) within a 
microscopic field. Whole-body autoradiography may be uti-
lized to qualitatively or quantitatively follow the distribution of 
radiolabeled test articles and metabolites throughout the ani-
mal. Again, further information on these techniques may be 
obtained in the scientific literature.20,24,65,147,164,271,272,306,316,317,373
12.2.3  Regulatory Guidance and Best Practice 
Recommendations for Pathology Sampling
Regulatory guidance and best practice recommendations with 
respect to pathology testing during toxicity studies are shaped 
by many factors. The most important drivers are the type of 
pathology endpoint, study design, species, and regulatory 
authority. Institutions often have some flexibility in designing 
the toxicologic pathology evaluation to fit their programmatic 
needs. In general, the pathology evaluation should be designed 
so that it addresses regulatory expectations but also provides 
sufficient data to support evidence-based decisions regarding 
the efficacy and potential toxicity of test articles.
12.2.3.1  Clinical Pathology
The selection of appropriate clinical pathology tests for tox-
icity studies ultimately depends on the study objective. If the 
purpose is simply to screen novel test articles for potential 
toxicity in the liver, the clinical pathology assessment might 
be limited to a few liver-specific tests such as serum activities 
of alanine aminotransferase (ALT) and sorbitol dehydroge-
nase (SDH) and/or the serum total bilirubin (TBil) con-
centration. In contrast, safety studies to support regulatory 
review and approval of new entities with potential human 
exposure (e.g., drugs, food additives, industrial chemicals, 
pesticides), batteries of tests generally are required and/
or recommended in guidance given by various regulatory 
agencies, international consortia, and professional societ-
ies.335 The published guidelines are generally similar with 
respect to clinical pathology testing, but notable differences 
and instances of ambiguous or inappropriate testing require-
ments can be seen when details in guidance documents are 
compared critically.
12.2.3.1.1  Regulatory Guidance for 
Clinical Pathology Testing
For acute toxicity studies (e.g., single-dose toxicokinetic [TK] 
or 1–2 week lead candidate selection studies), regulatory guid-
ance does not require clinical pathology testing although it 
is not uncommon to include basic clinical pathology evalua-
tions (e.g., hematology and clinical chemistry) in such stud-
ies. Therefore, the sponsor chooses whether or not to assess 
clinical pathology endpoints (and which ones) during dose 
range-finding studies where animals are exposed once to a 
relatively high dose of test article and then observed for 1 or 
2 weeks. Information regarding alterations in clinical pathol-
ogy parameters following acute, high-dose exposures may be 
useful in predicting potential effects that might occur dur-
ing longer term, repeat-dose toxicity studies aiding in study

610
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
design. In our experience, clinical pathology assessments in 
this context are most often limited to hematology and clinical 
chemistry endpoints.
For repeat-dose toxicity studies, regulatory guidance 
requires some clinical pathology testing, although the battery 
of suggested tests differs among regulatory bodies and study 
designs. For example, the U.S. Food and Drug Administration 
(FDA) recommends hematology, clinical chemistry, and uri-
nalysis endpoints for acute (14- to 28-day), subchronic (90-
day), and chronic (12 or more months) studies in both rodents 
and non-rodents, including rodent lifetime carcinogenicity 
bioassays but excluding special studies like stand-alone DART 
(developmental and reproductive toxicity) and neurotoxic-
ity screens.121 In contrast, the U.S. Environmental Protection 
Agency (EPA) recommends that hematology, clinical chemis-
try, and coagulation endpoints are assessed for short-term (28- 
and 90-day) repeat-dose studies in all species.94–99 Urinalysis 
is required by EPA for all repeat-dose toxicity studies in non-
rodents but only for chronic toxicity studies (i.e., 12 months 
or more) in rodents, though it is recommended for shorter 
term rodent experiments.95–98,101 For special studies, EPA 
does not include clinical pathology testing (e.g., stand-alone 
DART screens,99 immunotoxicity studies,106 or neurotoxicity 
tests104,105) or limits testing (e.g., hematology only for stand-
alone rodent carcinogenicity bioassays102); EPA does recom-
mend hematology, clinical chemistry, and urinalysis testing 
for combination studies where repeat-dose toxicity is evalu-
ated together with either DART or carcinogenicity.100,103 The 
Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development 
(OECD) – a multinational consortium (but not a regulatory 
agency) dedicated to producing globally accepted product 
development guidance – has released recommendations for 
clinical pathology testing in toxicity studies that are simi-
lar to those provided by EPA244–246,249–252 except that OECD 
leaves the decision regarding whether or not to perform clini-
cal pathology tests during stand-alone rodent carcinogenicity 
studies to the discretion of the study director250 and suggests 
that thyroid hormone levels be considered for evaluation for 
some repeat-dose toxicity studies in rodents, including adults 
and pups in DART screens.245,248
12.2.3.1.2  Best Practice Recommendations 
for Clinical Pathology Testing
The global toxicologic pathology community has generated 
“best practice” recommendations designed to both address the 
general direction provided by regulatory guidance and offer 
specific advice regarding the minimum standard for clinical 
pathology analysis in animal toxicity studies. Expert work-
ing groups of toxicologic clinical pathologists have defined 
generally applicable core batteries for hematology, clinical 
chemistry, coagulation, and urinalysis testing in toxicity stud-
ies.312,335,336,355 The expert groups also have devised clinical 
pathology recommendations to address focused questions, 
such as the appropriate practices for bone marrow evalua-
tion,279 a menu of tests to assess hepatic function,37 and advice 
to limit clinical pathology evaluation in rodent carcinogenic-
ity studies to collection of blood smears, which may be exam-
ined if needed to identify the cell lineage for hematopoietic 
neoplasms.371
Clinical pathology tests commonly used in conventional 
toxicity studies are listed in Table 12.2. The hematology end-
points examine the health of multiple blood cells and their 
precursors. The menu of clinical chemistry analytes in blood 
assesses the integrity of the principal excretory (kidney, liver) 
and metabolic (bone, liver, pancreatic islets) organs. Where 
multiple options exist, regulatory guidance permits the study 
director to select a subset (typically two to three analytes) of 
tests (e.g., ALT and aspartate aminotransferase [AST], alka-
line phosphatase [ALP], gamma-glutamyl transferase [GGT, 
mainly measured in non-rodents], and SDH [assessed in mini-
pigs] for liver) to examine organ function. Coagulation assays 
screen the functions of cells and proteins involved in hemo-
stasis. Urinalysis primarily surveys kidney function. Further 
details on these tests are given below (see Section 12.3, 
“Clinical Pathology Tests”).
Additional non-standard but well-characterized tests may 
be needed to evaluate potential test article–related effects, 
depending on the study objectives and the known or suspected 
actions of the test article. Details regarding these options may 
be found in standard references.196,322The list of potentially 
useful clinical pathology tests will continue to expand because 
of industrial and regulatory agency interest in improving the 
menu of biomarkers to detect and characterize the efficacy and 
toxicity of new entities. Information regarding such innovative 
endpoints may be obtained from the recent scientific literature. 
Such tests should be employed to achieve a specific purpose, 
and only after the method has been confirmed to be relevant to 
the animal species in question.
12.2.3.2  Anatomic Pathology
For acute toxicity studies, which are often single-dose studies, 
regulatory guidance recommends body weight assessments 
and a thorough gross examination (e.g., organs as well as body 
surfaces and cavities) at necropsy. Microscopic evaluation of 
macroscopic findings “may be considered” to obtain addi-
tional information.93,247
For repeat-dose toxicity studies, regulatory guidance rec-
ommends a comprehensive anatomic pathology evaluation. 
The recommendations for this assessment are relatively con-
sistent across regulatory agencies and study designs, and they 
may be implemented readily by following best practice proce-
dures generated by committees of expert toxicologic pathol-
ogists (e.g., those produced by the Society of Toxicologic 
Pathology [STP]44). Total body weight is measured prior to the 
study, at least weekly during the study for the first 13 weeks 
and at least monthly thereafter, and at necropsy. Selected 
organs (Table 12.3) are weighed as soon as possible at nec-
ropsy, with paired organs (and closely intermingled organs, 
such as thyroid glands and parathyroid glands) being weighed 
together. Recommendations for which organs to weigh vary 
with the study design and species.95,101,309 Some institutions 
prefer to weigh brain after fixation to reduce the extent of 
pressure-induced neuronal artifacts associated with handling 
unfixed brain tissue. Organs to be weighed must be prepared 
identically for all animals; adrenal gland and kidney weights, 
among others, are sensitive to the amount of excess fat that 
remains attached to the capsule, and brain weights in rodents

vary substantially depending on whether the olfactory bulbs 
(which comprise 6%–7% of the organ mass34) are included 
when weighing. Numerous organs and tissues are harvested 
(Table 12.3) and preserved using an appropriate fixative to 
permit later microscopic evaluation.44 The regional variation 
in organ complexity is acknowledged in some guidance docu-
ments by recommendations to specifically sample particular 
areas (e.g., cerebral cortex, hippocampus, cerebellum, and 
pons/medulla oblongata for brain95,105); societies of toxicologic 
pathology have produced “best practice” recommendations for 
tissue collection and processing of several organs to encom-
pass regulatory guidance while ensuring a consistent and thor-
ough evaluation.34,35,44,187,228,292 Extensive collection of tissues 
is recommended at necropsy even if no microscopic evaluation 
is planned since samples not fixed and placed in the sample 
archive at this time will be lost forever. Further details on ana-
tomic pathology endpoints are given below (see Section 12.4, 
“Anatomic Pathology Endpoints”).
12.2.3.3  Molecular Pathology
In general, regulatory guidance does not require collection 
of fluid or tissue samples for molecular pathology testing. 
Guidance for some types of toxicity studies does suggest that 
IHC for cell type-specific biomarkers may be appropriate meth-
ods to help characterize test article–related changes, subject to 
the professional judgment of the study pathologist. Examples 
of this approach by regulatory bodies include the EPA sugges-
tion that IHC for glial fibrillary acidic protein (GFAP) may 
aid in localizing test article–related findings in the central ner-
vous system (CNS)104 and FDA statements that IHC may be a 
sensitive ancillary means for evaluating the distribution and/
or effects of novel cell and gene therapy products119 as well as 
supporting the qualification of new biomarkers.118 Molecular 
pathology methods often may be employed on the same tissue 
samples used for routine microscopic evaluation, but parallel 
specimens of the same tissue may be necessary if molecular 
expression is to be both quantified (e.g., by enzyme-linked 
immunosorbent assay [ELISA] or Northern or Western blot) 
and localized (e.g., by IHC or ISH) and/or because tissue fixa-
tion may damage fragile molecular markers.
12.2.4  Pathology Sampling in Routine 
Toxicity Studies
Toxicologic pathology evaluations are designed to provide a 
“whole animal” assessment of health following exposure to 
test articles. The sampling strategy is influenced by such fac-
tors as the animal species, endpoint of interest, and invasive-
ness of the collection procedure.
12.2.4.1  Sample Types
For routine toxicity studies, clinical pathology specimens typi-
cally consist of fluids and isolated cells collected either during 
life or at necropsy, while anatomic pathology specimens usu-
ally comprise tissue pieces obtained at necropsy. Other kinds 
of anatomic pathology data (i.e., macroscopic observations, 
organ weights) are acquired at necropsy but involve visual 
inspection and real-time measurements rather than sample 
collection for later analysis.
12.2.4.1.1  Clinical Pathology
Fluids are the primary specimens acquired for clinical pathol-
ogy testing in toxicity studies both during life and at nec-
ropsy. The most common samples in animal studies are blood

In practice, CROs routinely prepare SOPs for toxicologic pathology endpoints to collect and perform microscopic evaluations on most tissues in this 
list for all species.
(Continued )

614
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
(sometimes processed to serum and/or plasma) and urine, but 
if necessary, other fluids also may be obtained (e.g., cerebro-
spinal fluid [CSF]). Saliva, semen, and aqueous or vitreous 
humors often are collected from humans for toxicology test-
ing in diagnostic, forensic, and occupational investigations, 
but these fluids generally are not examined in animal-based 
studies.
Blood may be obtained from many sites, the choice of 
which depends on the analytes (i.e., endpoints) of interest, ani-
mal species, and timing.78,261 In rodents, common options are 
intracardiac puncture or sampling from the vena cava or aorta 
(performed at necropsy immediately after euthanasia) and 
venipuncture using a large superficial vein (e.g., jugular vein, 
lateral tail vein, saphenous vein) in conscious animals or the 
retro-orbital venous plexus in anesthetized animals (although 
the retro-orbital plexus generally is avoided in modern toxic-
ity studies for humane reasons). The sample size depends on 
the chosen site, with more being available via the retro-orbital 
plexus and heart routes. All these sites are suitable for basic 
hematology and clinical chemistry analytes, but the retro-
orbital plexus typically should be avoided when measuring 
coagulation parameters since the relatively greater degree of 
tissue factor released by tissue trauma during blood collec-
tion may initiate coagulation in the container. In non-rodents, 
blood collection typically is undertaken using a large superfi-
cial vein (e.g., cephalic, saphenous).
For blood of all species, the choice of container to hold the 
sample depends on the analytes to be quantified (Table 12.4). 
In the toxicology laboratory, the containers have color-
coded caps to minimize the possibility of sampling errors. 
Hematology endpoints typically are assessed in whole blood 
mixed with an anticoagulant like ethylenediaminetetraacetic 
acid (EDTA, lavender cap) or heparin (green cap). Clinical 
chemistry endpoints are quantified in either plasma (i.e., the 
fluid remaining after an anticoagulant-treated sample has been 
centrifuged) or serum (i.e., the fluid extruded from a contract-
ing clot in blood that has not been mixed with an anticoagu-
lant [red or gold cap]). Coagulation endpoints are measured 
in blood samples that have been mixed with the anticoagu-
lant citrate (blue cap). Citrate is used for coagulation testing 
since it selectively but reversibly binds calcium (Ca2+) but does 
not bind other divalent ions needed for clotting (e.g., copper); 
other anticoagulants permanently reduce coagulation in blood 
samples by either irreversibly binding Ca2+ and other divalent 
ions (EDTA) or enhance the inhibitory activity of antithrom-
bin versus several enzymes needed for coagulation (heparin). 
For routine efficacy and toxicity studies, blood typically is 
collected into tubes containing EDTA (lavender cap), a serum 
separator with no anticoagulant (red cap), and a citrate tube 
(blue top) if coagulation is to be assessed.
Urine may be obtained from animals by several means.195 
For routine toxicity testing, metabolism caging or under-
cage collection systems (shallow pans) are used to collect 
urine over defined time periods (e.g., 0 to 6–12 hours, over-
night). Care should be taken to minimize sample contami-
nation by environmental debris (e.g., dirt, hair), food, and 
drinking water. While routinely used across industry, these 
techniques are prone to pre-analytical variability. In some 
circumstances (e.g., where smaller numbers of animals are 
being collected), other means of obtaining urine samples 
such as cystocentesis or manual voiding may be performed 
at necropsy instead.
Collection of CSF during animal toxicity studies typically 
is performed where the test article has been administered 
directly into the brain by intraparenchymal injection or CSF 
by intrathecal injection into a brain ventricle or the lumbar cis-
tern. Samples may be harvested during life from anesthetized 
animals.163,209,311 Specimens are placed in plastic or silicone-
coated glass tubes that contain no preservative.
Cells suitable for cytological evaluation usually are collected 
in toxicity studies, but in most cases are assessed under spe-
cific circumstances (e.g., to characterize flagged values from 
hematology instruments or unexplained hematology alterations 
suggestive of effects on bone marrow). The most common cell 
preparations are blood smears and bone marrow smears.279 The 
preparations often are stained and archived for possible future 
analysis. Squash and touch preparations (made, respectively, by 
smashing a small tissue piece between two slides or applying 
a slide to the surface of a freshly made cut through an organ) 
typically are not generated during toxicity studies.
12.2.4.1.2  Anatomic Pathology
Tissue collection for animal toxicity studies typically occurs 
at necropsy, although sampling during life may be performed 
in rare cases by utilizing percutaneous needle biopsies in 
conscious animals or excisional biopsies in anesthetized ani-
mals. Prior to anatomic pathology sampling, the macroscopic 
(“gross”) analysis is completed and organ weights are col-
lected. The approach used for tissue acquisition and preserva-
tion depends on the anatomic pathology endpoints chosen for 
evaluation. Identical considerations apply to samples collected 
at necropsy and during life.

615
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
The choices for the fixation procedure in animal efficacy 
and toxicity studies depend on the evaluation to be per-
formed.202 For general toxicity studies, tissues are usually 
fixed by immersion in aldehyde-based (cross-linking) fixatives 
since this method is fast and simple.185 The workhorse fixative 
for most studies is neutral buffered 10% formalin (NBF, pH 
7.4) because it provides a reasonable balance between rapid 
penetration and tissue firmness, thereby affording suitable 
preservation for nearly all immersed organs. Other aldehyde-
containing fixatives (see Section 12.4.1.5 on “Tissue Fixation” 
for common options) may be preferred for especially dense or 
lipid-rich tissues, such as Bouin’s solution, Davidson’s solu-
tion, or modified Davidson’s solution for eyes and testes.199,201 
Aldehyde-containing fixatives may be introduced by intravas-
cular perfusion when rapid delivery of fixative solution to deep 
tissues is necessary.31,86 In general, perfusion is only used for 
special studies (e.g., dedicated neurotoxicity assays) due to the 
low throughput and need for special equipment and highly 
trained personnel.
Frozen tissues may be procured when warranted where spe-
cial questions must be answered using anatomic pathology tech-
niques. Examples include evaluation of target expression or test 
article distribution using IHC or EHC methods. In such cases, 
unfixed tissue blocks are obtained, trimmed, covered in medium 
(e.g., OCT [Optimal Cutting Temperature] compound), and 
snap-frozen by immersion in liquid nitrogen-cooled isopentane. 
Tissue should not be placed directly into liquid nitrogen since 
the thermal differential between the warm tissue and liquid 
will form a vapor barrier that actually retards freezing. Frozen 
samples then are wrapped and held at –80°C until sectioning. 
The frozen sections are fixed by brief immersion in an aldehyde 
solution or a coagulating fixative (e.g., acetone, methanol).
12.2.4.2  Sample Timing and Frequency
In general, the timing and frequency of sampling are primarily 
a concern when blood is to be collected intermittently during 
life. The timing and frequency may be modified depending on 
the study objectives, the test species, and the biological activ-
ity of the test article.355 
Timing and frequency considerations do not apply to fluid or 
tissue samples obtained at necropsy for clinical and anatomic 
pathology endpoints. The sample dimensions do matter for 
tissue samples since excessive thickness can prevent effective 
penetration of fixative.
12.2.4.2.1  Clinical Pathology
No specific regulatory guidance exists for clinical pathology 
sampling during dose range-finding studies (where animals 
are exposed once to a relatively high dose of test article and 
then observed for 1 or 2 weeks). Information regarding acute 
high-dose changes to clinical pathology parameters can be 
useful for predicting potential effects in longer term, repeat-
dose toxicity studies. If clinical pathology samples (typically 
limited to hematology and clinical chemistry) are obtained 
in single-dose studies, the best time for collection usually 
is 48–72 h post-dose; waiting to the end of the 1- to 2-week 
observation period may allow analytes to return to baseline 
values due to the rapid regenerative capacity and large func-
tional reserves of many organs (e.g., bone marrow, kidney, and 
liver) following an acute, transient insult. In some instances, 
samples for particular clinical pathology tests may need to be 
collected earlier (within 6–24 h of exposure) due to the very 
short half-life of the analyte (e.g., cardiac biomarkers, cyto-
kines, some enzymes, peptides). However, some test articles 
may require that sampling be performed later, and at multiple 
time points, due to the slow and variable development of the 
peak response. For example, a single-dose 4-week study of a 
novel cytotoxic chemotherapeutic agent in a non-rodent typi-
cally will be designed to include collection of two pre-study 
blood samples, several interim blood samples (e.g., study days 
4, 7, 10, 14, 21), and a terminal blood sample (study day 28) 
so that hematologic analysis may be undertaken to assess the 
nadir for circulating leukocyte numbers as well as the evolu-
tion of hematopoietic recovery. In general, clinical chemistry 
analysis also will be performed on the pre-study and some 
within-study time points (e.g., study days 7, 14, and 28) to 
detect any impact on non-hematopoietic organs. In such cases, 
the clinical chemistry battery may be focused on a small sub-
set of routine tests to screen for degenerative changes in liver 
(e.g., hepatocyte cytosolic [or “leakage”] enzymes like ALT 
and AST), or it may include a full set of clinical chemistry 
tests (Table 12.2) as well as one or more novel biomarkers (e.g., 
cardiac troponin [cTn]281,302) to detect degeneration in other 
organs often targeted by anti-cancer chemotherapeutics.
For repeat-dose toxicity studies in rodents, clinical pathol-
ogy testing generally is done at necropsy and may be under-
taken at interim time points. Blood sampling at multiple time 
points may be helpful in identifying and characterizing subtle 
effects of test articles. Possible designs include interim blood 
collection at 6 weeks for 13-week studies, at 6 and 13 weeks 
for 26-week studies, and at 6, 13, and 26 weeks for 52-week 
(12-month) studies. Interim clinical pathology testing may 
not be necessary for longer term studies (e.g., 13 weeks or 
longer) if testing was performed during a short-term study 
(e.g., 4 weeks) conducted using equivalent dose levels of the 
test article. In general, clinical pathology testing is not rec-
ommended for time points in rodent studies after 52 weeks 
due to the likelihood of interpretive difficulties resulting from 
spurious abnormalities produced by spontaneous age-related 
diseases (e.g., chronic progressive nephropathy [CPN], cuta-
neous ulceration leading to systemic inflammation associated 
with large mammary gland tumors, endocrine disruption due 
to pituitary gland tumors). The exception to this rule is that 
EPA suggests preparation of blood smears at interim (12- and 
18-month) and terminal (24-month) time points as a backup 
method to determine the origin of hematopoietic neoplasms.102 
The FDA recommendations for clinical pathology testing in 
rodent carcinogenicity studies involve sampling blood and 
urine from a subset (at least 10 per sex per dose group) within 
the first 2 weeks of the study and at 13, 26, and 52 weeks.121 
Additional testing at 18 and 24 months may be warranted if 
clinical pathology values for earlier time points suggest a clear 
trend (biologically or statistically significant) that might be 
explained by such data.121 Pre-study clinical pathology test-
ing is not recommended for rodents because of the relatively 
large number of animals per group, the expected physiological 
similarity of individuals from a genetically homogeneous pop-
ulation, the likely irrelevance of pre-study data due to substan-
tial age-related changes in values for rapidly growing young

616
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
rodents, and the risk of adversely affecting the health of small 
animals due to excessive blood loss or the stress of the blood 
collection procedure.
For repeat-dose toxicity studies in non-rodents, blood sam-
pling for clinical pathology testing should be performed at 
minimum prior to study initiation and at necropsy. Pre-study 
sampling may be done once (usually between 2 and 7 days 
before initial test article dosing) or twice (typically at 7–14 days 
and again at 2–3 days before initial dosing). Pre-study sam-
pling should be done after a several-day period of post-
shipping environmental acclimatization (often 7 to 14 days). 
Interim sampling for clinical pathology testing during repeat-
dose toxicity studies in dogs and NHPs often is influenced by 
the nature of the test article. For study durations of 2, 4, or 
6 weeks, the interim time point typically occurs within the 
first 7 days to detect changes associated with initial test article 
exposure, such as acute but transient peaks in serum activities 
of cardiomyocyte281,302 or hepatocyte68 leakage enzymes.
Urinalysis testing typically should be performed for repeat-
dose toxicity studies and generally is conducted using the 
same schedule as the hematology, clinical chemistry, and 
coagulation endpoints. The FDA recommends urinalysis for 
all species.121 For rodents, the FDA advises one assessment 
within a week of the necropsy for acute (28-day) and sub-
chronic (90-day) studies and multiple assessments (3, 6, and 
12 months) for chronic studies.121 For non-rodents, the FDA 
advocates one assessment within a week of the necropsy for 
acute (28-day) studies and multiple assessments for longer bio-
assays: pre-study, either 1 and 2 months (for subchronic stud-
ies) or every 3 months (for chronic studies), and once within a 
week of necropsy.121 In contrast, the EPA recommends urine 
collection in rodents for chronic studies (including those con-
ducted together with a carcinogenicity assessment), but not 
90-day studies, and advises that the analysis be confined to the 
terminal time point.95,101,103 For non-rodents, the EPA requires 
urine sampling prior to study initiation, midway through the 
treatment phase, and at the end of the study.96,101 In contrast, 
the OECD guidance for urinalysis testing is comparable to 
that rendered by EPA.245,246,251 For stand-alone rodent carci-
nogenicity bioassays, urinalysis is not listed as an EPA102 or 
OECD250 requirement but is recommended by FDA prior to 
initiation of the study as well as at 3, 6, and 12 months.121 The 
consensus among toxicologic pathologists is that urinalysis 
testing in mice is impractical as a routine test.150
12.2.4.2.2  Anatomic Pathology
In general, anatomic pathology sampling for toxicity studies 
is performed at necropsy. The progression of macroscopic, 
microscopic, and organ weight changes is followed by includ-
ing interim necropsies in addition to the final necropsy. Interim 
necropsies typically are included only for subchronic and 
chronic studies; possible designs include 30- or 45-day time 
points for a 90-day study, and 60- or 90-day time points for a 
180-day study. The selection of interim time points typically is 
left to the study director.
In exceptional cases, tissue samples may be obtained from 
animals at interim time points so that early effects may be 
compared to findings at the terminal necropsy for the same 
subject. Examples include tissue collection using a needle 
biopsy to assess the evolution of liver disease over time58,61 or a 
punch biopsy of the NHP footpad to enumerate intraepidermal 
nerve fibers).211 In general, such procedures are performed for 
research and efficacy studies rather than toxicity studies.
12.2.4.3  Sample Sizes
The sizes of fluid and tissue samples in large part depend on the 
species and the sampling time. Larger fluid volumes are obtain-
able during life from large non-rodents (e.g., rabbits, dogs, mini-
pigs, NHPs) relative to small rodents. Larger fluid and ­tissue 
samples may be obtained at necropsy than during life.
12.2.4.3.1  Clinical Pathology
The blood volume in circulation ranges from approximately 
65 (rat, cynomolgus monkey) to 85 (Beagle dog) mL/kg body 
weight for common test species. The amount of blood that may 
be withdrawn depends on the amount to be collected and the 
length of time between collections. In general, blood volumes 
of 7.5%, 10%, and 15% may be taken if they are followed by 
a recovery period of 1, 2, or 4 weeks, respectively, although 
Institutional Animal Care and Use Committee (IACUC) 
guidelines vary slightly among research facilities.
For mice, blood sampling generally is limited to collection 
at necropsy (~0.5–1.0 mL) because of the small volumes that 
may be sampled during life (between 0.1 and 0.2 mL for a 
young adult mouse). If a full panel of clinical pathology tests is 
planned for this species, subsets or cohorts of mice should be 
assigned to hematology OR clinical chemistry testing in order 
to obtain sufficient blood volume for complete analysis. A pos-
sible compromise to permit analysis of hematology and clini-
cal chemistry in all animals is to perform a full hematology 
profile (as all parameters may be measured in samples of 50 
to 300 µL of blood depending on the hematology instrument 
and its multi-species analytical software) but then limit clini-
cal chemistry tests to those that are most relevant as screening 
tools for major organ toxicity (~100–150 µL of serum). Pooling 
of blood samples should not be done.
For rats, the greater circulating blood volume permits more 
frequent sampling during life. The blood volumes that may be 
safely collected for a 7.5% sample range from 0.72 mL for a 
150 g young adult rat to 1.01 mL for a 210 g rat to 1.20 mL for a 
250 g mature adult rat. Therefore, repeated blood sampling at 
intervals of 1 week should allow a full battery of hematology, 
clinical chemistry, and coagulation tests to be performed.
For non-rodents, the large blood volume readily supports 
frequent sampling to support clinical pathology testing. Blood 
collection may be performed as a single withdrawal, as is com-
monly done for interim time points during toxicity studies, or 
as a series of smaller aliquots obtained within a 24-h period, 
which is common for TK analysis.
Urine volumes in all species should be standardized to 
permit reliable urinalysis. In practice, this approach requires 
collecting a volume of 2–3 mL from rats (usually obtained by 
overnight sampling in a metabolic cage) and 5 mL from larger 
animals.39 Such standardization is needed to permit consistent 
comparisons both within and between studies.

617
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
12.2.4.3.2  Anatomic Pathology
Since tissue samples typically are obtained at necropsy, suf-
ficient tissue should be available from all animals for all but 
the smallest organs (e.g., endocrine glands of mice). The main 
limits on sample size are the study objective and the rate of 
fixative penetration.
Tissues slated for light microscopic evaluation should be 
removed as slices or blocks with a thickness of 0.5 to 1 cm. 
This dimension is dictated by the ability of NBF to penetrate 
immersed tissue within 24 h. In the rare cases where tissues 
are obtained during life, conventional needle and punch 
biopsy specimens always will have one dimension that is less 
than 0.5 cm thick. Eyes and testes, which are fixed whole and 
measure larger than 1 cm in non-rodent species, are often pre-
served in a multi-component fixative (mixtures of aldehydes, 
acids, and/or alcohols) with more rapid penetration proper-
ties such as Bouin’s solution, Davidson’s solution, or modified 
Davidson’s solution.201
Tissues destined for TEM evaluation should have a maximum 
dimension of 1 mm to ensure that fixative penetrates quickly 
enough to preserve fine cytoarchitectural features. The standard 
means for attaining this size is to place a thin piece of tissue (an 
entire small organ or a thin slice of a large organ) in a droplet of 
fixative and then mince it into 1 mm3 pieces using a sharp blade.
12.2.5  Control Data for Pathology Endpoints
An obvious advantage for interpreting data from animal toxic-
ity studies is the accessibility of relevant control data. Many 
categories of control data may be utilized, most of which are 
dictated by differences in the study design and demographic 
attributes (species, stock/strain/breed, age, sex, source, etc.).
12.2.5.1  Concurrent Control Data
Animal studies typically include one or more concurrent con-
trol groups. Subjects in these groups are manipulated in paral-
lel with individuals in the various groups that are administered 
the test article. The most common concurrent control group 
in conventional toxicity studies is exposure to vehicle alone 
(i.e., “negative” control [equivalent to the placebo in clinical 
trials]). Other concurrent control groups employed in some 
animal studies include administration of a substance known 
to induce a particular response (“positive” control, to confirm 
the ability of the experimental methods and technical staff to 
detect an effect), surgical intervention without the introduc-
tion of the test article (“sham-operated” or “technical” control, 
to discriminate between effects of the test article and tissue 
manipulation), and unhandled animals (i.e., “untreated” con-
trol, to discern the effects of experimental and environmental 
stress). Concurrent control values for both clinical pathology 
and anatomic pathology endpoints typically are acquired at 
the same times as those for animals in the test article–treated 
groups. In some cases, a single control group may be assem-
bled by evaluating half of all control animals at an interim 
time point and the other half at the terminal necropsy. This 
means of reducing animal use usually is utilized for discovery 
or efficacy studies and not for toxicity studies.
As noted above, pre-study (baseline) clinical pathology val-
ues are commonly acquired for non-rodent toxicity studies as 
a means of ensuring a robust data set despite the small group 
sizes. One major benefit of pre-study analysis is to identify 
and avoid animals with possible health problems or clearly 
divergent baseline values (relative to their peers); pre-emptive 
exclusion of divergent subjects is an important means for min-
imizing excess variation (“noise”) that might obscure subtle 
but real test article–induced effects (“signal”). Anomalous 
pre-study clinical pathology findings that may keep animals 
from being enrolled in a study include decreased values for 
key erythrocyte parameters (e.g., cell count, hemoglobin 
concentration, hematocrit, mean cell volume), reduced or 
increased total leukocyte or neutrophil counts, low total pro-
tein and albumin concentrations, high globulin concentration, 
and elevated serum (or plasma) activities for hepatocyte leak-
age enzymes. A second benefit of pre-study clinical pathology 
analysis is that the baseline values permit each animal in the 
study to serve as its own control with respect to the evolution 
of test article–related effects over time.
12.2.5.2  Historical Control Data (HCD) 
and Reference Intervals
Compilations of prior findings for genetically homogeneous 
animals (rodents > rabbits, dogs, minipigs > NHPs) act as HCD 
that may be helpful in interpreting results of new studies. Such 
databases not only are commonly applied in interpreting the 
relevance of rare proliferative lesions in rodent carcinogenicity 
studies72,178,179 but also may be utilized in other types of toxic-
ity studies.91,197 The term “historical control data” (HCD) typi-
cally is applied to anatomic pathology HCD, while the term 
“reference interval” (or “reference range”) is typically used 
for clinical pathology HCD. The HCD by any of these names 
reflect expected values to be found in healthy individuals that 
share the same demographic characteristics (species, stock/
strain/breed, sex, age, animal source, etc.) and often other 
factors related to the study design and husbandry practices 
(Table 12.5). The HCD “borrowed” from prior studies may 
act as a “virtual control” group321 to define normal reference 
ranges for quantitative measurements (e.g., body weight, organ 
weight, serum hormone concentrations) or set the expected 
spontaneous prevalence for various adverse outcomes (e.g., 
liver tumors, developmental defects). In particular, HCD are 
essential in interpreting whether a relationship exists between 
test article exposure and the occurrence of either a rare tumor 
or a marginal increase (relative to concurrent control animals) 
in numbers of a common spontaneous tumor.214 The value of 
HCD also depends on genetic, phenotypic, and diagnostic con-
sistency over time.72,90,178 In general, HCD from peer-reviewed 
studies (i.e., where pathology data have been examined by two 
or more pathologists) are deemed to be more reliable.
Best practice recommendations regarding appropriate use of 
anatomic pathology HCD have been generated by the STP.178 
The STP recommendations are consistent with guidance offered 
by the EPA, EMA (European Medicines Agency), FDA, and 
OECD that the concurrent control data are the most relevant 
point of comparison for determining the potential significance

618
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
of effects and their relationship to test article exposure in a 
study. The role of HCD in such comparisons is to enhance the 
weight of evidence supporting a particular interpretation. In 
general, HCD from the laboratory that performed the current 
study is likely to provide a more focused reference range than 
will HCD compiled from several laboratories. Furthermore, 
HCD usually are restricted to a limited time span (typically 2 
to 7 years) in order to minimize the impact of genetic, pheno-
typic, and diagnostic drift. Accordingly, the possible relevance 
of published HCD should be considered with care.
Reference intervals for clinical pathology analytes typi-
cally are constructed using values acquired from vehicle-
treated (control) animals for prior toxicity studies and (if 
available) pre-study values obtained from clinically healthy, 
untreated animals. Current best practice in both medical and 
veterinary medical practice is to establish historical refer-
ence intervals using 120 or more demographically matched 
individuals when possible.57,130 The usefulness of reference 
intervals in data interpretation is a function of the partition-
ing factors (parameters) that define the population used to 
develop the interval. Many potential partitioning factors exist 
(Table 12.5). For routine studies, the most common choices 
are species, stock/strain/breed, sex, and age. This approach 
is utilized by many laboratories since incorporation of addi-
tional partitioning factors lowers the number of animals who 
meet all criteria needed for inclusion as a reference individ-
ual, thereby increasing the difficulty of assembling reference 
intervals relevant to many studies. However, inclusion of too 
few partitioning factors leads to incorporation of data (“lump-
ing”) from studies with many dissimilar designs or from sam-
ples that have been collected and handled differently, thus 
resulting in broader reference intervals with less relevance for 
data interpretation. Ideally, a new reference interval is gener-
ated whenever a new analytical instrument is brought on line, 
but in practice, many facilities rely on “bridging” assays to 
confirm consistency between data used in assembling the ref-
erence intervals for the old and new equipment.
Data from a current study that fall outside the ranges of 
HCD or reference intervals do not automatically indicate the 
existence of a genuine test article–induced abnormality.360 
Data for concurrent control animals for a given study do not 
recapitulate historical data in terms of either group mean val-
ues or data distribution (variance). Therefore, both HCD and 
reference intervals should not be interpreted as “normal” but 
rather as an indication of variation considered to occur (i.e., 
“within normal limits” [WNL]) for the majority of population

In routine toxicity studies, the terms HCD and RI are applied, respectively, to anatomic pathology and clinical pathology 
endpoints.

619
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
members. For reference intervals, clinical pathology values 
from the reference individuals are subjected to nonpara-
metric statistics so that outlying results are disregarded. For 
example, if the lowest and highest 2.5% of values are dis-
carded, the resulting reference interval represents the central 
95% of values. By definition, when the central 95% is used 
as the reference interval, 5% of values (1 of 20) from healthy 
individuals will fall outside the reference interval for any 
given test.
The usefulness of HCD and reference intervals for data 
interpretation may be overestimated, and the potential for their 
improper use is great.149,354 The appropriate application of ref-
erence intervals is to offer perspective on values that might 
be expected in healthy animals. Reference intervals should 
not be the main reason for interpreting apparent differences 
between clinical pathology results of concurrent control and 
test article–treated animals as either genuine and/or adverse 
effects, or as incidental and biologically insignificant findings.
12.2.6  Qualifications and Responsibilities 
of Pathology Personnel
As with all aspects of conventional toxicity studies, personnel 
involved in the toxicologic pathology evaluation must be famil-
iar with the facility’s pathology-related standard operating 
procedures (SOPs) as well as details regarding the pathology 
analysis as defined in particular study protocols. In addition, 
pathology staff must possess suitable training and experience 
to fulfill their complex technical roles. Lifelong dedication to 
continuing education (professional society meetings, reading, 
etc.) is expected of toxicologic pathology personnel, with the 
exact nature of such endeavors depending on the knowledge 
needed for an individual to succeed at their assigned tasks.
12.2.6.1  Toxicologic Pathologists
Pathologists may be responsible for the generation and inter-
pretation of anatomic pathology data (e.g., organ weights, mac-
roscopic and microscopic findings) OR clinical pathology data 
(clinical chemistry, hematology, coagulation, urinalysis) OR 
both anatomic pathology and clinical pathology data depend-
ing on individual, institutional, and societal preferences. 
Proficient toxicologic pathologists must have wide-ranging 
knowledge and experience in many scientific fields, which can 
be summarized under three major headings:
	
1.	Normal and morbid biology (e.g., anatomy, physiol-
ogy, biochemistry, medicine), in particular causes 
and mechanisms of incidental (background) and tox-
icant-induced diseases of common laboratory animal 
species and humans;
	
2.	Key investigative pathology techniques for evaluat-
ing changes in tissues (e.g., macroscopic observa-
tions, light and electron microscopy, and molecular 
pathology methods) and fluids (hematology, clinical 
chemistry, coagulation, and urinalysis); and
	
3.	Principles, practices, and regulations applicable to 
safety testing and risk assessment.
Such knowledge is essential for both anatomic pathologists 
and clinical pathologists.30 In general, knowledge and experi-
ence related to the first two headings may be obtained at aca-
demic institutions while acquisition of skills related to safety 
assessment typically must be sought at industrial or regulatory 
agencies that are heavily invested in toxicity testing. Other 
skills and knowledge will be increasingly important for toxi-
cologic pathologists, including familiarity with recent scien-
tific (e.g., –omics technologies259) and technical (e.g., digital 
pathology2,3) innovations.
The education and experiences of toxicologic pathology dif-
fer significantly depending on the country in which they work. 
Nonetheless, global societies of toxicologic pathology have rec-
ognized a common framework that is necessary to ensure that 
the relevant knowledge and skills for proficient practice have 
been developed.30 Generally accepted expectations are that toxi-
cologic pathologists should have a clinical degree in veterinary 
medicine (e.g., BVSc, DVM, or the equivalent) or medicine (MD 
or the equivalent) followed by additional post-graduate training 
in pathology gained via a formal residency and/or on-the-job 
“apprenticeship.” Many toxicologic pathologists also have one 
or more formal qualifications (or certifications) in pathology, 
toxicologic pathology, and/or toxicology that attest to their capa-
bilities in this role. Possession of a pathology credential is an 
essential prerequisite for toxicologic pathologists in many but 
not all institutions and geographic regions.30
12.2.6.2  Technical Staff
Facilities that perform animal toxicity studies commonly 
employ many technical staff with roles in toxicologic pathol-
ogy evaluations. Most facilities have separate laboratories for 
clinical pathology and anatomic pathology tasks. Staff may 
be assigned permanently to one of the labs, or they may rotate 
among roles and even between pathology labs depending on 
their training, experience, and individual and institutional 
preferences.
Position titles vary substantially among institutions. 
Common examples include research associate, research spe-
cialist, technician, and technologist. The same position titles 
at different institutions often utilize different standards with 
respect to educational and training requirements as well as 
duties. Instead, technical proficiency usually is attested by 
professional certification. Several credentials are available for 
clinical pathology and histology staff, but at present, no such 
qualification exists for necropsy technicians.
12.3  Clinical Pathology Tests
Generation and interpretation of clinical pathology data from 
a toxicity study differs greatly from the situation in which an 
individual patient is suffering from an unknown illness. The 
most obvious divergence is that prospective toxicity studies 
permit comparison of clinical pathology data from animals 
given one of several increasing doses of test article to data 
from a cohort of age- and/or weight- and sex-matched concur-
rent control subjects that have been exposed under the identical

620
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
experimental and environmental conditions. For larger ani-
mals (e.g., rabbits, dogs, minipigs, NHPs), the study design 
often permits collection of pre-study baseline data to serve 
as an internal comparator. Clinical pathology data should be 
integrated and interpreted with anatomic pathology as well as 
other relevant information (TK values, in-life findings, safety 
pharmacology findings, etc.) for an individual animal and/or 
group. Taken together, these factors permit pathology data 
generally, and clinical pathology data specifically, to identify 
subtle changes related to test article exposure that would not 
be apparent when viewing the results for an individual xeno-
biotic-exposed patient. Finally, an additional challenge and 
advantage when evaluating clinical pathology data sets from 
toxicity studies is that common sources of pre-analytical and 
analytical variation may be anticipated and/or controlled to 
enhance the accuracy of the data set.
A key challenge faced when interpreting clinical pathology 
data for a toxicity study is to discriminate potentially harmful 
(adverse) test article–related effects from benign (non-adverse) 
homeostatic, metabolic, or adaptive changes related to test 
article exposure or an experimental procedure. Interpretation 
of clinical pathology data from a toxicity study requires under-
standing not only of results for the individual tests but also 
how results of multiple tests together provide a pattern that 
indicates the likelihood of a test article–related primary or 
secondary effect on a specific organ or system.
12.3.1  Hematology Tests
Many commercially available automated hematology analyz-
ers can examine all routinely recommended hematology tests 
using approximately 250–300 μL of anticoagulated whole 
blood. Both measured and calculated indices may be obtained 
for erythrocytes (red blood cells [RBC]), leukocytes (white 
blood cells [WBC]), and thrombocytes (platelets). Hematology 
instruments with software validated for use with animal blood 
samples should be utilized for hematology testing during tox-
icity studies because blood cells of laboratory animal species 
vary in shape and size relative to human blood cells, and ana-
lyzers lacking multi-species capabilities are likely to return 
an automated leukocyte differential that is incorrect.280 The 
hemogram represents the results for all blood cell lineages 
present in a blood sample.
12.3.1.1  Erythrocytes
Erythrocytes and their precursors in the bone marrow are col-
lectively termed the erythron. Erythrocytes primarily func-
tion to transport oxygen, but they also have the capacity to 
carry bound chemicals (including biomolecules and small 
molecules). Hematologic analysis frequently offers insight 
regarding erythrocyte function, production, and turnover. The 
erythrogram is the group of blood analytes that collectively 
assess the adequacy of erythrocytic cells.
Changes in erythrocytic indices usually reflect an altered 
balance between cell generation and removal. Genuine test 
article–related effects include reduced production (hypopla-
sia or aplasia in the bone marrow and [in rodents] the spleen), 
enhanced destruction (intravascular hemolysis or increased 
phagocytosis), or frank loss (internal or external hemorrhage). 
Erythrocytic parameters may be impacted indirectly by 
changes in plasma volume associated with clinical dehydration 
or volume expansion. In general, three main measured eryth-
rocytic indices – the RBC count, hematocrit percentage (Hct), 
and hemoglobin concentration (Hgb) – are used collectively 
to quantify red cell mass (and by proxy the oxygen-carrying 
capacity of the blood). An increased number of circulating 
reticulocytes (immature RBCs) and/or nucleated RBCs (meta-
rubricytes, which are erythroid precursors that normally are 
confined to bone marrow) may suggest that RBCs are being 
released early (i.e. increased erythropoiesis), bearing in mind 
that comparatively higher numbers of reticulocytes in the 
peripheral circulation are normal and expected in animals 
with shorter erythrocyte life span (e.g., rodents). Normal val-
ues for erythrocytic parameters can vary substantially with the 
species, stock/strain/breed, sex, age, animal source, and study 
conditions.274,369 Combinations of changes in erythrocyte indi-
ces yield patterns that aid in identifying potential or likely 
mechanisms responsible for test article–related RBC effects.
12.3.1.1.1  Decreased Red Blood Cell Mass
Reduced RBC count, Hct, and/or Hgb concentration (relative to 
concurrent controls) are common findings in test article–treated 
animals (more often in rodents) in conventional toxicity stud-
ies. Effects among these endpoints often behave in parallel and 
collectively are indicative of decreased RBC mass (i.e., reduced 
oxygen-carrying capacity of the system). In humans, Hgb varies 
by race, sex and age, with the lower limits of reference inter-
vals for men and women ranging from 12.7 to 13.7 g/dL and 
11.5 to 12.2 g/dL, respectively.26 Clinical signs related to anemia 
in humans (including exercise intolerance, pallor, tachycardia, 
tachypnea, and/or weakness from diminished oxygen delivery 
to tissues) typically do not develop until Hgb drops below 8 g/
dL,329 indicating that the pathology diagnosis “decreased RBC 
mass” cannot be equated with the clinical condition “anemia.” 
In other words, the clinical term “anemia” should not be used in 
nonclinical studies for clinical pathology profiles indicative of 
“decreased RBC mass;” instead, reductions in the RBC count, 
Hct, and Hgb for test article–treated animals in toxicity stud-
ies typically should be discussed using descriptive terminol-
ogy (related to the magnitude of differences when comparing 
control and treated groups). This recommendation is consistent 
with prior experience showing that test article–related reduc-
tions from control values for these parameters have different 
clinical implications: decreases of up to 10% are mild and non-
adverse, of approximately 10% to 30% are moderate and may or 
may not be adverse, and of 30% or more are marked, clinically 
evident, and adverse.150 Mild (non-adverse) Hgb reductions may 
reflect adverse processes in other organs (e.g., gastric ulceration, 
immune-mediated RBC destruction).
The physiological response to decreased RBC mass can 
be broadly classified as regenerative or non-regenerative. A 
regenerative response reflects an appropriate increase in RBC 
production (increased erythropoiesis), while a non-regenera-
tive response does not exhibit this expected increase. The most 
important parameter for differentiating between regenerative 
or non-regenerative responses is the absolute reticulocyte 
(Retic) count, where Retics represent immature, non-nucleated

621
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
RBCs in circulation. Proper interpretation necessitates that 
the absolute Retic count (Retics/μL) should be multiplied by 
the relative Retic count (Retics as a percentage of all eryth-
rocytes). This transformation is needed because animals with 
decreased RBC mass may possess an elevated relative Retic 
count together with a decreased absolute Retic count (where 
the latter value is most relevant as an estimation of the effec-
tiveness of the erythropoietic response).
12.3.1.1.1.1  Regenerative Responses to Decreased RBC Mass  
Decreased RBC mass may incite an appropriate regenerative 
response in two situations: blood loss (hemorrhage) and accel-
erated RBC destruction (hemolysis). Hemorrhage typically 
results from comorbid disease (e.g., cystitis, extensive cuta-
neous or gastric ulceration, epistaxis [nasal bleeding]) and/or 
collection of multiple blood samples over a short period (e.g., 
for PK or TK investigations). Hemolysis in toxicity studies 
usually stems from extravascular destruction of RBCs due to 
immune-mediated (uncommon) or oxidative (fairly common) 
injury rather than from intravascular destruction of RBCs due 
to immune-mediated or parasitic membrane damage150,223; 
commonly, components of both extra- and intravascular 
hemolysis are present, with one being more prominent. The 
pattern of hemolysis depends on the nature of the xenobiotic. 
Oxidizing chemicals tend to produce dose-dependent destruc-
tion of RBCs in many animals shortly after exposure while 
immune-mediated loss generally presents as a dose-inde-
pendent idiosyncratic phenomenon in one or a few animals 
that arises at least 1 week after test article administration.150 
Parameters measuring RBC mass will remain fairly stable as 
long as the regenerative response matches the degree of RBC 
destruction or loss. The RBC mass will be restored to normal 
levels if either RBC production exceeds RBC loss or the cause 
of RBC destruction or loss is removed.
In terms of hematologic testing, circulating Retic num-
bers serve as the principal indicator of an RBC regenera-
tive response. Retic numbers start to rise within 2–4 days 
after hemorrhage, hemolysis, or aggressive blood collection. 
During strong regenerative responses, Retic counts may be 
both boosted by 6- to 8-fold over normal levels and occur in 
conjunction with an increased mean cell volume (MCV) and 
decreased mean cell hemoglobin concentration (MCHC)150; 
the latter two changes reflect the larger cell size and lower 
Hgb content of Retics (i.e., immature RBCs with maturing 
hemoglobin concentration) relative to mature RBCs. In tox-
icity studies, increased MCV is seen more commonly than 
decreased MCHC, particularly in dogs and NHPs. The greater 
variation in RBC size during RBC regenerative responses also 
may increase the RBC distribution width (RDW, an index 
of cell size differences). Other hematology analytes provide 
additional information regarding the mechanisms behind an 
RBC regenerative response. Acute hemorrhage always should 
be considered as a likely cause when reduced RBC mass is 
accompanied by decreased serum levels of total protein. 
Hemolysis may lead to increased bilirubin levels in serum 
(bilirubinemia) and urine (bilirubinuria). Intravascular hemo-
lysis frequently is associated with free (extracellular) Hgb in 
circulation, resulting in red-tinged plasma (hemoglobinemia) 
or urine (hemoglobinuria).
Cytomorphological features of RBCs in routinely prepared 
blood smears may be used to confirm the presence of a regenera-
tive response. Retics often are easy to find, appearing as large 
non-nucleated cells with pale blue cytoplasm interspersed among 
mature RBCs (smaller cells with pale pink cytoplasm) in blood 
smears prepared with Romanowsky-type stains (e.g., Giemsa or 
Wright stain). Other cytomorphological traits in blood smears 
indicative of erythropoiesis include anisocytosis (variable cell 
size) and polychromasia (variable cell color), both of which 
reflect an increased number of circulating Retics. Spherocytes 
are small, round, dense (darker stained) RBCs resulting from 
partial removal of an antibody-coated RBC’s membrane by 
phagocytic cells (especially in the spleen); these cells can be 
prominent in immune-mediated hemolysis, more commonly in 
dogs and NHPs. In some cases, metarubricytes (nucleated RBCs) 
and Howell–Jolly bodies (small retained nuclear fragments) may 
be detected. The presence of nuclear material in circulating 
RBCs shows that very immature RBCs have been rushed into 
circulation. The existence of intracellular Heinz bodies (aggre-
gates of irreversibly denatured Hgb attached to the inner sur-
face of the RBC plasma membrane) is suggestive of oxidative 
injury. The numbers and sizes of Heinz bodies depend on the 
causative agent, dose, and time following exposure. Heinz bodies 
are observed more easily in smears made using supravital stains 
(e.g., methylene blue) rather than Romanowsky-type stains. 
Hemolysis in NHPs (even if captive-bred) may be complicated 
by malarial (Plasmodium) infections.285 Organisms may be seen 
in blood smears from healthy animals with normal RBC indices 
(depending on the stage of infection in the parasitic life cycle),8 
but in rare cases, stress (i.e., shipping, study-related procedures) 
or test article administration may allow their numbers to climb 
so far that a hemolytic crisis ensues.
Hematology effects indicative of decreased RBC mass often 
are accompanied by anatomic pathology changes. Hemorrhage 
may be seen macroscopically during life (cutaneous ulcer-
ation, epistaxis) or at necropsy (cystitis, gastric ulceration, 
melena [black stool from digested blood]). Extravascular 
hemolysis often presents at necropsy as splenomegaly (splenic 
enlargement) with red bone marrow. Microscopic correlates of 
these two findings are extramedullary hematopoiesis (EMH, 
especially in rodents) and pigment accumulation in resident 
phagocytes in the spleen and erythrocyte lineage hypercellu-
larity in the bone marrow. Hemolysis due to oxidant exposure 
may induce such macroscopic changes as cyanotic (blue/pur-
ple) mucous membranes and/or brown-colored blood resulting 
from formation of methemoglobin, an Hgb variant in which 
reversible oxidation of ferrous (Fe2+) to ferric (Fe3+) iron yields 
a reduced ability to transport oxygen; test articles causing 
Heinz body formation may induce methemoglobinemia and 
vice versa. Accurate quantification of methemoglobin is possi-
ble using specialized photometers but is not a standard feature 
of conventional toxicity studies. Agents that cause extensive 
intravascular hemolysis often induce local endothelial dam-
age at the site of intravenous test article delivery; an adverse 
level of intravascular hemolysis rarely results from test articles 
given by other routes of administration. Intravascular hemo-
lysis also may be detected microscopically by intraluminal 
hemoglobin in renal tubules (hemoglobinuric nephrosis) and 
intracellular pigment in renal tubular epithelium.

622
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
12.3.1.1.1.2  Non-Regenerative Responses to Decreased RBC 
Mass  In the absence of an appropriate regenerative response, 
RBC mass decreases due to a progressive decline in eryth-
rocyte numbers over time. Non-regenerative responses occur 
when normal RBC turnover is not followed by RBC renewal. 
Substantial or persistent non-regenerative responses may lead 
to profound decreases in RBC mass with significant clinical 
signs indicative of ill health (e.g., deficient grooming, dimin-
ished activity, and/or reductions in body weight, body weight 
gain, and food consumption). More often, however, decreased 
RBC mass presents in toxicity studies as mild (5% to 10%) 
declines in RBC counts, Hct, and/or Hgb (in all species) and 
sometimes a mild reduction in MCV (mainly in rodents) rela-
tive to values in concurrent control animals that may or may 
not be accompanied by clinical signs.
Direct toxic causes of non-regenerative responses induce 
damage to hematopoietic stem cells and/or the stromal micro-
environment that alters the differentiation and maturation of 
erythrocytic cells. Pure red cell aplasia (i.e., hematopoietic 
failure limited to RBCs) may occur in humans as an idiosyn-
cratic, possibly immune-mediated reaction following exposure 
to specific drugs, but in animal toxicity studies the more com-
mon hematotoxic response is aplastic anemia characterized 
by steep declines in stem cells of all hematopoietic lineages: 
erythrocytes, leukocytes (WBCs), and thrombocytes (plate-
lets). Removal of the toxic agent (e.g., alkylating and cyto-
toxic anti-cancer agents) usually is accompanied by gradual 
bone marrow recovery and renascent blood cell production. 
Non-regenerative anemia is a side effect of some hematopoi-
etic tumors and leukemias. Tumor-associated mechanisms 
culminating in reduced erythropoiesis include attenuated 
responsiveness to erythropoietin (a hormone produced pre-
dominantly in the kidney that stimulates RBC production); 
excessive generation of master pro-inflammatory cytokines 
like interleukin (IL)-1, IL-6, and tumor necrosis factor alpha 
(TNFα) that act to limit erythropoiesis; hemolysis; nutritional 
deficiencies (e.g., folate, vitamin B12); and physical competi-
tion for bone marrow space.50
Indirect causes of non-regenerative responses often are 
assumed when a specific mechanism cannot be identified. 
In such instances, the presumptive mechanism is a general-
ized reduction in anabolic metabolism (often termed anemia 
of chronic disease) that impacts hematopoietic tissue among 
other systems.150 For example, chronic inflammation inhibits 
erythropoiesis by lowering iron availability and decreasing 
responsiveness to erythropoietin. Endocrine dysfunction (e.g., 
hypothyroidism) may reduce the basal metabolic rate, thereby 
lessening the need for oxygen delivery. Kidney dysfunction 
impacts RBC mass by reducing renal production of erythropoi-
etin and lessening clearance of hematotoxic metabolic byprod-
ucts. Liver dysfunction may lead to effects of RBC mass when 
altered cholesterol metabolism results in abnormal composition 
and increased fragility of RBC membranes, often resulting in 
premature clearance by the phagocytic system. These indirect 
effects tend to be milder and of less toxicologic significance 
compared to direct causes of non-regenerative anemia.
In terms of hematologic testing, the profile of a non-regen-
erative response is simultaneous decreases in RBC counts, 
Hct, and Hgb in conjunction with a decreased Retic count. 
Decreases in other erythrocytic parameters also may occur; 
for example, chronic iron deficiency (which is rare in toxicity 
studies but may occur in instances of long-term hemorrhage 
or altered iron metabolism) produces a hypochromic (low 
MCHC), microcytic (low MCV) anemia. Decreased RBC 
indices are most likely to be encountered in rodent toxicity 
studies, for several reasons. First, dose levels in rodent studies 
usually are higher than those in non-rodent studies, thereby 
providing a greater potential for hematotoxicity. Second, inter-
