individual variability of hematology data is relatively low in 
typical genetically homogeneous rodent populations, which 
permits readier identification of mild hematologic changes. 
Finally, species differences in the half-lives (t½) of mature 
RBCs in circulation mean that rodents (t½ = ~45 days in mice 
and ~60 days in rats)274 develop anemia faster than either dogs 
(t½ = ~120 days)85 or NHPs (t½ = ~100 days in macaques).274 
Other concurrent clinical pathology changes that may develop 
in non-regenerative anemia include reduced WBC and platelet 
counts as well as reduced serum concentrations of total protein 
and albumin. In toxicity studies, decreased WBC and platelet 
counts typically occur earlier in the study (typically 5–10 days 
after initial administration150) due to the shorter lifes pans of 
these cells in circulation (e.g., t½ = 12–24 hours for neutrophils 
and 7–10 days for platelets) compared to RBCs. That said, 
Retic counts are likely to provide a better estimate of hemato-
toxic injury and recovery in toxicity studies since higher Retic 
counts in normal young rodents facilitate recognition of eryth-
ropoietic deficits.
Cytological evaluation of RBC morphology provides evi-
dence regarding the presence of a non-regenerative response. 
Deficient erythropoiesis results in circulating RBCs having 
variable colors (polychromasia) and sizes (anisocytosis). 
Most RBCs in the blood possess the expected color (normo-
chromic) and size (normocytic); this pattern correlates with 
an unchanged MCV (in non-rodents) or mildly decreased 
MCV (in rodents). Absolute Retic counts may be relatively 
normal if an erythropoietic response is present but inad-
equate (which is common for indirect causes), but marked 
reductions in Retic numbers are likely if erythropoiesis is 
suppressed or failed (which is typical of directly hematotoxic 
mechanisms). Removal of the hematotoxic cause may lead 
to a rebound effect in which circulating numbers of Retics, 
platelets, and sometimes neutrophils may be substantially 
increased. Because the latency to peak effect and the dura-
tion of the effect on blood cell numbers vary depending on 
the nature of the test article, hematology testing must be 
performed at multiple time points during a toxicity study to 
ensure proper interpretation.
Anatomic pathology correlates of altered erythropoiesis 
include bone marrow hypocellularity and (in rodents) reduced 
EMH in the spleen. Pure red cell aplasia would be indicated by 
fewer cells of the erythrocytic lineage. Multi-lineage aplasia 
typical of direct hematotoxicity would be suggested by micro-
scopically visible deficits in hematopoietic cells of all lineages, 
and possibly by pallor of the bone marrow when observed 
macroscopically at necropsy. Recovery following removal of 
the hematotoxic agent presents as restoration of hematopoietic 
tissue, typically EMH in the spleen before the bone marrow 
in rodents.

623
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
12.3.1.1.2  Increased Red Blood Cell Mass
Higher RBC mass due to a direct test article–related effect 
is an uncommon finding in toxicity studies. The typical sce-
nario is when animals have received a test article that induces 
a decrease in RBC mass leading to a subsequent regenerative 
response. In such cases, subjects in recovery groups may have 
transient elevations in RBC counts, Hct, and Hgb compared to 
values measured in concurrent control animals as an appropri-
ate secondary response following an initial decline in RBC 
mass. Primary increases in RBC mass reflect neoplastic trans-
formation of erythrocytic precursors. The clinical condition 
(termed polycythemia vera) is a myelodysplastic disease that 
is rare in animals, especially young individuals encountered in 
conventional toxicity studies.
Dehydration secondary to comorbidity is common in toxic-
ity studies and may present as an apparent increase in RBC 
mass. Hemoconcentration due to reduced plasma volume but 
normal RBC numbers results in mild increases in RBC counts, 
Hct, and Hgb of approximately 5%–10%. This effect may be 
observed in rodents where food and water were removed from 
the cage in the morning several hours before the necropsy 
was performed. A ready means for minimizing this effect is 
to avoid block effects in the necropsy sequence (i.e., process 
animals in random order rather than all of the control subjects 
first and all of the high-dose subjects last).
12.3.1.2  Leukocytes
Leukocytes (white blood cells [WBCs]) and their precur-
sors in bone marrow are collectively termed the leukon. The 
various leukocyte classes are engaged in systemic defense as 
primary components of the innate immune response (granu-
locytes [neutrophils, eosinophils, basophils], monocytes/mac-
rophages, and natural killer [NK] lymphocytes) or acquired/
adaptive immune response (lymphocytes and plasma cells). 
Hematologic analysis of peripheral blood reliably highlights 
key aspects of leukocyte function, production, and turnover, 
thereby providing insight regarding the status of innate and 
acquired immune capabilities. The leukogram is the group of 
blood analytes that collectively assess the adequacy of various 
leukocyte classes. This concept may be focused by the use of 
an appropriate adjective. For example, an “inflammatory leuk-
ogram” reflects increased utilization and subsequent enhanced 
production of neutrophils as a response to an infection and/or 
substantial tissue destruction.
Changes in leukocytic parameters observed during toxic-
ity studies may reflect physiological influences, test article–
induced alterations in the balance between cell generation 
and removal, or procedural effects related to tissue damage 
(with or without concurrent microbial infection). Genuine test 
article–related effects include enhanced utilization and pro-
duction (hyperplasia in the bone marrow and [in rodents] the 
spleen) and reduced production (hypoplasia or aplasia in the 
bone marrow). In most toxic conditions, absolute differential 
counts (on a cells/µL basis) for various leukocyte classes are 
the most consequential hematologic endpoints; relative counts 
(i.e., the number of cells for a leukocyte class as a percentage 
of the total WBC count) has essentially no value in interpreting 
leukocytic responses. For example, a relative neutrophil count 
of 45% in an animal may be normal, increased, or decreased 
depending on the total WBC count. In modern laboratories, 
differential counts are generated using automated hematol-
ogy analyzers rather than manual techniques as automated 
counts are more accurate than manual counts, especially when 
it comes to discriminating effects on minor leukocyte classes 
(e.g., decreased eosinophils). Normal values for leukocytic 
analytes differ considerably depending on the species, stock/
strain/breed, sex, and age.274
Lymphocytes and neutrophils are the most numerous cir-
culating blood cells in test animals and so comprise the leu-
kocyte classes that are affected most often in routine toxicity 
studies. Typical numbers of lymphocytes in common test spe-
cies are about 70%–80% in mice, 60%–75% in rats, 15%–25% 
in dogs, and 55%–70% in NHPs (cynomolgus macaques).274,286 
Similarly, average numbers of mature neutrophils in animals 
are approximately 20%–30% in mice, 10%–40% in rats, 
50%–70% in dogs, and 25%–40% in NHPs.274,286 Direct toxic 
effects impacting these two leukocyte classes occur infre-
quently, while indirect effects related to study procedures or 
test article–related effects in non-leukocytic tissues are rela-
tively common.356
As noted above for interpreting RBC findings, altered leu-
kocyte counts with or without correlative clinical signs should 
be communicated in the report narrative and data tables 
using descriptive terminology and the leukocyte class (e.g., 
decreased WBCs, increased neutrophils). Some investiga-
tors may still prefer to communicate pronounced changes in 
cell counts using clinical diagnostic terms in the report nar-
rative where the two suffixes “-cytosis” (for lymphocytes, 
monocytes, and plasma cells) or “-philia” (for granulocyte 
classes) designate an increase and the suffix “-penia” denotes 
a decrease. By this convention, leukocytosis and leukopenia 
would represent, respectively, an increase or decrease for all 
WBCs together, lymphocytosis and lymphopenia indicate an 
increase or decrease for lymphocytes only, and neutrophilia 
or neutropenia apply to an increase or decrease for neutrophils 
only. In general, however, authors of clinical pathology reports 
for nonclinical toxicity studies should avoid the use of clinical 
terms in describing these leukocyte changes.
12.3.1.2.1  Biological Responses of Leukocytes
Circulating leukocyte numbers may be altered in the absence 
of test article administration. The causes are endogenous sig-
naling molecules that impact the movement of various leuko-
cyte classes from the circulation into tissues.
Physiological leukocytosis (i.e., a “physiological leuko-
gram”) is a finding in excited or frightened animals that may 
cause the total WBC count to increase notably in the absence 
of an inflammatory response. The effect results from the 
release of endogenous catecholamines, which lead to accel-
erated heart rate, higher blood pressure, and more skeletal 
muscle activity. The hemodynamic changes related to these 
events lead to a shift of leukocytes out of the marginal pool 
(i.e., intravascular cells adhered to endothelium of small blood 
vessels or vascular beds in the spleen) into the circulating 
pool. The type and magnitude of mobilized cells depend on 
the species; lymphocytes are released in rodents, segmented

624
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
(mature) neutrophils in dogs, and lymphocytes and neutrophils 
in NHPs. The change is fairly common in animals not accus-
tomed to handling and blood sampling, so in many toxicity 
studies, at least a few animals will have substantially increased 
WBC counts at the first blood collection. Recognition of this 
phenomenon is essential when interpreting data from studies 
with small group sizes (usually dogs or NHPs) and no accli-
matization period for blood sampling to avoid the erroneous 
conclusion that a test article with high initial WBC counts and 
lower counts at later blood collection intervals has suppressed 
WBC production. For this reason, strong consideration should 
be given to incorporating at least two pre-study hematology 
samples in larger animal species (e.g., rabbits, dogs, NHPs) to 
ease data interpretation and assess potential procedure-related 
trends, particularly in studies where leukocyte counts are criti-
cal endpoints.
The stress-induced leukocyte response (a “stress leuko-
gram”) occurs in animals subjected to chronic stress. This 
physiological effect results from the increased release of 
endogenous glucocorticoids, but it also may be elicited by the 
administration of exogenous glucocorticoids. The hematologic 
profile is characterized primarily by increased segmented 
neutrophil counts in conjunction with decreased lymphocyte 
and eosinophil counts; increased monocyte counts may or 
may not be evident. The subtle extent of the eosinophil change 
usually can be identified only in automated counts. This pat-
tern typically is observed in individual animals and not entire 
groups if the response stems from procedure-related stress but 
may affect many animals per group if significant test article–
related toxicity is present. Leukocyte changes indicative of a 
stress response can be interpreted together with other clinical 
and pathology data to assess the extent to which stress might 
be a confounding influence.116
12.3.1.2.2  Neutrophil Responses
Increased neutrophil counts typically result from two main 
causes: augmented production (inflammation due to infectious 
or non-infectious causes) or release from the marginal pool to 
the circulating pool (physiological leukocytosis in dogs and 
NHPs or a stress-induced leukocyte response in all species). In 
routine toxicity studies, increased neutrophil production may 
be associated with the intrinsic or direct activity of the test 
article (e.g., some cytokines and hematopoietic growth fac-
tors) or it may be a secondary response to a test article–related 
toxic effect (e.g., intravascular hemolysis or tissue necrosis) or 
study procedure (e.g., long-term catheterization or repeated 
injections). The degree of increase induced by cytokines and 
hematopoietic growth factors or tissue destruction usually 
ranges from mild to marked, while that associated with study 
procedures in the absence of an infection typically is mini-
mal to mild. Increased neutrophil counts due to enhanced cell 
production may include a left shift, which is characterized by 
increased numbers of immature neutrophils (called “band” 
neutrophils due to their non-segmented nuclei) in circulation. 
Left shifts leading to marked neutrophilia are common in 
inflammatory conditions due to heightened neutrophil demand 
but also may be produced by administration of pro-inflamma-
tory test articles. A degenerative left shift is identified when the 
absolute neutrophil count is normal or decreased and consists 
of more immature than mature neutrophils. This leukocyte 
profile indicates that the need for neutrophils in an inflamed 
tissue has outstripped the ability of the bone marrow to supply 
them, and it commonly results from infection-driven inflam-
matory conditions; in toxicity studies, test article–independent 
degenerative left shifts may occur with mucosal necrosis or 
perforation of the digestive tract, septicemia (e.g., related to 
bacterial contamination of an indwelling intrathecal or intra-
vascular catheter), or severe inflammatory processes related to 
local bacterial colonization of internal spaces and tissues (e.g., 
liver, nasal passages, urinary bladder) or procedural errors 
(e.g., aspiration pneumonia or pharyngeal abscesses secondary 
to gavage accidents). Importantly, not all foci of inflammation 
will have an increased number of circulating inflammatory 
cells. The great need for neutrophils in such scenarios can lead 
to the presence of “toxic neutrophils” in circulation. These 
cells possess distinct cytoplasmic features indicative of accel-
erated neutrophil production, such as basophilia, granulation, 
and vacuolation of the cytoplasm and sometimes the presence 
of Döhle bodies (small, blue-gray cytoplasmic inclusions com-
prised of aggregated rough endoplasmic reticulum). The adjec-
tive “toxic” as applied to cells exhibiting this appearance is a 
misnomer as it does not imply an injury induced by a toxic 
test article but merely intensive metabolic activity regardless 
of the cause; “toxic” changes may be noted in neutrophils fol-
lowing administration of hematopoietic growth factors. Long-
lasting and sustained (i.e., “chronic active”) inflammation 
may produce a “leukemoid” response that is characterized by 
severe neutrophilia and a massive increase in granulopoiesis 
within the bone marrow, spleen (especially rodents), and often 
other organs (e.g., lymph nodes); the number of cells in circu-
lation needed to exceed the leukemoid threshold depends on 
the species, but a good rule of thumb is that neutrophil counts 
indicative of a leukemoid responses are 2-fold higher than 
mean values in healthy individuals. Exposure to bacterial tox-
ins, some chemicals (e.g., ethylene glycol), and certain drugs 
(including antibiotics, corticosteroids, and hematopoietic 
growth factors) has been linked to induction of a leukemoid 
response in humans,295 while transplanted tumors and some 
carcinogenic chemicals have been shown to engender this 
response in rodents. A third, less frequent cause of increased 
neutrophil counts is reduced migration of cells from blood ves-
sels into tissues. This effect stems from the ability of some 
agents (e.g., glucocorticoids) to diminish neutrophil egress.
Decreased neutrophil counts reflect lower production, aug-
mented retention in the marginal pool, increased consumption, 
or increased movement from the circulation into tissues. Lower 
neutrophil production is the most frequent cause encountered 
in toxicity studies. Anti-proliferative or cytotoxic agents incite 
reduced neutrophil counts in most or all animals from groups 
given toxic doses of the test article. Non-cytotoxic test articles 
may elicit substantial decreases in neutrophil numbers, but 
typically this finding is limited to one or two individuals per 
group.
The implications for interpreting decreased neutrophil 
counts depend on the species. For example, in mice and 
young rats, circulating neutrophil counts are very low (typi-
cally <1,000/μL). Such low baseline levels make recognition 
of decreased neutrophil numbers challenging in these species.

625
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
In addition, because lymphocytes comprise the major circu-
lating leukocyte class in rodents, ultra-low neutrophil counts 
(<500/μL) in mice and rats generally are not associated with a 
greater incidence of infection. In contrast, non-rodent species 
are more dependent on neutrophils, so low neutrophil counts 
(<1,000/μL) in dogs and primates (NHPs and humans) do sig-
nal the potential for an increased incidence of infection.150 
Immune-mediated reductions in neutrophil numbers are pos-
sible but relatively rare.27,356 Such reactions are often idiosyn-
cratic, and thus dose-independent and difficult to identify in 
routine safety studies due to small group sizes.
In terms of hematologic testing, detection of altered neu-
trophil counts depends on the frequency and timing of blood 
and bone marrow sampling. For example, single or short-term 
administration of potent cytotoxic agents leads to a fairly rapid 
decline in neutrophil counts within a few days to a week. This 
pattern of hematotoxicity reflects the brief life span of neu-
trophils in circulation relative to other blood cell types. Once 
administration of the cytotoxic agent is halted, neutrophil 
numbers will recover, perhaps with a transient period during 
which immature neutrophils may be detected and often lead-
ing to a temporary state in which neutrophil numbers exceed 
normal levels (i.e., “rebound neutrophilia”). Selective damage 
to granulocyte precursors without affecting erythropoiesis 
(RBC production) or thrombopoiesis (platelet production) is 
unusual. Instead, cytotoxic test articles usually injure pluripo-
tent stem cells or oligopotent stem cells for several hemato-
poietic lineages simultaneously. Therefore, reduced neutrophil 
numbers typically will be accompanied by decreased numbers 
of other blood cell types including erythrocytes (and Retics), 
platelets, and often other leukocyte classes (especially mono-
cytes and eosinophils, but possibly also lymphocytes).
12.3.1.2.3  Lymphocyte Responses
Increased lymphocyte counts may result from enhanced pro-
duction, decreased margination, or redistribution. In toxicity 
studies, physiological leukocytosis (i.e., where excitement leads 
to catecholamine discharge and release of the marginal pool) 
is the most frequent cause and should be considered when only 
a few animals have higher lymphocyte counts. Test articles 
seldom induce increased lymphocyte counts as a direct toxic 
effect, but lymphocyte numbers may rise (chiefly in rodents) 
if the test article causes chronic inflammation or incites an 
immune response. Some immunomodulatory drugs may drive 
increased lymphocyte counts by altering cell traffic to and from 
tissues. Occasionally, cynomolgus monkeys may have markedly 
higher lymphocyte counts (>60,000/μL).150 This finding is tran-
sient, and to date, no mechanism has been identified.
Decreased lymphocyte counts may result from reduced 
production, an expanded marginal pool, or redistribution. In 
toxicity studies, a stress-induced leukocyte response (i.e., from 
long-term exposure to endogenous or exogenous glucocorti-
coids) is a common reaction that usually reduces leukocyte 
counts in only a few animals per group. Marked lympho-
penia is common as a terminal event in moribund animals. 
Some cytotoxic test articles may lead to decreased lympho-
cyte counts, but the degree of the decline is less prominent 
than the effect noted for neutrophils; this pattern typically is 
easier to appreciate in rodents since lymphocytes outnumber 
neutrophils in these species. Low lymphocyte counts tend to 
persist longer than decreased neutrophil counts due to the lon-
ger half-lives of circulating lymphocytes (t½ = days to weeks 
[naïve cells] or years [memory cells]) relative to neutrophils 
(t½ = 12–24 hours). The gradual rate of lymphocyte replenish-
ment generally prevents a prominent rebound response in this 
cell population.
In terms of hematologic testing, WBC differential counts 
using conventional hematology analyzers and cytological 
evaluation of routinely prepared blood smears can only assess 
the total lymphocytic population. If more specific evaluation 
of lymphocyte subtypes is warranted, flow cytometry may be 
used to identify multiple lymphocyte subpopulations (e.g., B 
cells, T cells, NK cells) based on the expression of multiple 
markers (e.g., cluster of differentiation [CD] antigens on the 
cell surface). Small decreases in absolute lymphocyte numbers 
may represent depletion of a single subset rather than a modest, 
across-the-board decline in all subsets.151 Immunophenotyping 
by flow cytometry is an increasingly important technique for 
evaluating this possibility in toxicity studies.190,349
12.3.1.2.4  Responses of Other Leukocytes: 
Monocytes, Eosinophils, Basophils, 
and Large Unstained Cells
Absolute counts for other leukocyte classes generally are quite 
low (<1,000/μL). Even so, test article–induced effects may be 
evident as subtle shifts in cell numbers as determined by auto-
mated differential counts.
Increased numbers of these cells may be a primary effect of 
test article exposure (e.g., for certain cytokines or hematopoi-
etic growth factors), but more often such increases are sequelae 
of conditions causing substantial cell destruction (e.g., hemoly-
sis or necrosis) or inflammation. The affected leukocyte class 
depends on the stimulus. Monocyte numbers rise when phago-
cytosis is necessary to clear cell debris; monocytes may fulfill 
this role in the circulation, or they may pass from the vascu-
lature to become tissues macrophages and serve the identical 
function. Eosinophil counts may be increased occasionally 
during hypersensitivity episodes or when an animal bears a 
heavy parasite burden (e.g., wild-caught NHPs with numer-
ous intestinal helminths). In addition, platelet aggregates may 
be counted as eosinophils by some hematology analyzers.115 
Basophil numbers are rarely increased, and indeed often are 
undetectable, in toxicity studies for all animal species. Large 
unstained cells (LUCs) cannot be assigned to one of the main 
leukocyte classes by automated hematology analyzers, but 
cytological evaluation often suggests that they are lympho-
cytes (mature or immature) or monocytes. In general, LUC 
counts increase during inflammation along with increases in 
other leukocyte cell types.
Decreased numbers of other leukocyte types usually are 
reflected in numbers of circulating monocytes and eosino-
phils. Both cell classes are reduced in number following 
administration of chemotherapeutic agents that cause bone 
marrow cytotoxicity. Eosinophil counts also may be decreased 
as part of the stress-induced leukocyte response. Basophil and 
LUC numbers vary to such a degree that declines related to 
test article administration typically cannot be detected by the 
designs of routine toxicity studies.

626
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
12.3.1.2.5  Leukocytic Neoplasia
Neoplasms originating from a blood cell lineage are observed 
with some frequency in lifetime (2-year) rodent carcinoge-
nicity studies, both as a spontaneous finding and as a lesion 
induced by test articles. Depending on the distribution, these 
tumors are designated as either “leukemia” (to reflect their 
concentration in the blood and bone marrow) or as a “sar-
coma” (thereby acknowledging a greater dispersion within 
the parenchyma of organs), with the lineage appended as a 
modifier. Examples of this nomenclature convention include 
myeloid leukemia (a blood-borne cancer of granulocytic [often 
neutrophil] derivation)’ histiocytic sarcoma (a solid tumor of 
monocyte/macrophage origin), and lymphosarcoma (often 
rendered as “lymphoma,” a solid neoplasm of lymphocytic lin-
eage).365 Leukocytic tumors typically begin at a single site, but 
more advanced tumors usually are multi-centric, exhibiting 
widespread tumor cell dispersion in both the blood and bone 
marrow as well as around vessels in many organs.
In terms of hematology testing, leukocytic neoplasms may 
be detected using automated differential counts acquired from 
blood samples taken at regularly scheduled intervals. In such 
samples, hematopoietic tumors present as a great increase 
in an affected cell lineage relative to the normal reference 
interval; abnormal hemograms may show a skewed pattern if 
neoplastic precursors (termed “blast” cells) are present in the 
circulation as they may be misclassified as another blood cell 
type due to their larger sizes and unusual morphological fea-
tures. However, some animals with leukemia may not exhibit 
marked elevations of WBC numbers in the blood, and not 
all cases are characterized by circulating blasts. Even when 
a diagnosis of leukemia is warranted, the cell of origin may 
be uncertain since precursors of many hematopoietic lineages 
(e.g., erythrocytic, lymphocytic, myelomonocytic) share simi-
lar features when assessed in standard cytological prepara-
tions by routine light microscopy.
Recognition of hematopoietic neoplasms often is easier 
during histopathologic evaluation of tissue sections. This 
approach often permits detection of increased leukocyte num-
bers in many blood vessels as well as perivascular neoplastic 
infiltration in highly vascularized organs (e.g., bone marrow, 
kidney, liver, lymph nodes, lung, spleen).
Lymphoma is the most common hematopoietic neoplasm in 
rodents.131,350 The incidence of disease varies with the species, 
stock/strain, sex, and age, but such tumors are seen sporadi-
cally as an incidental finding even in 90-day toxicity studies. 
The tendency of Fischer 344 (F344) rats to develop mono-
nuclear cell leukemia (i.e., large granular lymphocytic [LGL] 
leukemia) at a 25%–50% incidence during lifetime carcinoge-
nicity bioassays is perhaps the most prominent example of this 
finding.283 The tumor arises in the spleen and induces sple-
nomegaly (splenic enlargement) and often lymphadenopathy 
(lymph node enlargement). The hemogram is characterized by 
a marked increase in lymphocyte numbers and often decreased 
erythrocyte counts (brought on by immune-mediated hemo-
lytic anemia). Tumor cells appear in blood smears as large, 
immature lymphocytes. Some cells contain prominent azuro-
philic (i.e., blue/purple using Romanowsky stains) cytoplasmic 
granules, suggesting that they are of the NK cell lineage.
12.3.1.3  Thrombocytes
Thrombocytes (platelets [PLT]) and their precursors in bone 
marrow are collectively termed the thrombon. Platelets are 
generated in the bone marrow by megakaryocytes, which com-
prise approximately 1% of hematopoietic stem cells.263 Upon 
entering the circulation, platelets are available to form a pri-
mary platelet plug by binding to exposed subendothelial tissue 
at sites of endothelial damage in blood vessels. Hematologic 
analysis of peripheral blood measures the production and turn-
over of platelets, thus providing insight regarding the coagu-
lation process. The term thrombogram does not apply to 
analysis of platelet parameters but instead is reserved for quan-
tification of thrombin, an essential enzyme in thrombus (clot) 
formation that catalyzes the conversion of fibrinogen to fibrin. 
The weakly adhered initial platelet aggregate is anchored in 
place by fibrin deposition. Platelet numbers in healthy animals 
range from several hundred thousand to over a million per µL 
depending on the species, stock/strain/breed, sex, and age.
Increased platelet counts result from increased production 
or release of sequestered platelets from the sinusoids in the 
spleen. In toxicity studies, increased platelet counts usually 
arise as a secondary (indirect) effect associated with gener-
alized hematopoietic stimulation associated with hemolysis, 
hemorrhage, or inflammation (acute or chronic). Signaling 
molecules including some cytokines (IL-6, IL-11) and hema-
topoietic growth factors (erythropoietin) may act as ancillary 
drivers to enhance platelet production in such situations.23,177 
Platelet increases from these causes typically are small 
and therefore produce no biological effects in test animals. 
Increased platelet counts of modest degree may occur during 
physiological leukocytosis. Test article–related increases in 
platelet numbers as a primary (direct) outcome may occur if 
an agent acts on megakaryocytes as a hematopoietic growth 
factor (e.g., thrombopoietin) or cytotoxic agent. In humans, 
marked increases in platelet numbers (thrombocytosis) may 
contribute to a pro-thrombotic state, but this condition is rarely 
if ever encountered in animals during toxicity studies.
Decreased platelet counts arise by reduced produc-
tion, heightened consumption, or increased sequestration. 
Decreased platelet production usually develops after a gener-
alized cytotoxic insult to hematopoietic cells, and thus often 
occurs together with reduced generation of erythroid and 
myeloid cells. In general, the decline in circulating platelets 
occurs after neutrophil counts fall and well before erythro-
cyte numbers begin their drop, which reflects the half-lives of 
these cells in circulation. Decreased platelet counts resulting 
from increased consumption can reflect extensive hemorrhage 
at one or multiple sites. Severe and widespread thrombosis 
(i.e., disseminated intravascular coagulation [DIC]) leads to 
the formation of myriad microthrombi in small blood vessels, 
which often will manifest as a marked and fulminant decline 
in circulating platelet numbers. Immune-mediated destruction 
of platelets, which is induced by many drugs,353 occurs as an 
idiosyncratic, dose-independent response and so is rarely rec-
ognized in toxicity studies; when it does develop following test 
article administration, it typically occurs in only a few ani-
mals per group. In some cases, test articles produce decreased 
platelet counts by activating platelets as an off-target effect,

627
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
which results in their removal from the circulating pool.297 
Reduced platelet counts tend not to elicit clinical signs unless 
counts have fallen to ultra-low levels (<20,000/μL) or a hemo-
static challenge (e.g., major surgery) is superimposed, neither 
of which is likely to be encountered during routine toxicity 
studies. When present, however, clinical signs indicative of 
a platelet deficit are related to impaired coagulation leading 
to enhanced bleeding. In such cases, key manifestations in 
humans and animals include visible evidence of hemorrhage 
(external or internal) and sometimes prolonged bleeding from 
insignificant wounds (e.g., venipuncture sites).
In terms of routine hematology testing, platelet counts 
exhibit substantial variation among animals regardless of any 
treatment (whether test article or vehicle). Decreased platelet 
counts produced by cytotoxic agents or immune-mediated 
mechanisms often occur in tandem with reductions in the num-
bers of other blood cells, especially erythrocytes (for immune-
mediated declines) and neutrophils (for cytotoxic drugs).150 
Decreased platelet counts associated with an increase in mean 
platelet volume (MPV) are indicative of more immature plate-
lets in the circulation, which occurs when platelet production 
is increased in response to consumption. Low platelet counts 
are fairly common spurious findings seen during toxicity tests 
as a result of difficult sample collection (i.e., traumatic veni-
puncture) and/or inadequate anti-coagulation (e.g., failure to 
mix the blood and anticoagulant by inverting the blood tube). 
Platelet aggregation in vitro usually can be confirmed by 
observing platelet clumps at the edge of the blood smear or by 
observing an altered histogram (i.e., scatter-gram plots of cell 
size vs. cell number) produced by the hematology analyzer.331 
Although usually observed for only a few animals per study, 
these spurious decreases in platelet number sometimes may 
appear to be related to test article exposure because blood col-
lection from treated animals may be harder due to dehydration 
or reduced body size relative to control animals.
Special procedures may be useful in investigating plate-
let production and function. Flow cytometry may be used to 
characterize platelet density or reticulated platelets, which can 
help define the mechanism responsible for platelet decline.159 
Reticulated platelets are immature platelets that contain resid-
ual ribonucleic acid (RNA) (i.e., the platelet equivalent of an 
erythrocytic Retic). Decreased platelet counts with increased 
numbers of reticulated platelets arise from increased platelet 
consumption, while low platelet counts with reduced reticu-
lated platelet numbers reflect deficient production. Many 
platelet function tests (e.g. activation studies, aggregometry, 
thromboelastography) may be used to evaluate hemostatic and 
platelet disorders,260 but these assays generally are not used 
in toxicity studies due to substantial analytical and inter-indi-
vidual variability. If performed, the assessment may be more 
meaningful if the analysis compares the results for a given 
animal before and after test article exposure rather than the 
mean values for various treatment groups. Measurable effects 
on platelet function in vitro may have no in vivo counterpart.150
12.3.1.4  Bone Marrow Smears
The most critical aspect of bone marrow evaluation in toxicity 
testing is to understand when the analysis should and should 
not be performed.279 Collection of bone marrow and subse-
quent smear preparation is advisable for most repeat-dose tox-
icity studies since uncollected specimens cannot be retrieved 
at a later time. In general, though, bone marrow smears may 
be archived without evaluation as standard hematology assess-
ment using an automated, high-throughput instrument provides 
sufficient information regarding bone marrow health for safety 
screening. Therefore, bone marrow evaluation should only be 
undertaken to clarify unexplained hematologic findings. If 
an animal’s hemogram is unaffected or the effects on one or 
more circulating blood cell populations are fairly small, the 
communal experience of decades has shown that analysis of 
its bone marrow smear is extremely unlikely to add any addi-
tional evidence of a significant test article–related hematotoxic 
effect. Even if hematology data identify a test article–induced 
effect, evaluation of bone marrow provides no additional ben-
efit if the mechanisms leading to the hematologic changes are 
expected and/or can be discerned from the hemogram and his-
topathologic appearance of the bone marrow. Results of bone 
marrow smear evaluation should be integrated with findings 
from a histopathologic evaluation; bone marrow cytology pro-
vides better definition of cell features needed to discriminate 
major cell lineages and their stages (Figure 12.1A), while his-
topathology offers a more reliable estimate of cell numbers 
(Figure 12.1B) and major processes (e.g., tissue necrosis). In 
all cases, findings from cytological and/or histopathological 
evaluation of bone marrow should be interpreted in conjunc-
tion with concurrent hematologic results.
The primary indications for evaluating bone marrow smears 
in toxicity studies are to explore the mechanisms of moderate 
to marked non-regenerative decreased RBC mass, decreased 
leukocytes (for one or several WBC classes), and/or decreased 
platelets as a means of determining the cause of any significant 
cytological abnormalities observed in blood smears. The main 
objective of a bone marrow smear evaluation is to assess the 
relative numbers of hematopoietic precursor cells at various 
stages of maturation for all major lineages. In toxicity stud-
ies, bone marrow smears for cytological evaluation and bone 
marrow sections for histopathological evaluation typically are 
prepared using the femur (whole bone in rodents, proximal 
metaphysis in non-rodents) and/or sternum, but other bones 
may be used as long as they contain red (hematopoietic cell-
rich) marrow.279 Morphological analysis of spleen touch prep-
arations (cytology) and/or spleen sections (histopathology) in 
mice and rats may offer additional information regarding the 
sufficiency of hematopoiesis but cannot replace bone marrow 
assessment.
Three major approaches may be utilized in evaluating 
bone marrow smears. The first and simplest but least infor-
mative option is to calculate the myeloid-to-erythroid (M:E) 
ratio. This determination requires manual counting of nucle-
ated cells (typically 200–500 per smear) and classifying their 
lineage as either myeloid (granulocytic) or erythroid. An 
increased M:E ratio (i.e., granulocytes are more common than 
erythrocytes) may indicate enhanced granulocyte produc-
tion, reduced erythrocyte production, or a combination of the 
two. However, this same information can be obtained with 
less effort from the automated hemogram, so a bone marrow 
evaluation performed for only this endpoint typically is not

628
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
warranted. The second approach is to perform a bone mar-
row differential count. This labor-intensive option requires 
classification of at least 500 nucleated cells by lineage and 
stage of development. The resulting data indicate whether or 
not cell lineages are maturing normally, can be used to cal-
culate the M:E ratio, and permit the identification of abnor-
mal cytological details which may provide insight regarding 
the maturation process. In recent years, flow cytometric 
methods have been devised to quantify bone marrow cell 
types and stages more rapidly and accurately than manual 
counts.279 The third approach to bone marrow evaluation is 
a qualitative cytological examination. This option is both 
cost-effective and informative as the pathologist views a 
specimen and then records an interpretation reflecting key 
features (e.g., cellularity, relative percentages and matura-
tion state of the major cell lineages [erythrocytes, granulo-
cytes, and megakaryocytes]) averaged for the entire smear. 
Abnormal morphologic characteristics for expected cell 
types as well as unusually large numbers of other cell types 
(e.g., lymphocytes, plasma cells, monocytes, mast cells, and 
tumor cells) are recorded. Regardless of the approach, the 
objective of smear evaluation is to determine whether or not 
test article exposure impacts the number or maturation of 
hematopoietic precursors.28,279,282
12.3.2  Coagulation Tests
Automated coagulation analyzers can examine the time 
required for fibrin clot formation using 100–300 μL (depend-
ing on the analytical instrument) of citrated plasma (where 
citrate is used to temporarily remove the clotting cofactor 
Ca2+ until analysis). Commercial analyzers typically operate 
with software designed to accommodate species-specific dif-
ferences in clotting times. Among the noteworthy differences 
for laboratory animal species are the divergence in one-stage 
prothrombin times (PT) for dogs (e.g., relatively fast at 6–8 
seconds) and guinea pigs (e.g., fairly slow at 30–40 seconds). 
Coagulation times for control samples often shift slightly 
when a new reagent lot is introduced.
The coagulation process may be modeled in two fashions. 
The cascade model is built as a step-wise series of events 
where pro-enzymes are activated in two arms – an intrinsic 
pathway launched by endothelial damage that exposes sub-
endothelial molecules like collagen and an extrinsic pathway 
initiated by exposure to tissue factor expressed by cells deep in 
the vascular wall – that converge to act via a common terminal 
sequence of events.315 More recently, a cell-based model has 
integrated cell surfaces and clotting factors into a sequence of 
overlapping events (initiation, amplification, and propagation) 
that is thought to better reflect the mechanism of clot forma-
tion in vivo.315
Common coagulation tests used in toxicity studies afford 
suitable screening for one or both arms of the cascade model. 
Using a coagulation analyzer, the activated partial thrombo-
plastin time (APTT) and PT assess the intrinsic and extrin-
sic pathways, respectively. The activated coagulation time 
(ACT) is an alternative test for the intrinsic pathway that may 
be performed simply and rapidly without a coagulation ana-
lyzer although this assay is uncommonly performed in larger 
toxicity studies.48,300 These tests are nonspecific and rela-
tively insensitive; activity of a single clotting factor must fall 
by about 70% before a delay in an individual’s clotting time 
can be detected.150 That said, these tests may detect small but 
statistically significant differences in group means (e.g., pro-
longation by 2 seconds or less in high-dose animals relative 
to controls) that do not reflect a clinically evident pro-hemor-
rhagic tendency (termed a “bleeding diathesis”) but that may 
signal the existence of a potential test article–related effect on 
coagulation homeostasis.150 Fibrinogen concentration may be 
measured along with the APTT and PT as a coagulation test. 
However, in toxicity testing, the more common application of 
fibrinogen is as a positive acute-phase protein, the increased 
concentration of which is nearly always indicative of an acute-
phase (i.e., inflammatory) response.305
FIGURE 12.1  Morphological evaluation of bone marrow involves high-power magnification of smears (A) and histopathological evaluation of tissue 
sections (B). Cytology of smears provides better definition of cell features for various blood cell lineages, thereby allowing staging (i.e., differential 
counts) to assess maturation of key lineages and a detailed examination of cell-level pathological changes. Histopathology of sections permits more 
reliable estimation of cell numbers and tissue-level pathological processes. Adult control Sprague-Dawley rat.

629
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
The design of routine toxicity studies (intermittent but 
chronic repeated dosing to maintain high-dose exposure for 
extended periods) usually will reveal major test article–related 
effects on production of clotting factors by inducing a clini-
cally obvious bleeding diathesis. For example, administra-
tion of vitamin K antagonists will prolong the APTT and PT 
because vitamin K is required by the liver for production of 
clotting factors involved in the extrinsic (VII), intrinsic (IX), 
and final common (II and X) pathways of the coagulation 
cascade; in theory, the PT will be altered first because factor 
VII has the shortest half-life. Even though the liver produces 
nearly all clotting factors, delays in the APTT and PT are not 
affected until liver disease (including hepatotoxicity) is severe 
enough to exhaust the liver’s vast functional reserve. In DIC, 
simultaneous depletion of all clotting factors as well as plate-
let consumption results in moderately to markedly prolonged 
coagulation times. Indeed, in many instances, plasma samples 
from individuals with DIC fail to clot at all.
As with platelet counts, coagulation times in laboratory 
animals may be altered by elements other than test article 
exposure. For example, factor VII deficiency is an autosomal 
recessive hemostatic disorder in Beagles and some other dog 
breeds that are largely asymptomatic under normal conditions 
but that may yield prolonged bleeding following tissue trauma 
(e.g., experimental surgery).49 Affected individuals may be 
identified using PCR-based diagnostic assays for the mutant 
gene or by pre-study screening to detect an increased PT (typi-
cally 2–3 seconds longer than sex- and age-matched animals). 
Both APTT and PT may be prolonged spuriously by blood 
collection difficulties. Long coagulation times in combination 
with a decreased platelet count and/or low fibrinogen concen-
tration in an otherwise healthy animal generally is an indica-
tion of poor sample quality. Similarly, APTT and PT may be 
artifactually prolonged if the amount of citrate anticoagulant 
in the collection tube is excessive194; this situation may reflect 
allocation of insufficient blood volume when filling the tube, 
hemoconcentration (elevated Hct due to dehydration), or rarely 
test article–related polycythemia (increased RBC numbers in 
blood due to enhanced production in the bone marrow).
12.3.3  Clinical Chemistry Tests
Commercially available automated instruments for clinical 
chemistry analysis can quantify values for as many as 20 rou-
tine chemistry tests using 250 μL or less of either plasma or 
serum. Plasma and serum (hereafter referred to collectively as 
“serum” for convenience) comprise the approximately 45%–
55% of fluid present within a whole blood sample. Plasma is 
obtained by centrifuging anticoagulated blood to separate the 
blood cells from fluid. Thus, the protein fraction of plasma 
contains clotting proteins. Serum is produced by allowing 
whole blood (without added anticoagulant) to clot. Contraction 
of the clot leads to extrusion of the fluid; the protein fraction 
of this sample lacks clotting proteins. The usual sample in 
routine animal toxicity studies is serum. The small sample 
size allows for the acquisition of complete clinical chemistry 
profiles in adult rats and non-rodents at multiple time points 
during a study. Routinely performed clinical chemistry tests 
assess hepatic, metabolic, and renal function, among others. 
Most common clinical chemistry assays used in human diag-
nostic pathology may be utilized in animal toxicity studies.
12.3.3.1  Hepatobiliary Tests
Hepatobiliary tests are used to assess hepatocellular (i.e., 
primarily hepatocyte) and biliary (i.e., bile and bile duct epi-
thelium) integrity, in particular, the excretory, metabolic, and 
synthetic functions of major cell populations in a key organ 
targeted by many toxicants.361 The battery of hepatobiliary 
tests that comprise a “hepatic panel” are sometimes referred 
to incorrectly as “liver function tests” (LFTs), but in fact, 
only some of the tests are related to liver function (e.g., serum 
albumin levels) while others assess the cellular integrity but 
not function of hepatocytes (e.g., serum activities of alanine 
[ALT] and aspartate AST] aminotransferases) or biliary epi-
thelium (e.g., ALP and GGT).198 A considerable amount of 
parenchymal mass may be lost with little or no functional 
change in common chemistry tests due to the enormous func-
tional reserve. No single hepatic function test is able to detect 
all types of primary or secondary toxic liver injury. Instead, 
the pattern of abnormal findings obtained when evaluating 
a battery of hepatobiliary-focused tests helps in identifying 
the location and estimating the severity of toxicant-induced 
lesions in the liver.322,361
12.3.3.1.1  Hepatocellular Markers – Increased 
Serum Enzyme Activities
Serum activities of liver enzymes do not measure liver func-
tion but rather are employed to identify hepatocyte injury and 
cholestasis (i.e., slowed bile excretion), occurring with or with-
out the presence of bile duct epithelial injury. Enzyme activ-
ity (i.e., the amount of substrate converted to a product in a 
particular length of time) is reported in international units per 
liter (IU/L) and should not be confused with (or reported as) 
an enzyme concentration or level (i.e., the amount of protein in 
a given volume). Hepatocyte enzymes are sometimes referred 
to as “hepatocellular leakage enzymes” since the normal cell 
location of these proteins is in the hepatocyte cytosol and their 
presence in serum often results from a loss of plasma mem-
brane integrity (typically during cell degeneration or necrosis) 
leading to escape of cytosolic enzymes from injured hepato-
cytes and not a change in liver function. Accordingly, liver 
enzyme activities cannot automatically be tied to the level 
of liver function. Focal necrotic lesions may lead to a large 
increase in serum liver enzyme activities without any impact 
on liver function (due to the large functional reserve provided 
by the remaining parenchyma). Similarly, sizable differences 
in serum activities of liver enzymes may be detected among 
concurrent control animals, especially rodents and NHPs, 
which may mask subtle hepatotoxic effects in test article–
treated animals. Conversely, severe, diffuse hepatocyte deple-
tion with fibrosis (i.e., cirrhosis) may present with little or no 
elevation in serum enzyme activities since the extensive hepa-
tocellular loss occurred primarily in the past. Taken together, 
these patterns indicate that the absence of measurable serum 
liver enzyme activities does not preclude the possibility that 
the test article has produced a hepatotoxic effect.

630
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
The specificity and sensitivity, and thus the predictive value, 
of liver enzyme assays depend chiefly on the kind of hepa-
tocellular damage, which varies with the disease model and 
toxicant. For this reason, clinical chemistry analysis as a diag-
nostic tool for hepatotoxicity during animal toxicity studies 
is based on assessing a battery of liver enzymes. In general, 
hepatocellular disturbance or injury is accompanied by simul-
taneous increases in the serum activities of several enzymes. 
The utility of any single liver enzyme in assessing the location 
and extent of hepatocellular injury depends on many factors, 
including the enzyme’s distribution in the body, intrahepatic 
and intracellular locations, the gradient between cellular and 
serum enzyme concentrations (i.e., quantity of intracellular 
and extracellular protein molecules), the serum half-life for 
enzyme activity, stability within the sample, and cost.41,182,361 
For toxicity studies, the clinical chemistry battery routinely 
assays serum activities of alanine (ALT) and aspartate (AST) 
aminotransferases.37 Less often, activities of glutamate dehy-
drogenase (GDH or GLDH), lactate dehydrogenase (LDH), 
and/or SDH are measured as indices of hepatocellular injury.150
In general, ALT is the most valuable enzyme for detecting 
hepatocellular injury in most species. While present in other tis-
sues in small quantities, ALT is present in highest quantities in 
hepatocytes of most animal species (e.g., most rodents, dogs, 
NHPs) used in toxicity testing. The ALT concentration inside 
cells of these species is up to 10,000-fold higher than the enzyme 
levels in serum. Accordingly, significantly elevated serum ALT 
activity following test article exposure typically reflects release 
of intracellular (cytosolic) ALT from injured hepatocytes. 
Animal species with relatively low hepatocyte ALT concentra-
tions, for which serum ALT activity is less useful as a marker of 
liver toxicity, include selected laboratory animals such as guinea 
pigs56 and minipigs as well as large domestic animals including 
cattle, goats, sheep, horses, and standard pigs.41,161,330
Release of ALT usually results from frank disruption of 
membrane integrity (e.g., degeneration or necrosis), but other 
mechanisms (e.g., bleb formation) may allow enzyme transfer 
across the membrane as high serum ALT activities may occur 
in the absence of cell death.318 The magnitude of serum ALT 
activity is proportional to the number of affected hepatocytes. 
Following an acute hepatotoxic event, serum ALT activity rises 
rapidly, peaking at 1–2 days before declining rapidly over the 
next 2–4 days; biologically significant liver toxicity may be 
missed if clinical chemistry testing is delayed until 1 or 2 weeks 
following initial test article exposure. Sustained elevation of 
ALT activity in serum after a single toxic insult may indicate 
either robust regeneration of liver tissue (which is accompa-
nied by extensive ALT production and a proportional leakage 
of freshly constituted enzyme from newly formed cells) or/and 
continued loss of ALT from additional rounds of hepatocyte 
damage. As a rule, increases in serum ALT activity above 200 
IU/L typically are attended by histopathologic lesions indicative 
of hepatocyte damage, while elevated serum activities below 
this threshold may or may not be accompanied by microscopic 
findings in the liver.150 The presence of high serum ALT activity 
has no implications with respect to lesion reversibility except in 
so far as extremely high values suggest extensive damage and 
that the individual might expire before acute liver damage may 
be repaired by hepatocyte regeneration.
Serum ALT activity may be increased by conditions other 
than primary hepatocyte injury. For example, biliary disease 
may indirectly produce elevated serum ALT activity as bile sta-
sis permits accumulating bile salts to destabilize the cell mem-
branes of adjacent hepatocytes. In like manner, severe and/or 
widespread damage to skeletal muscle may cause increased 
serum ALT activity in the absence of hepatic injury.146 Certain 
drugs (e.g., anticonvulsants, glucocorticoids) have been linked 
to increased serum ALT activity, presumably as a consequence 
of increased protein release associated with increased intracel-
lular enzyme concentrations.318 Elevated serum ALT activity 
may result from subclinical hepatic disease, such as enzootic 
hepatitis A infection in NHPs.313 Increased serum ALT also 
may arise from damage associated with restraint that affects 
either the liver (in mice) or skeletal muscle (in mice, rats, dogs, 
and NHPs).150 Indeed, in mouse studies, a few animals, includ-
ing controls, often have relatively high serum ALT activities 
(usually 200–400 IU/L) in the absence of substantial hepato-
toxic effects.328
In most toxicity studies, serum AST or LDH activities are 
measured along with ALT activity as potential measures of 
hepatotoxicity. Serum activities of AST and LDH typically 
rise in tandem with serum ALT activity when liver damage 
has occurred.361 However, AST and LDH activities both are 
less specific to liver since they also occur in large amounts 
in other tissues, particularly cardiac239 and skeletal146 muscle. 
In rodent toxicity studies, both AST and LDH exhibit greater 
variability among animals, including controls; of the two, 
LDH is more variable, so for routine toxicity studies, AST and 
ALT assays are performed to probe hepatocellular integrity. 
In general, hepatotoxicity leads to less pronounced increases 
in serum AST activity relative to ALT activity, likely because 
AST is divided between the cytosolic and mitochondrial com-
partments as well as the relatively shorter half-life of cytosolic 
AST vs. cytosolic ALT. A greater degree of hepatocyte dam-
age presumably is required to release the mitochondrial AST 
fraction. As with ALT, elevated AST intracellular levels and 
serum activity have been associated with administration of 
certain drugs (e.g., anticonvulsants, glucocorticoids).
Increased serum GDH (GLDH) and SDH activities have 
been proposed as specific and relatively sensitive markers of 
acute hepatotoxicity in laboratory animals,355,361 and thus may 
be especially appropriate as additional hepatic function tests 
when liver injury is a particular concern.114 Although these 
assays are useful markers, enzyme stability and reagent avail-
ability can limit their use in nonclinical toxicity studies with 
standard species (e.g., rodents, dogs, and NHPs). In terms of 
cell location, GDH is found in mitochondria while SDH is con-
fined to the cytosol. Serum GDH activity is more specific for 
hepatotoxicity than ALT as it is not impacted by skeletal mus-
cle damage or glucocorticoid exposure, and in some instances, 
elevated serum GDH activity as a marker of liver injury in 
rats appears to rise higher and persist longer than associated 
changes in serum ALT activity.240 Serum GDH or SDH activ-
ity is particularly useful as a hepatotoxicity marker in species 
like the guinea pig and minipig because ALT is not a spe-
cific indicator of liver injury in these species.361 Elevations in 
SDH activity fall much more quickly due to its short half-life 
in serum relative to those of ALT, AST, and GDH. For this

631
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
reason, a liver profile in which serum ALT activity is elevated 
markedly but serum SDH activity is not altered suggests the 
presence of ongoing hepatocyte damage.161
12.3.3.1.2  Hepatocellular Markers – Decreased 
Serum Enzyme Activities
In routine toxicity studies, low serum ALT and AST activities 
are observed occasionally. The most likely causes for this pro-
file are reduced enzyme synthesis and release (i.e. diminished 
cell function), enzyme inhibition, and assay interference.150 
For example, test article–related changes in the availability of 
pyridoxal 5΄-phosphate (vitamin B6, a cofactor needed for ami-
notransferase activity) have been shown to lower the activi-
ties for these enzymes in both cells and serum.73,216 Decreased 
ALT and AST activities seen during animal toxicity studies 
generally do not reflect biologically significant effects on liver 
function and structure.
12.3.3.1.3  Hepatobiliary Markers – Increased 
Serum Enzyme Activities
ALP and GGT are two enzymes in the apical microvilli (“brush 
border”) of bile duct epithelial cells that are more specific and 
sensitive markers of hepatobiliary than hepatocellular injury. 
Serum ALP and GGT activities are increased in toxicity stud-
ies when a test article induces biliary hyperplasia (i.e., prolif-
eration of bile duct epithelium with concomitant elevations in 
enzyme synthesis); cholestasis (due to intrahepatic or extrahe-
patic obstruction, both of which lead to intrahepatic bile sta-
sis and plasma membrane disruption due to accumulating bile 
salts); or altered bile composition. 5΄-Nucleotidase (5NT), an 
enzyme found in numerous cell types, also may serve as a sen-
sitive marker of hepatobiliary disease, but in routine toxicity 
studies, ALP (all species) and GGT (especially non-rodents) 
are more likely to be included in the clinical chemistry panel.
Serum ALP activity reflects the combined action of mul-
tiple related enzymes produced by several tissues. In labo-
ratory animals, two ALP genes code for intestinal and 
tissue-nonspecific isoenzymes. The primary serum sources for 
the tissue-nonspecific isoenzyme are bone, kidney, and liver; 
the enzymes from these organs are isoforms (i.e., proteins 
encoded by the same gene) that may be differentiated based 
on unique patterns of posttranslational glycosylation.160,358,362 
In contrast, humans possess at least four ALP genes that code 
for different ALP isoenzymes: germ cell, intestinal, placental, 
and tissue-nonspecific (in bone, kidney, and liver). In all spe-
cies, the contribution of each isoenzyme and isoform to total 
serum ALP activity depends on the serum half-life and extent 
of tissue production. For example, bone ALP is made by osteo-
blasts and so is a significant contributor to serum ALP activity 
mainly in young animals. Serum ALP activity is predomi-
nantly related to the bone isoform in 6-week-old rats (60% 
of the total160) and puppies (95% of the total150). In adult ani-
mals, serum ALP activity is comprised primarily of the liver 
isoform. The intestinal isoenzyme and kidney isoform have 
short half-lives and thus are rarely identified in animal serum, 
although the contribution of the intestinal isoenzyme fluctu-
ates in rats with feed availability (higher after feeding, lower 
when fasting).348 A distinctive steroid-induced ALP activity 
reflecting a hyperglycosylated isoform of the intestinal ALP 
isoenzyme is produced in the livers of dogs in response to high 
levels of endogenous or administered glucocorticoids.358 This 
steroid-induced variant has been detected in dogs experienc-
ing chronic stress (reviewed in Ref. No. 116116), but to date, 
it has not been reported as a significant contributor to serum 
ALP activity in dogs enrolled in toxicity studies.
Increased serum ALP activity in laboratory animals 
(regardless of the combination of ALP types) generally signals 
the presence of biliary effects. In the rat and especially the 
dog, this analyte is a sensitive marker that usually rises well 
in advance of GGT activity and other indicators of biliary dis-
ease (e.g., total bilirubin); serum ALP activity in NHPs is less 
useful in identifying cholestatic conditions due to the higher 
inter-individual variability in this species.150 Extrahepatic 
cholestasis due to bile duct obstruction (e.g., due to choleliths 
[bile concretions, or “stones”], inflammation or neoplasia of 
the pancreas, or traumatic bile duct cannulation) is associated 
with higher serum ALP activity than intrahepatic cholestasis 
since bile flow from the entire liver is diminished or halted. 
Intrahepatic cholestasis leads to a lesser increase in serum 
ALP activity, with hepatic lesions near portal areas (i.e., the 
location of larger bile ducts that collect bile from entire liver 
lobules) leading to greater elevations in comparison to cen-
trilobular lesions (i.e., the location of small-caliber bile can-
aliculi that deliver bile to the portal bile ducts). Non-biliary 
liver damage, such as primary hepatocellular toxicity of mild 
to moderate severity, may increase serum ALP activity if 
damaged hepatocytes swell sufficiently to occlude small bile 
ductules.150 Certain drugs (e.g., anticonvulsants, glucocorti-
coids) may induce the synthesis of the liver ALP isoform with 
or without causing hepatobiliary disease. In dogs treated with 
corticosteroids, the liver isoform of ALP rises more rapidly 
(by day 3 post-exposure) than the steroid-induced isoform 
(starting at day 7), while other LFTs (ALT and GGT activities) 
begin to increase at day 5.319
Increased serum GGT activity is an increasingly popular 
marker of hepatobiliary disease due to its greater specificity 
relative to ALP activity in certain rat models of biliary toxic-
ity. Causes of elevated GGT activity include biliary epithelial 
necrosis and cholestasis in all species as well as enhanced 
synthesis of microsomal enzymes following administration of 
certain drugs (e.g., anticonvulsants, glucocorticoids).361 Serum 
GGT activity is less likely than ALP activity to be elevated as 
an indirect consequence of primary hepatocellular toxicity or 
intrahepatic cholestasis due to swelling of injured hepatocytes. 
The impact on GGT activity depends on the sample and spe-
cies. For example, phenobarbital treatment induces high cellu-
lar and serum GGT activities in rabbits, and modestly greater 
cellular GGT activity but no serum GGT activity in rats.287 In 
fact, serum GGT activity is often undetectable in rodents, so 
even small increases may be significant.
Serum 5NT activity has been proposed as an alternative to 
ALP and GGT measurements as a specific marker for hepa-
tobiliary diseases. This enzyme is found in many cell types 
of numerous tissues; in the liver, 5NT is localized to the 
plasma membrane of hepatocyte surfaces that form the bile 
canaliculi and sinusoids as well as on connective tissue in 
the portal area.361 In animal models of cholestasis, the serum 
increase in 5NT activity is reported to mirror those observed

632
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
for serum ALP and GGT activities.192 This similarity coupled 
with generally greater familiarity with existing ALP and GGT 
methods has limited acceptance of 5NT as a routine endpoint 
in toxicity studies.277
A potential confounder to interpretation of ALP values is 
that serum ALP activity may serve as an indicator of bone for-
mation. Increased serum ALP activity arising from escalated 
osteoclast activity usually is less pronounced (no more than 
2- to 3-fold higher relative to control) than the greater eleva-
tions associated with cholestasis.150 If a test article is likely to 
induce biological effects in both liver and bone, measurement 
of the bone-specific ALP isoform may be warranted. As an 
alternative, serum GGT and 5NT activities are not altered in 
individuals undergoing bone growth or disease, so their analy-
sis may prove beneficial in distinguishing between hepatobili-
ary and bone conditions.
12.3.3.1.4  Hepatobiliary Markers – Increased Serum 
Concentrations of Bile Constituents
Bilirubin and bile acids are key serum components that fluctu-
ate depending on liver function. Serum concentrations of TBil 
and total bile acids may be utilized in combination with other 
analytes to provide evidence of potential hepatobiliary disease, 
but they do not discriminate by themselves between differ-
ent kinds of hepatotoxic disease. Their sensitivity as markers 
for hepatotoxicity is reduced to some degree due to the large 
hepatic functional reserve (for total bilirubin) and fairly high 
inter- and intra-animal variability (for total bile acids).
Bilirubin is a byproduct of heme degradation that occurs 
when senescent erythrocytes are taken in and broken down 
by mononuclear phagocytes. Bilirubin is released from phago-
cytes as an unconjugated (also termed free or pre-hepatic) 
molecule. This water-insoluble form travels in the blood 
while bound to albumin, a major blood protein. Upon reach-
ing the liver, unconjugated bilirubin is taken up by hepato-
cytes, conjugated to glucuronic acid by uridine 5’-diphospho 
(UDP)-glucuronosyltransferase (UDPGT) to bolster its water 
solubility, and secreted into the bile canaliculi for excretion 
into the intestine; a small portion of conjugated bilirubin is 
released into the blood. Therefore, serum TBil is a mixture 
of both unconjugated and conjugated forms. In the absence of 
widespread hemolysis, an increased serum TBil concentration 
(or “hyperbilirubinemia”) reflects abnormal uptake, metabo-
lism, secretion, and/or excretion of bilirubin by the liver. The 
mechanism responsible for the increase may be explored by 
defining whether the elevation results from accumulation of 
the conjugated, unconjugated, or both forms of bilirubin (often 
there will be an increase in both types, with a more predomi-
nant increase in one or the other).
Increased conjugated bilirubin levels in serum reflect liver 
disease and result from impaired secretion of bilirubin from 
hepatocytes, cholestasis, or both. Secretion of conjugated bili-
rubin into bile canaliculi is the rate-limiting step in hepatocyte 
processing of unconjugated bilirubin, so any delay in transfer-
ring conjugated bilirubin into the bile will result in enhanced 
release of the molecule into the blood. Higher serum bilirubin 
concentrations are more characteristic of hepatocyte lesions 
affecting the periportal rather than centrilobular zones and 
occur more readily during extrahepatic cholestasis relative to 
intrahepatic cholestasis.150 When the elevated serum bilirubin 
concentration arises from cholestasis, serum ALP activity is 
generally higher as well, particularly in the dog.
In contrast, increased unconjugated bilirubin levels usually 
stem from acute, relatively severe hemolysis. The high amount 
of circulating unconjugated bilirubin overloads the liver’s abil-
ity to process the molecule. A hemolytic event sufficient to 
overwhelm a normal liver always occurs in tandem with other 
pathology findings indicative of hemolysis (e.g., reduced RBC 
and increased Retic counts). While uncommon, comparatively 
modest hemolysis may produce an increase in serum unconju-
gated bilirubin levels if liver integrity and function has already 
been compromised.
The Van den Bergh test may be used to distinguish con-
jugated (or direct) from unconjugated (or indirect) bilirubin. 
This assay is utilized in diagnostic pathology practice to 
discriminate pre-hepatic (e.g., hemolysis) from hepatic (e.g., 
inflammation, parenchymal loss) and post-hepatic (e.g., biliary 
obstruction) causes of bilirubin accumulation. This assay typi-
cally is not performed during toxicity studies as other clinical 
pathology values (e.g., decreased hematocrit, increased serum 
liver enzyme activities) and anatomic pathology findings (e.g., 
hemosiderin accumulation in macrophages in the splenic red 
pulp, periportal hepatocellular necrosis) permit identification 
of the mechanisms responsible for hyperbilirubinemia. The 
correlation between the conjugation state of bilirubin and the 
underlying mechanisms is often unclear for small increases 
observed in toxicity studies, so laboratory testing to distinguish 
conjugated vs. unconjugated bilirubin should not be used as a 
routine endpoint in toxicity testing.150 In special instances, bil-
irubin fractionation may be helpful in determining the mecha-
nism of increased serum bilirubin concentration. For example, 
test articles that inhibit the enzyme UDPGT, which conjugates 
bilirubin to glucuronic acid, may produce substantial eleva-
tions in serum unconjugated bilirubin in the absence of any 
other liver effects.37,375
Unexpected serum bilirubin profiles may be detected on 
occasion. For example, apparent increases in serum bilirubin 
concentration may be produced by test articles that interfere 
with the routine diazo enzymatic assay used to quantify bili-
rubin levels. In such cases, an alternative vanadate method has 
been shown to reliably determine the total and direct (con-
jugated) bilirubin concentrations in serum of rats, dogs, and 
NHPs.9 Decreased concentrations of serum bilirubin may be 
seen infrequently if the test article induces synthesis of micro-
somal enzymes, including those involved in bilirubin conjuga-
tion.1,140 Enzyme induction enhances the excretion of bilirubin, 
which in principle could mask a modest elevation in serum 
bilirubin.
Like serum bilirubin, serum total bile acid concentration 
measures both hepatocyte and biliary function. Bile acids are 
synthesized in hepatocytes by conjugation of an amino acid 
with cholesterol. The resulting molecules are secreted into the 
bile and excreted in the proximal small intestine (duodenum) 
where they aid in digestion of fats.215 Bile acids are recycled 
efficiently (i.e., enterohepatic circulation), with most reab-
sorption taking place in the caudal small intestine (ileum). 
Hepatotoxicity may lead to increased serum total bile acid 
concentrations. However, serum bile acid analysis has not

633
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
enhanced the likelihood of identifying hepatotoxic test articles 
to a sufficient degree to warrant its inclusion as a component of 
routine liver function testing in toxicity studies.
12.3.3.1.5  Novel Liver-Specific Markers
Drug-induced liver injury is the most common cause of 
safety-related test article attrition in therapeutic develop-
ment.176 New hepatic function markers are of interest as a 
means of enhancing the predictivity and improving mecha-
nistic understanding of test article–related toxic effects to the 
liver.55,132 For example, in comparison to conventional liver 
function analytes, liver-specific microRNAs like miR-122 
rise more quickly during hepatotoxicity in rats, dogs, and 
humans and exhibit greater stability. However, miRs also are 
released actively in the absence of liver injury and display 
considerable inter-individual variability. Similarly, macro-
phage colony-stimulating factor receptor (MCSFR) released 
by activated hepatic sinusoidal macrophages (Kupffer cells) 
has been used as an indicator of idiosyncratic hepatotoxicity 
in humans. Keratin 18 (K18) is a cytoskeletal protein that is 
highly expressed in hepatocytes and bile duct epithelium (as 
well as other single-layer epithelial cells of other organs). In 
hepatotoxicity, K18 and a caspase-cleaved fragment desig-
nated caspase-cleaved keratin 18 (ccK18) are being explored 
as potential markers for cell death by necrosis and apoptosis, 
respectively. In general, these novel markers have not been 
investigated systemically or undergone robust validations as 
yet for use in nonclinical toxicity studies.
12.3.3.1.6  Other Liver-Related Markers
The liver is instrumental in metabolism as a whole, being par-
tially or wholly responsible for the synthesis of many essen-
tial molecules (e.g., cholesterol, glucose, numerous proteins) 
and key waste products (e.g., urea). Severe hepatocellular dys-
function impacts many clinical pathology analytes. The usual 
clinical pathology profile for liver disease is decreased serum 
concentrations of cholesterol, glucose, protein (especially albu-
min and many essential clotting factors), and/or urea. However, 
in some cases, increased serum levels of cholesterol and globu-
lins (a key protein class) may occur. The patterns of clinical 
pathology abnormalities induced for these other liver-related 
markers by hepatotoxic test articles overlap. Accordingly, these 
markers must be assessed in combination with the complete 
battery of liver function assays as well as anatomic pathology 
findings in order to properly determine the impact of test article 
administration on liver integrity and function.
12.3.3.2  Renal Function
Renal function tests are used to assess the excretory capabilities 
of an organ targeted by many toxicants. Altered renal function 
is a frequent consequence of test article–induced kidney tox-
icity in animal toxicity studies. However, the abundant func-
tional reserve often means that little or no functional change 
will be detected using routine chemistry tests unless a large 
amount of parenchymal tissue is lost. No single renal function 
test detects all categories of toxic kidney injury. Instead, the 
pattern of abnormal findings observed when a battery of renal 
function tests is employed helps in identifying the location and 
estimating the severity of toxicant-induced lesions in the kid-
ney.39,43,322 A host of novel renal biomarkers are undergoing 
evaluation as possible means for better bridging nonclinical 
findings to clinical monitoring.92,242
Renal function may be evaluated using blood (plasma or 
serum) or urine samples. The most common blood-based 
renal function tests measure serum concentrations of two 
nitrogenous waste products: urea nitrogen (often referred to 
as “blood urea nitrogen” [BUN] or simply “urea nitrogen”) 
and creatinine. In toxicity studies, these assays commonly 
are paired with urine-based renal function tests (described 
in Section 12.3.4 “Urinalysis”) that quantify urine volume 
and concentration (e.g., urine specific gravity or osmolal-
ity) and provide semi-quantitative information regarding the 
biochemical composition of urine.39,322 This battery of tests 
provides a simple, rapid, and inexpensive screen for substan-
tial renal toxicity, but the combination is relatively insensi-
tive to modest effects on renal function. A key consideration 
when interpreting such data is that renal function may be 
impacted by non-renal factors.
12.3.3.2.1  Standard Renal Markers – Serum Urea 
Nitrogen and Serum Creatinine
Urea is synthesized by the liver from ammonia released by 
protein catabolism or absorbed from the intestine. Circulating 
urea is filtered freely into the urine via the glomerulus. 
Excreted urea is reabsorbed passively (along with water) in 
the proximal tubule; urea reabsorption rises as the urine flow 
rate falls. Therefore, the serum urea nitrogen concentration is 
influenced by its rate of production, the glomerular filtration 
rate (GFR), and the rate of urine flow within renal tubules. 
An increased serum urea nitrogen concentration (termed azo-
temia) may occur due to prerenal, renal, or postrenal mecha-
nisms. Toxicants may share in any of these mechanisms.
Prerenal causes of increased serum urea nitrogen include 
increased urea synthesis and lower renal blood flow. Increased 
urea synthesis occurs following consumption of high-protein 
diets or in disease states that enhance protein catabolism. 
During toxicity studies, protein catabolism may be elevated 
if animals develop cachexia of chronic disease (e.g., cancer or 
inflammation), severe gastrointestinal hemorrhage, or exten-
sive tissue necrosis; starvation, another cause of increased 
protein catabolism, should not be a consideration in a well-
conducted toxicity study. Lower renal blood flow (which 
reduces the GFR and thus diminishes urea nitrogen filtration) 
typically results from hypotension and usually is associated 
with chronic heart disease, acute shock, test article–induced 
vasodilation, and reduced blood volume (e.g., decreased GFR 
or dehydration – the most common prerenal mechanisms of 
azotemia in toxicity studies – or hemorrhage). The extent of 
increased serum urea nitrogen is typically small if driven 
by prerenal causes, although severe reductions in GFR may 
yield larger increases that are indistinguishable from changes 
induced by primary renal failure. In addition, renal concen-
trating ability generally is maintained when increased urea 
nitrogen results from prerenal causes. If the prerenal mecha-
nism is dehydration, the increase in serum urea nitrogen will 
occur together with a decreased urine volume and increased 
urine concentration.

634
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
Renal causes of increased serum urea nitrogen involve dis-
eases, including toxicity, affecting the renal parenchyma. In 
clinical practice, serum urea nitrogen does not rise significantly 
until the renal functional reserve (i.e., about 75% of the neph-
rons [excretory units, consisting of a glomerulus and its renal 
tubule] in both kidneys) has been exhausted. However, in toxic-
ity studies, differences between concurrent control and test arti-
cle–treated animals typically can be distinguished before such 
drastic damage has taken place.150 Renal causes of increased 
serum urea nitrogen often are accompanied by a reduced spe-
cific gravity (i.e., diminished ability to concentrate urine) and 
histopathologic evidence of renal damage (e.g., CPN, a common 
spontaneous background finding in aged [especially male] rats, 
and test article–induced renal tubular necrosis).
Postrenal causes of increased serum urea nitrogen involve 
obstruction of urine outflow, which indirectly increases the 
pressure within renal tubules and culminates in reduced GFR. 
Known toxic causes of urinary tract obstruction in laboratory 
animals include urinary calculi (filling the renal pelvis or uri-
nary bladder) and neoplasms.
Creatinine is produced at a relatively constant rate by non-
enzymatic degradation of creatine, a non-protein breakdown 
product of phosphocreatine (an energy storage substrate in 
muscle). The serum creatinine concentration is independent of 
diet and protein catabolism but may be influenced by skeletal 
muscle mass and physical conditioning. Like urea, creatinine is 
freely filtered by the glomerulus. Unlike urea, excreted creati-
nine is not reabsorbed in renal tubules. Thus, serum creatinine
