is usually a better reflection of glomerular function since it is 
affected by fewer factors than serum urea nitrogen. Increases 
in serum creatinine parallel those of serum urea nitrogen, but 
creatinine levels rise more slowly and achieve a lower peak 
concentration relative to urea nitrogen. Creatinine measure-
ments made using standard chemical methods (i.e., the Jaffe 
reaction) may be impacted by the presence of non-creatinine 
chromogens. The ability of such chromogens to interfere with 
creatinine determinations may be explored by expensive enzy-
matic techniques, but this non-routine approach is typically 
reserved for situations in which increased serum creatinine 
concentrations occur in the absence of increased serum urea 
nitrogen levels or renal histopathologic lesions.150
12.3.3.2.2  Other Kidney-Related Serum Markers
Significant renal impairment may be accompanied by other 
serum chemistry abnormalities. In general, these are related to 
altered serum levels of electrolytes and minerals. For example, 
decreased sodium and chloride levels result from abnormal 
tubular epithelial function leading to reduced electrolyte reab-
sorption. Similarly, increased serum phosphorus concentra-
tion typically reflects reduced glomerular filtration. Clinical 
pathology practices related to measurement of these analytes 
are described more completely below.
12.3.3.3  Metabolic Function
Broadly defined, metabolism involves the synthesis, circula-
tion, and degradation or excretion of essential cell components 
within the body. Clinical chemistry assays exist to measure a 
large number of essential proteins, carbohydrates, and lipids.
12.3.3.3.1  Metabolic Markers – Serum Proteins
Serum total protein concentration is a measure of all plasma 
proteins with the exception of those consumed in coagulation 
(clot formation). Accordingly, serum total protein is mod-
estly lower (by 0.3–0.5 g/dL) relative to plasma total protein. 
Albumin is the most abundant serum protein and acts as a car-
rier for many endogenous molecules that lack specific trans-
port proteins (e.g., unconjugated bilirubin) as well as many test 
articles. Globulins are a heterogeneous class of proteins sup-
porting diverse functions: clotting factors, enzymes, immune 
reactions (antibodies [i.e., immunoglobulins] and inflamma-
tion mediators [e.g., acute-phase proteins, complement]), and 
specific transport proteins (e.g., haptoglobin for hemoglobin, 
lipoproteins for lipids, and transferrin for iron). Globulins may 
be categorized loosely based on their migration in an elec-
tric field as α, β, or γ globulins, but specific proteins cannot 
be identified in an electrophoretogram.83 Immunoglobulins 
are generally classified as γ globulins, but some (especially 
IgM) also migrate within the β region. The liver (specifically 
hepatocytes) synthesizes albumin and most globulins, while 
B-lymphocytes and plasma cells produce immunoglobu-
lins. Serum concentrations of total protein and albumin are 
measured directly, while the serum globulin concentration is 
calculated by subtracting the albumin value from the total pro-
tein value. The calculated albumin-to-globulin (A/G [or A:G]) 
ratio may be used as a rapid means of comparing serum pro-
tein fractions.
Increased serum protein concentrations during toxicity stud-
ies most often reflect dehydration (either subclinical or clini-
cal). In such instances, serum albumin and globulin levels are 
increased proportionally. Possible correlative clinical obser-
vations of dehydration include gastrointestinal fluid loss (e.g., 
diarrhea, emesis, excessive salivation), polyuria (increased 
urination), and decreased water consumption. The other major 
cause of high serum protein concentrations is inflammation, 
which manifests as enhanced synthesis of immunoglobu-
lins and/or acute-phase proteins (another class of globulins). 
In general, animals mounting an immune response produce 
a spectrum of acute-phase proteins, but the spectrum varies 
with the species (Table 12.6).63,305 These newly synthesized 
molecules are “positive” acute-phase proteins as their concen-
trations rise during the immune response; the serum albumin 
concentration often falls simultaneously since albumin serves 
as a negative acute-phase indicator. The opposing shifts in 
serum albumin and globulin concentrations may cancel each 
other out, resulting in an unchanged serum total protein con-
centration, but the reduced A/G ratio will serve to identify an 
underlying disease state. The serum protein profile is observed 
with some frequency in animals in toxicity studies that have 
experienced long-term intravenous catheterization.150
Decreased serum protein concentrations in toxicity studies 
stem from either reduced protein synthesis or increased protein 
loss. Common causes of diminished protein synthesis include 
low food consumption, gastrointestinal dysfunction (either 
maldigestion or malabsorption), or liver damage. Comparison 
of serum protein levels between test article–exposed and 
concurrent control animals may unveil small differences in 
protein synthesis due to relatively modest degrees of hepa-
totoxicity. Protein loss occurs by many mechanisms, and the

635
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
type(s) of decreased protein provide an indication regarding 
the mechanism of protein loss. Both albumin and globulins are 
diminished with exudative lesions (e.g., severe dermal necro-
sis in which the cutaneous barrier is broken), hemorrhage, and 
infrequently with severe diarrhea. Albumin is smaller in size 
relative to globulins, so albumin is more likely to be lost in 
protein-losing intestinal (enteropathy) and renal (nephropathy) 
diseases. In intestinal disease, serum globulin concentrations 
may increase in response to systemic exposure to invasive bac-
teria or absorbed bacterial toxins.
A small reduction in serum albumin concentration is one 
of the most common findings in animals administered poorly 
tolerated test articles. This pattern is usually interpreted as a 
nonspecific indicator of the individual’s overall poor condi-
tion, especially if it is accompanied by small decreases in other 
clinical observations (e.g., total body weight and body weight 
gain) and/or clinical pathology parameters (e.g., hematocrit, 
serum cholesterol and/or glucose concentrations). Decreased 
serum albumin concentration is noted more often in rodents 
than non-rodents. This species dependency may reflect the 
faster turnover of albumin in small animals.83 The half-life of 
albumin is approximately 1 day in mice, 2 days in rats, 8 days 
for dogs, 16 days for baboons, and 21 days in humans.
Reduced serum globulin concentrations without propor-
tional decreases in serum albumin may be indicative of lower 
immunoglobulin synthesis. A concurrent reduction in the 
absolute lymphocyte count and/or histopathologic lesions in 
lymphoid organs provide additional evidence to support this 
interpretation. Decreased serum globulin concentrations have 
been observed following a long course (weeks) of antibiotics 
in young animals, possibly due to reduced antigenic stimula-
tion arising from inhibition of both bacterial pathogens and 
the normal bacterial flora.150 The meaning of a reduced serum 
protein concentration must be determined after considering 
an individual’s hydration status. As with decreased red cell 
mass, reduced serum protein concentrations may be masked 
by dehydration.
12.3.3.3.2  Metabolic Markers – Glucose
Serum glucose concentration reflects the dynamic equilib-
rium among intestinal absorption, hepatic synthesis, and tis-
sue uptake and utilization or storage of glucose. The balance 
between hepatic synthesis and tissue utilization is influenced 
by a plethora of signaling molecules, including many hormones 
(e.g., insulin, glucagon, glucocorticoids, growth hormone) and 
catecholamines. In simplified terms, insulin promotes and 
growth hormone suppresses tissue uptake of glucose, glucagon 
and glucocorticoids stimulate hepatic gluconeogenesis (i.e., 
glucose synthesis from amino acids and other acids), and cat-
echolamines and glucagon stimulate glycogenolysis (i.e., the 
breakdown of glycogen, a molecule stored in the cytoplasm of 
hepatocytes and muscle cells as an easily mobilized, transient 
glucose reserve).
Increased serum glucose concentrations (termed hyper-
glycemia in the clinical setting) arise most frequently as an 
incidental consequence of animal or sample handling. Several 
normal physiological processes may be responsible for high 
serum glucose levels. Excitement and fear boost circulating cat-
echolamine concentrations, while stress (e.g., including mori-
bund states) augment blood catecholamine and glucocorticoid

levels. Failure to collect blood samples from fasted animals 
may yield increased serum glucose levels since fed individu-
als maintain serum glucose by metabolizing stored glycogen. 
Any long delay in separating serum from blood cells after 
the blood sample is collected leads to a decline in the serum 
glucose concentration of approximately 7–10 mg/dL/hr. This 
in vitro phenomenon may confound interpretation of serum 
glucose levels if the sampling order during a toxicity study is 
performed in group order (i.e., starting with all controls and 
leaving all high-dose animals until the end). Practical solu-
tions to this issue are to randomize the blood sampling order 
across all groups and to separate the serum from the blood clot 
promptly (within 30–60 minutes). Less often, increased serum 
glucose concentrations observed during toxicity studies may 
result from clinical disease (e.g., diabetes mellitus, pancreati-
tis), steroid therapy, or test article administration.
Decreased serum glucose concentrations (or hypoglycemia) 
during toxicity studies are induced frequently by test articles that 
diminish food consumption and/or limit body weight gain. In such 
cases, the difference in serum glucose concentrations between 
control and test article–treated animals is modest, usually about 
10–15 mg/dL.150 This serum glucose decrease commonly is paired 
with small reductions in circulating RBC numbers and serum 
protein concentration; collectively, changes in these parameters 
indicate a “failure to thrive.” Decreased serum glucose levels also 
occur in many clinical conditions including severe hepatic disease 
(leading to reduced glucose synthesis), intestinal disease (less-
ened glucose absorption), endotoxemia, and certain tumors (e.g., 
insulinomas). Test articles may act pharmacologically to lower 
serum glucose levels directly (e.g., insulin analogs) or indirectly 
(e.g., thiazolidinediones [“glitazones”], which act to improve cell 
sensitivity to insulin). Repeated serum glucose monitoring and 
supplemental glucose in the diet may be needed in non-rodent 
toxicity studies to avoid severe glucose declines produced by 
some glucose-lowering test articles.
12.3.3.3.3  Metabolic Markers – Lipids
Cholesterol and triglycerides are the primary serum lipids 
measured in toxicity studies.46,346 Cholesterol is a major sub-
strate for cell membranes, bile acids, corticosteroids, and 
some sex steroids, while triglycerides are a key cellular energy 
source. Cholesterol and triglycerides are amassed by hepatic 
synthesis and intestinal absorption. Serum cholesterol and tri-
glycerides are not soluble in water, so they circulate in blood as 
constituents of chylomicrons (comprised of absorbed dietary 
triglycerides) or high-density (HDL), low-density (LDL), and 
very-low-density (VLDL) lipoproteins (consisting of choles-
terol and some triglyceride). The triglyceride content deter-
mines the lipoprotein density; for instance, loss of triglyceride 
transforms VLDL to LDL. Triglycerides transported in chy-
lomicrons and VLDLs are metabolized to free fatty acids and 
monoglycerides by lipoprotein lipase anchored to the surface 
of endothelial cells, particularly in capillaries of adipose tissue 
and skeletal muscle. Subsequently, adipocytes re-esterify fatty 
acids to triglycerides for storage in fat droplets, while myo-
cytes oxidize free fatty acids to fuel cell processes. The pre-
dominant lipoprotein for transporting cholesterol varies with 
the species. Most cholesterol in humans (about two-thirds) is 
carried by LDLs, while in laboratory animals most is borne 
by HDL. Inter-species differences in lipid metabolism pose 
challenges when attempting to extrapolate lipid effects seen in 
animal models to predict potential effects in humans.22
Small shifts in serum cholesterol and triglyceride concentra-
tions (increases and decreases) are relatively frequent findings in 
toxicity studies. Mechanisms responsible for such changes are 
seldom identified. Possible contributing factors include altered 
food consumption, digestion, and/or intestinal absorption; energy 
balance; genetic determinants related to body composition; liver 
function; and hormone balance. These findings are thought to 
reflect minor variations in lipid metabolism, and as such they 
generally are interpreted as having no adverse impact on health.
In other situations, serum cholesterol and triglyceride lev-
els may be associated reliably with specific physiological pro-
cesses. For example, following consumption of a fatty meal, 
the serum triglyceride concentration is increased while the 
serum cholesterol concentration is fairly stable. During fat 
resorption to meet systemic energy needs, serum triglycer-
ide concentration rises to a moderate or marked degree while 
the serum cholesterol concentration varies. Increased serum 
cholesterol and triglyceride concentrations may be present in 
some clinical diseases, including diabetes mellitus and hypo-
thyroidism. In most species, serum triglyceride is more pro-
nounced in diabetes mellitus while serum cholesterol is higher 
in hypothyroidism. Serum cholesterol usually is increased 
during cholestasis as the liver is the major route for choles-
terol excretion. However, serum cholesterol may be reduced 
in diseases featuring substantial loss of hepatic parenchyma as 
hepatocytes are the major site for cholesterol synthesis. Subtle 
test article–related effects on lipids are more likely to be iden-
tified in short-term rat toxicity studies because young adult 
rats (7–20 weeks of age) typically have less inter-individual 
variation in serum cholesterol and triglyceride concentrations 
relative to dogs and NHPs. Serum cholesterol and triglyceride 
concentrations in older rats vary considerably due to a number 
of naturally occurring diseases.
12.3.3.4  Electrolytes and Minerals
Electrolytes and minerals are required to provide the neces-
sary intracellular and extracellular microenvironments to 
support optimal cell functions. These elements are essential 
determinants of such processes as bone formation, coagula-
tion, muscle cell contractility, neurotransmission, and pres-
ervation of fluid osmolality (i.e., the number of particles in 
a fluid). In routine toxicity studies, the electrolytes sodium 
(Na+), potassium (K+), and chloride (Cl–) as well as the mineral 
ions calcium (Ca2+) and phosphorus are the most commonly 
measured analytes.171,203
12.3.3.4.1  Serum Electrolytes
Sodium is the primary extracellular cation in plasma and inter-
stitial fluid, where it serves as the major regulator of extra-
cellular fluid volume. Potassium is the principal intracellular 
cation, fulfilling a vital role in cell excitation for muscle con-
traction and neurotransmission. Chloride is the main extracel-
lular anion in plasma and interstitial fluid, where it supports 
fluid homeostasis and balances cation movements across 
membranes. Reference intervals for electrolyte levels in labo-
ratory animal colonies are fairly narrow relative to the range of 
values seen in heterogeneous animal (and human) populations.

637
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
Serum sodium and chloride concentrations tend to shift in 
parallel to each other in conditions involving altered fluid bal-
ance. For example, serum sodium and chloride concentrations 
exhibit approximately proportional decreases when fluid is lost 
during altered function of the adrenal gland (e.g., mineralo-
corticoid insufficiency), gastrointestinal tract (e.g., diarrhea 
and vomiting or diarrhea), and kidney (e.g., renal failure). Test 
articles that enhance urine flow (especially loop diuretics) also 
may yield a proportional decline in serum sodium and chloride 
levels. Decreased serum chloride concentration without an 
effect on serum sodium may occur due to vomiting as stomach 
secretions are rich in chloride. Genuine (i.e., biologically sig-
nificant) increases or decreases in serum sodium concentration 
alone are rare in toxicity studies.
Serum chloride concentrations are disproportionately affected 
in disorders affecting acid-base balance. A reciprocal decline in 
serum chloride concentration occurs during metabolic alkalosis 
to balance accumulation of bicarbonate anion (HCO3
–) in body 
fluids. On occasion, increased serum chloride may result from 
metabolic acidosis associated with diarrhea. In this condition, 
chloride reabsorption in renal tubules is enhanced due to the 
decreased availability of bicarbonate.
Serum potassium concentrations are impacted under many con-
ditions. Increased serum potassium occurs commonly during aci-
dosis because efforts to restore physiologic pH involve exchange 
of extracellular hydrogen ions (H+) for intracellular potassium 
ions. Infrequently, increased serum potassium is produced by cell 
disintegration (e.g., thrombocytosis [which consumes potassium-
rich platelets] and severe tissue necrosis) or oliguric renal fail-
ure. A spurious increase in serum potassium may be observed 
in hemolyzed blood samples (due to traumatic sampling or test 
article effects) in species with potassium-rich RBCs, such as 
NHPs. Decreased serum potassium concentrations (occurring 
in combination with altered sodium and chloride levels) may be 
measured in conditions leading to substantial gastrointestinal- or 
renal-based fluid loss. Decreased serum potassium also may be 
encountered during persistent vomiting as loss of hydrogen ions 
in gastric secretions requires an exchange of intracellular hydro-
gen ions for extracellular potassium ions in order to sustain gastric 
acidity. Serum potassium is sensitive to dietary potassium intake, 
so reduced food consumption is likely to yield a decreased serum 
potassium concentration.
12.3.3.4.2  Serum Minerals
Serum calcium and inorganic phosphorus concentrations are 
defined by a tightly controlled equilibrium among intestinal 
uptake, bone metabolism (formation and resorption), and renal 
excretion. These minerals are regulated by several hormones 
including calcitonin, parathyroid hormone (PTH), and vitamin 
D3 (a steroid hormone).171 Serum phosphorus concentration 
is more sensitive than calcium to intestinal intake and renal 
excretion. Calcium in serum possesses many key attributes, the 
most important of which are its ionization state (approximately 
50% exists in the biologically active, ionized form [Ca2+]) and 
location (about 40% is bound to albumin while the remainder 
is complexed with anions like phosphate and citrate).150
Increased serum calcium concentration is uncommon in tox-
icity studies unless the test article specifically modulates bone 
metabolism. Examples of marketed bone-modifying agents 
include anabolic steroids (which stimulate bone formation), 
hormone analogs with PTH- or vitamin D3-like activity, and 
osteoblast inhibitors (which impede bone resorption). Elevated 
serum calcium concentrations may be observed in many clini-
cal conditions (e.g., osteoporosis, primary and secondary hyper-
parathyroidism, metastatic neoplasia, renal disease); in toxicity 
studies, these conditions are most likely to be encountered when 
using animal disease models or in aged animals (e.g., rodents in 
lifetime carcinogenicity bioassays). Mildly increased serum cal-
cium concentration may be seen occasionally when the serum 
albumin concentration is elevated. This phenomenon is a physi-
ologically appropriate, non-adverse finding related to extensive 
binding of calcium to albumin.
Decreased serum calcium concentration of mild degree is 
a frequent occurrence in toxicity studies. This modest decline 
is associated with decreased serum albumin concentration. 
No signs of toxicity related to low calcium levels (e.g., neu-
rological and neuromuscular abnormalities) are present since 
the serum level of biologically active ionized calcium is rela-
tively unaffected by reduced albumin levels. Rare causes of 
decreased serum calcium concentration in toxicity studies 
(usually seen during chronic or lifetime assays) include acute 
pancreatitis, hypoparathyroidism, nutritional hyperparathy-
roidism, and renal disease.
Increased serum phosphorus concentrations are common 
in young, rapidly growing animals, but these levels decrease 
progressively as the individual matures. Increased serum 
phosphorus often occurs in renal disease, where it typically 
presents together with increased serum levels of urea nitrogen 
and creatinine. Rarely, other clinical conditions may produce 
an increased serum phosphorus concentration including hypo-
parathyroidism, nutritional hyperparathyroidism due to excess 
dietary phosphorus, and hypervitaminosis D. Decreased 
serum phosphorus concentration in toxicity studies usually is 
related to lower food consumption.
12.3.3.5  Other Clinical Chemistry Assays
Other clinical chemistry tests may be used infrequently in 
toxicity studies. Key assays are to evaluate test article–related 
toxicity in endocrine organs and muscle (cardiac and skeletal). 
Data regarding such endpoints are usually obtained to provide 
further context for clinical or other pathology changes and/or 
to address questions posed by regulatory agencies. Many other 
serum analytes may be tested, including blood gases, comple-
ment factors, trace minerals, and transport proteins,174,196 but 
these parameters are seldom if ever assessed during toxicity 
studies and so are not reviewed here.
12.3.3.5.1  Endocrine Markers
Clinical chemistry analysis for endocrine toxicity involves 
quantification of serum hormone concentrations.141 The most 
commonly measured analytes in animal studies evaluate mol-
ecules made by the male (testosterone, follicle-stimulating 
hormone [FSH], luteinizing hormone [LH]) and female (estro-
gen, progesterone) gonads; adrenal gland (corticosterone [in 
mice, rats, and rabbits] and cortisol [in rabbits, dogs, NHPs, 
and humans]); thyroid gland (triiodothyronine [T3], thyrox-
ine [T4], thyroid-stimulating hormone [TSH]); endocrine 
pancreas (insulin and glucagon); pituitary gland (adreno-
corticotropic hormone [ACTH]), and ventral hypothalamus

638
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
(corticotropin-releasing hormone [CRH], thyrotropin-releas-
ing hormone [TRH]). Serum concentrations vary consider-
ably depending on numerous factors including species, sex, 
age, organ physiology (e.g., circadian rhythms and pattern of 
secretion), feeding status, and study duration. For this reason, 
serum hormone measurements typically are acquired to inves-
tigate specific hypotheses rather than as a routine screening 
exercise.
In general, several functionally linked hormones (e.g., T3, T4 
and TSH) rather than individual hormones should be assessed, 
and samples should be collected at several time points to mini-
mize inter-individual variability related to diurnal cycles of 
pulsatile hormone secretion. Hormone levels, especially for 
reproductive hormones, need to be interpreted in the context 
of other pathology data including organ weights (of endocrine 
organs and/or hormone-responsive tissues) and microscopic 
findings (for endocrine organs and hormone-responsive tis-
sues).10,51,320 Altered serum hormone concentrations often 
occur as indirect effects related to other conditions. Examples 
include increased TSH secretion leading to thyroid hyperpla-
sia and neoplasia in rodents due to faster clearance of serum 
T3 and T4 concentrations associated with test article–related 
induction of hepatic microsomal enzymes170,366 and adre-
nal gland inhibition related to administration of exogenous 
steroids. Care must be taken in translating animal-derived 
hormone data for human risk assessment. For instance, thy-
roid hyperplasia and neoplasia related to hepatic microsomal 
enzyme induction occurs in rodents only due to the shorter 
serum half-lives of thyroid hormones in this species (e.g., 
<24 hours in rats vs. 5–9 days in humans for T4).305
12.3.3.5.2  Myocardial Markers
Clinical chemistry analysis for cardiac muscle toxicity has 
transitioned to analysis of serum cardiac troponin (cTn) con-
centrations as a sensitive and exquisitely specific marker of 
acute myocardial injury.304 Troponin is a complex of globu-
lar proteins, with distinct functions; cTnC binds calcium, cTnI 
inhibits the interaction of actin and myosin, and cTnT binds 
tropomyosin. Increased serum cTn concentrations (mainly 
cTnI) are proportional to the degree of myocardial damage.239 
Restraint of NHPs has been shown to induce a small transient 
increase in serum cTnI concentration.302 Blood samples for cTn 
analysis should not be drawn by intracardiac puncture, and 
sampling should be performed antemortem or very rapidly 
after death since cessation of blood flow leads to rapid release 
of cTn from ischemic cells. The utility of commercial cTn 
assays for a particular species should be confirmed in advance 
since these antibody-based detection kits are not equally effec-
tive in all animal species.13
Incorporation of serum cTn measurements in toxicity stud-
ies will necessitate adjustments to the standard study designs 
for conventional toxicity studies. Serum cTn concentrations 
increase rapidly after myocardial injury due to mobilization of 
the labile pool of cytoplasmic cTn (which represents approxi-
mately 5% of the available protein in myocytes). Serum cTn 
levels typically return to baseline values within 24–48 hours 
in common laboratory animal species. Therefore, sample col-
lection for cTn analysis should be performed at multiple time 
points; a single cTn determination at the time of the terminal 
necropsy is insufficient.305 No single sampling schedule will be 
suitable for all study designs. A rational approach might be to 
collect at least one blood sample shortly after (e.g., 2–8 hours) 
the time of initial test article administration to identify any 
acute toxicity, a second during the middle of the dosing phase 
to evaluate the impact of repeated test article exposure, and a 
third sample at study termination in an attempt to match serum 
cTn concentrations with any microscopic evidence of myocar-
dial damage.
12.3.3.5.3  Skeletal Muscle Markers
Clinical chemistry analysis for skeletal muscle toxicity tradi-
tionally has relied on evaluation of serum creatine kinase (CK) 
activity. In toxicity studies, CK activity usually is measured 
without regard to distinguishing the major isoenzymes: CK-1 
(or CK-BB, primarily expressed in brain); CK-2 (CK-MB) and 
CK-3 (CKMM), found in cardiac and skeletal muscle; and 
CK-Mt (localized in mitochondria of many tissues). Increased 
serum CK activity in toxicity studies in all species indicates 
muscle injury (necrosis or degeneration, predominantly of 
skeletal muscle), and its short half-life (2–4 hours) means that 
this analyte will only be detectable shortly after test article 
administration. Consequently, clinical chemistry data sugges-
tive of muscle injury requires a “weight-of-evidence” approach 
that integrates serum ALT, AST, and CK activities in making 
a determination.146 Study-related procedures (e.g., intramus-
cular injections, surgical procedures, and poor venipuncture 
technique) may induce marked elevations in CK that preclude 
identification of meaningful differences between control and 
treated animals.
12.3.4  Urinalysis
Urinalysis is commonly included in the design of GLP-
compliant toxicity studies even though the cost-to-benefit 
ratio is low. Sample collection and processing is difficult, and 
relatively few patterns specific for a particular target organ or 
specific test article class will be identified using this battery of 
techniques. Specialized methods used to obtain high-quality 
samples (e.g., catheterization, cystocentesis, freshly voided 
urine, metabolic cages) generally are challenging to employ 
for large-scale animal studies. Urine collection during tox-
icity studies can be undertaken by cystocentesis at necropsy 
(though volume measurements vary greatly) or by “free catch” 
collection using a metabolic cage for a timed period prior to 
necropsy. Urine should be captured in deep, sterile containers 
and maintained on ice (to prevent evaporation- and microbial-
related changes to physicochemical properties).39 Samples 
should be acquired from non-fasted animals as fasting prior 
to urine collection alters the urine composition, including pH.
The urinalysis battery for routine toxicity studies incor-
porates a spectrum of assessments. Physical properties are 
evaluated by simple visual inspection (e.g., color, clarity) or 
quantitative analysis (e.g., osmolality or specific gravity, and 
where feasible volume determination during a specified time 
period). Biochemical composition is assessed using test strips 
with multiple reagents to permit qualitative or semi-quantita-
tive examination of pH, protein, glucose, ketones, bilirubin, 
urobilinogen, and blood. Microscopic analysis of urinary

639
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
sediment may be performed to detect cells (sloughed urinary 
epithelium, RBCs, WBCs), tubular casts, crystals, bacteria, 
and other formed elements; this portion of the urinalysis is the 
most laborious, expensive, slowest, and least informative and 
generally should be reserved for specific circumstances where 
effects are anticipated. In general, conditions that increase the 
numbers of cells and/or casts in urine sediment will be better 
characterized by histopathologic evaluation of the kidneys and 
urinary bladder.150
12.3.4.1  Urine Concentration and Volume
Measures of urine concentration (osmolality specific grav-
ity) and timed urine volume (e.g., 12–24 hours) are utilized to 
assess urine-concentrating ability. These assays are perhaps 
the most helpful of urinalysis parameters in evaluating renal 
function as loss of urine-concentrating ability usually precedes 
the onset of azotemia (i.e., increased serum concentrations of 
urea nitrogen and creatinine) in chronic kidney disease. Urine 
concentration usually varies inversely with the urine volume.
Urine concentration in toxicity studies commonly is mea-
sured as specific gravity rather than osmolality. Specific 
gravity provides a less accurate estimate of urine solute 
concentration, but the methods to measure specific gravity 
(quantitative refractometry and semi-quantitative reagent 
strips) are less laborious and expensive than the procedure 
for osmolality (usually freezing-point depression osmom-
etry). Reagent strip readings are less accurate, so specific 
gravity analysis in toxicity studies generally should be per-
formed by refractometry.
Decreased urine specific gravity or osmolality with 
increased urine volume are hallmarks of reduced urine-
concentrating ability resulting from advanced renal disease. 
Normal renal function is confirmed by specific gravity val-
ues ranging from 1.015 to 1.045 depending on the species.322 
Increased urine specific gravity (generally 1.030 or higher) 
confirms the capacity to concentrate urine, while reduced 
specific gravity to a fixed range of 1.008–1.012 (termed isos-
thenuria) or below (<1.008) is indicative of no urine-concen-
trating ability. The combination of isosthenuria and azotemia 
generally reflects the presence of primary renal dysfunction.
Test articles may impact urine concentration and volume 
without causing renal toxicity. For example, diuretics pro-
mote increased water consumption, which leads to produc-
tion of increased volumes of dilute urine, but in most cases, 
serum urea nitrogen and creatinine concentrations are unaf-
fected. High urine specific gravity in test article–treated 
animals, especially rodents, may be an indirect consequence 
of reduced food and water consumption rather than direct 
renal toxicity.
Husbandry conditions may impact urine concentration and 
volume values. Dehydration often results in very high urine 
specific gravity values since normal kidneys act to conserve 
body water by excreting more solutes in lower urine volumes. 
Faulty watering systems in metabolic cages may generate arti-
factually low specific gravity values and high urine volumes if 
spilled water is able to contaminate the urine sample. Samples 
with specific gravity values of 1.001–1.003 should be consid-
ered suspect for drinking water contamination.
12.3.4.2  Reagent Strip Tests
Reagent strips provide semi-quantitative biochemical screens 
for urine composition. Test article–related effects identified 
using a reagent strip may be confirmed by a more quantitative 
assay if warranted. Strips may be evaluated manually or using 
automated instruments. Abnormally dark urine (e.g., highly 
concentrated or containing pigment molecules [e.g., hemo-
globin, myoglobin]) may interfere with automated analysis as 
reagent strip readers employ colorimetric (grayscale) standards 
to assess urine composition. Common multi-reagent strips 
used in toxicity studies have squares to measure urine pH, 
protein, glucose, ketones, bilirubin, urobilinogen, and blood. 
Other parameters may be used to examine additional analytes 
but are not used for routine screening studies. Reagent strips 
for urine specific gravity are insufficiently accurate and should 
not be utilized in toxicity testing.
Urine pH is affected by many factors. In toxicity studies, the 
most common factors are diet, test article chemistry, and sam-
ple handling. Animals consuming high-protein, meat-based 
diets tend to have more acidic urine relative to animals that 
eat plant-based diets. Acidic or alkaline test articles can alter 
urine pH if large quantities are administered and then excreted 
in urine. A frequent reason for artifactually elevated urine pH 
in toxicity studies is poor collection technique during over-
night sampling in open containers. In this setting, contamina-
tion by urease-producing bacteria (if present) often results in 
conversion of urea to ammonia, and carbon dioxide in urine 
will be lost. Urine pH generally is not a good indicator of sys-
temic acid-base balance.
Urine protein in most animals typically is low, especially 
young animals enrolled in routine toxicity studies. Increased 
urine protein concentration (termed proteinuria) usually 
reflects renal disease (glomerular injury, defective tubular 
reabsorption, hemorrhage, and/or inflammation) or contami-
nation of voided samples by secretions from the lower urogen-
ital tract. Urine sediment examination (see below) may help 
identify the source of protein. Marked proteinuria is common 
in CPN, a spontaneous background disease of old (especially 
male) rats.154 Increased urine protein concentrations may be 
observed as a false-positive artifact when using reagent strips 
to evaluate urine samples that are highly alkaline or contami-
nated with quaternary ammonium disinfectants. If warranted, 
urine protein can be quantified using standard automated clin-
ical chemistry analyzers.
Urine glucose should be absent in healthy animals as 
any glucose filtered through glomeruli will be resorbed by 
sodium-dependent active transport proteins in the renal proxi-
mal tubules.217 Glucose will be present in the urine if the 
serum glucose concentration (e.g., >180 mg/dL for the dog) 
is increased markedly because the active glucose transporters 
required for resorption will be unable to retrieve all filtered 
glucose. Urine also will contain glucose if test article–related 
renal toxicity results in damage to renal proximal tubular epi-
thelium. Anti-hyperglycemic therapies to treat type 2 diabetes 
mellitus by specifically targeting tubular reabsorption of glu-
cose (e.g., sodium-glucose cotransporter-2 [SGLT2] inhibitors) 
result in substantial glucosuria (i.e., increased urine glucose); 
these agents also will induce osmotic diuresis (i.e., increased

640
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
urine volume resulting from excess unreabsorbed solutes) that 
will indirectly increase excretion of other urine components 
as well (especially sodium ions, which are needed for proper 
function of glucose transporters in proximal tubular epithe-
lium).341 If urine glucose measurements are an important end-
point in toxicity studies, microbial contamination of samples 
must be avoided to prevent false-negative results arising from 
bacterial consumption of glucose.
Urine ketones normally are absent in urine of most animals. 
Minimal (“trace” to “1+”) urine ketone values may be observed 
occasionally in fasted NHPs and male rats. True ketonuria (i.e., 
increased ketone amounts in urine), which indicates that sys-
temic energy metabolism has shifted from glucose production 
to incomplete fatty acid oxidation, may be detected in anorectic 
animals or following prolonged fasting. Poor handling of sam-
ples may result in false-negative ketone values due to bacterial 
degradation and/or loss of volatile ketones from open containers.
Urine bilirubin typically is absent in urine of most animals. 
A small amount (“trace” to “1+”) is not uncommon in the urine 
of male dogs. Increased urine bilirubin concentration results 
from the same conditions that lead to increased serum biliru-
bin concentration, and the rise in urine bilirubin may precede 
the change in serum. False-negative urine bilirubin values may 
occur if samples are exposed to light for prolonged periods due 
to oxidation of bilirubin to biliverdin.
Urobilinogen is normally present in animal urine. 
Theoretically, urine urobilinogen affirms the patency of the 
bile duct as urobilinogen is made in the intestine by bacterial 
conversion of conjugated bilirubin. A negative urine urobilino-
gen value is interpreted clinically to suggest that the bile duct 
is obstructed, but this condition is very rare in toxicity studies; 
concurrent increases in serum total and conjugated bilirubin 
concentrations would be expected. In general, this test has little 
value in toxicity studies and is determined because the reagent 
square exists on the same strip as other common urine tests.
Blood is occasionally present in small quantities in the urine 
of healthy animals in laboratory animal studies. The origin 
of the blood usually is undetermined and varies by the ani-
mal sex and across laboratories but can reach up to 33% inci-
dence.18 Estrus is a common cause in female dogs and NHPs. 
The reagent strip tests do not differentiate reliably among 
erythrocytes (RBCs) and intracellular pigments released from 
damaged RBCs (i.e., hemoglobin) or muscle cells (i.e., myo-
globin). A urine sediment examination may help distinguish 
between blood (i.e.., hematuria) and filtered pigments (i.e., 
hemoglobinuria or myoglobinuria). Ancillary clinical pathol-
ogy tests to explore hematologic and coagulation parameters 
as well as histopathologic evaluation of blood-forming organs, 
major muscle groups, and the kidney also may be helpful in 
determining the source of blood in the urine.
12.3.4.3  Urine Sediment Evaluation
Microscopic examination of urine sediment is used to evalu-
ate formed elements such as cells, casts, and crystals. If a 
sediment examination is included in the study design, sam-
ples should be obtained at necropsy rather than by timed 
(overnight) collection as cells, casts, and crystals tend to dis-
integrate in sitting urine.
Cells (epithelial cells, erythrocytes, leukocytes) are found 
in small numbers in normal voided urine samples. Increased 
cell numbers may or may not be abnormal, with the deci-
sion regarding their meaning often depending on findings 
observed during gross and microscopic evaluation of the kid-
ney and urinary bladder. In general, increased numbers of 
small epithelial cells (i.e., renal tubular epithelium) often are 
indicative of kidney disease, especially if they are accom-
panied by cellular and/or granular casts. Increased numbers 
of large epithelial cells (i.e., squamous and/or transitional 
epithelium) generally do not indicate a significant abnormal-
ity. Unusual epithelial cells may be indicative of urogenital 
tract neoplasia, but this finding is very rare in conventional 
toxicity studies. Leukocytes are uncommon in animal urine. 
Increased numbers of neutrophils (the most common leuko-
cytes in urine) usually are indicative of an infection, particu-
larly if they are accompanied by large numbers of bacteria. 
Animal urine collected during toxicity studies commonly 
contains small numbers of bacteria. This finding coupled 
with few or no leukocytes indicates contamination of the 
sample, which is common due to the routine use of voided 
urine samples in large-scale studies. Antibiotic test articles 
may lead to a measurable decrease in the number of bacteria 
in urine relative to concurrent control animals.
Casts are elongated (cylindrical) strips formed by amalga-
mations of protein alone or protein and cells. Casts represent 
a “mold” of a segment of renal tubule. Such casts are infre-
quently seen in the urine of normal animals. Casts are clas-
sified based on their appearance as hyaline, cellular, waxy, or 
broad. Hyaline casts are made of protein alone. An occasional 
hyaline cast is normal, but increased numbers of hyaline casts 
suggest that the urine protein concentration is elevated, usually 
as a consequence of glomerular dysfunction. Cellular casts are 
formed of agglomerated epithelial cells, erythrocytes, and/or 
leukocytes. Cellular casts are rare, and when present usually 
indicate damage to renal tubules. Granular casts and later waxy 
casts arise from cellular casts, and their presence implies that 
urine stasis (i.e., decreased urine flow) had lasted for so long 
that the entrapped cells had time to degenerate. Broad casts 
are wider than other classes of casts as they formed in collect-
ing ducts or abnormally dilated renal tubules. Broad casts are 
indicative of intrarenal urine stasis. The origin of individual 
cells in casts often cannot be defined, but higher numbers of 
casts (termed “cylindruria” due to the elongate shape of casts) 
show that renal tubules were injured.
Crystals are common in the animal urine. The types of 
crystals depend on urine pH and the test article.84 Acidic 
urine usually is associated with urate and oxalate crystals. 
Alkaline urine typically yields triple phosphate, amorphous 
phosphate, calcium carbonate, and ammonium urate crys-
tals. Test article–specific crystals may form occasionally 
when a test article or its metabolite is highly concentrated 
in the urine. These crystals may be pathologically signifi-
cant if they damage renal tubular epithelium, obstruct renal 
tubules, or lead to the formation of kidney or urinary blad-
der calculi. Test article–related changes in urine composition 
that predispose to urine crystal formation have been demon-
strated to be a rodent-specific mechanism of urinary bladder 
carcinogenesis.76

641
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
12.3.4.4  Quantitative Urine Chemistry Tests
Several quantitative urine chemistry tests have been proposed 
as more suitable assays for identifying and characterizing 
early deficits in renal function. Their use is suggested because 
routine renal function tests (e.g., serum urea nitrogen, serum 
creatinine, standard urinalysis endpoints) are relatively insen-
sitive markers of renal injury. These quantitative tests have 
been valuable during investigational studies when deployed 
to address specific mechanistic questions related to the earli-
est toxic insults to kidneys.150 For example, these tests may be 
deployed for acute screening studies to accelerate lead candi-
date selection.
12.3.4.4.1  Urine Enzyme Activity
Urine activities of GGT, alpha-glutathione-S-transferase 
(αGST), N-acetyl-β-D-glucosaminidase (NAG), and other 
enzymes have been shown to be potentially useful markers 
of acute kidney toxicity.39,123 In toxicity studies, GGT, αGST, 
and NAG are likely markers because they are expressed at 
different sites in the kidney – GGT in the brush border and 
αGST in the cytosol of proximal tubular epithelium, and 
NAG in lysosomes of proximal tubular epithelium and, to a 
lesser extent, other parts of the nephron – and are fairly stable 
in urine held at room temperature (RT). At present, only GGT 
and NAG have been qualified as urine biomarkers for regula-
tory-type toxicity studies.39 Other urine enzymes assessed as 
potential indicators of nephrotoxicity include ALP (located 
in the brush border) as well as ALT, AST, and AST (all found 
primarily in the cytosol). Urine enzyme activities must be 
corrected for variations in urine concentration; common cal-
culations include the total enzyme activity per unit time (e.g., 
IU/16 hours) or the ratio of urine enzyme activity to urine 
creatinine concentration.150 Urine enzyme activities have less 
utility as markers for chronic renal toxicity (e.g., multi-week, 
repeat-dose studies) and do not provide information regarding 
renal function (i.e., glomerular filtration).
12.3.4.4.2  Urine Protein
Urine protein concentration may be quantified to evaluate 
the severity of protein-losing nephropathies. Test articles that 
induce renal toxicity leading to protein loss in the urine often 
have correlated microscopic lesions in the glomeruli (leading 
to higher protein filtration), renal tubules (yielding reduced 
protein resorption), or both. Urine protein concentrations 
should be corrected for urine concentration by calculating the 
total amount excreted per unit time (e.g., mg/16 hours) or the 
ratio of the urine protein to urine creatinine concentrations. 
Glomerular lesions tend to produce more elevated urine pro-
tein concentrations relative to tubular damage, but urine pro-
tein alone cannot identify the exact mechanism of protein loss. 
Glomerular vs. tubular lesions may be explored by measuring 
concentrations of serum proteins that are freely filtered through 
glomeruli and then completely resorbed in proximal tubules 
(e.g., β2-microglobulin). Sodium dodecyl sulfate–polyacryl-
amide gel electrophoresis (SDS–PAGE) has been suggested as 
a technique for discriminating glomerular from tubular injury 
by separating urine proteins by molecular weight.189 By this 
method, glomerular damage would be indicated by an increase 
in urine of high-molecular-weight proteins (e.g., larger in size 
than the 69 kDa limit above which serum proteins are not fil-
tered by normal glomeruli) while tubular damage would be 
demonstrated by an increase in low-molecular-weight proteins 
(e.g., 12–60 kDa).
Several other urine proteins have been shown to have util-
ity as biomarkers of acute renal damage.39,142,232,288,344 These 
markers tend to be functionally and structurally conserved 
across species, and some are sufficiently confined in terms of 
their renal expression to serve as markers for injury to particu-
lar portions of the nephron. Albumin (~66.5 kDa in humans) 
and cystatin C (~13.3 kDa in humans) enter the urine passively 
by glomerular filtration and are reabsorbed completely in the 
proximal tubules unless epithelial injury impedes their uptake. 
Kidney injury molecule-1 (KIM-1) is induced by damage to 
proximal tubule epithelium. Damage to the distal nephron is 
indicated by increased urine concentrations of osteopontin 
(an inducible protein) or renal papillary antigen-1 (RPA-1, a 
constitutively expressed protein). Clusterin and neutrophil 
gelatinase-associated lipocalin (NGAL, or lipocalin-2) are 
inducible proteins that are upregulated throughout the nephron 
following exposure to renal toxicants. Inducible markers (e.g., 
clusterin, KIM-1, and NGAL concentrations) may rise in urine 
slightly later than constitutive biomarkers (e.g., GGT and NAG 
activities and RPA-1 concentration). Of the novel biomarkers, 
clusterin and NGAL appear to perform well in rats, dogs, pigs, 
and primates, while KIM-1 and osteopontin currently have 
been shown to be effective in rats and NHPs.137,268,345
12.3.4.4.3  Urine Electrolytes
Urine electrolyte concentrations (sodium, potassium, and 
chloride) are measured commonly in timed urine collec-
tions because these assays are listed in regulatory guidance 
documents for some countries.148 However, urine electrolytes 
when interpreted as absolute values provide little information 
not already found in urine concentration measurements (i.e., 
osmolality or specific gravity). Additional information may 
be obtained by calculating electrolyte excretion per unit time 
(e.g., mmol/16 hours) or the fractional excretion (i.e., clear-
ance, or the amount of electrolyte excreted relative to the total 
quantity filtered by the glomeruli).
Effects on urine electrolytes are influenced chiefly by 
homeostatic mechanisms designed to maintain systemic elec-
trolyte and fluid balances. Therefore, these urine analytes are 
not specific and sensitive measures of renal function but rather 
reflect indirect effects produced by altered intake (e.g., normal 
vs. reduced food and/or water consumption) or loss (e.g., typi-
cal gastrointestinal emissions vs. diarrhea and/or vomiting) of 
electrolytes and fluids. Considerable inter-individual variabil-
ity may make interpretation of urine electrolyte concentrations 
challenging.
Increased electrolyte excretion in urine may be induced by 
diuretics or renal toxicants that damage renal tubular epithe-
lial cells that normally resorb electrolytes and water. The tim-
ing of diuretic administration and urine collection may have 
a substantial impact on urine electrolyte measurements. For 
instance, administration of diuretic test articles in the morn-
ing will lead to high urine electrolyte levels for several hours 
during the middle of the day, but these changes will be missed

642
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
if the urine collection is performed overnight beginning in the 
evening. In general, other serum chemistry and urinalysis tests 
as well as microscopic evaluation of the kidney are more cost-
effective and sensitive means for identifying sites and potential 
mechanisms of renal toxicity.
12.3.5  QC for Clinical Pathology Endpoints
Toxicologic pathology analyses must minimize known sources 
of variation to ensure that the resulting data are accurate. 
Many factors affect data quality, and these may exert their 
impact during sample collection and processing (i.e., the pre-
analytical phase) or when the sample is being evaluated (i.e., 
the analytical phase). Collective experience over decades has 
identified many beneficial practices to limit the impact of bio-
logical and experimental variation when generating and inter-
preting clinical pathology data.
12.3.5.1  Pre-Analytical Variation
Many factors may impact the variability of clinical pathol-
ogy data, even under the relatively controlled conditions of a 
properly designed and conducted toxicity study. Pre-analytical 
sources of variation (Table 12.7) are numerous, but many are 
amenable to control by sufficient attention to detail in collect-
ing, analyzing, and storing blood and urine samples.15 That 
said, some degree of intrinsic variability is inevitable given 
current designs for routine toxicity studies.
12.3.5.1.1  Physiological Sources
Strain/breed differences are key factors to consider when 
evaluating clinical pathology data, especially in rodent stud-
ies. Differences in hematology values tend to be the most 
pronounced.150 Sprague-Dawley rats have higher leukocyte 
counts than do F344 rats, but F344 rats are predisposed to 
develop large granular lymphocytic (LGL) leukemia.218,283 
Erythrocyte (red blood cell [RBC]) size in cynomolgus mon-
keys (Macaca fascicularis) varies with the geographic origin, 
with animals from mainland sources (China and Vietnam) 
having fewer but larger RBCs compared to animals from 
islands (Indonesia, Mauritius, and the Philippines).274,298 The 
difference is so profound that the expected ranges of normal 
biological variability (i.e., reference intervals) for erythrocytic 
parameters like the RBC count and MCV may not overlap for 
animals from these two populations, suggesting that interpre-
tation of altered erythrocytic indices might be compromised 
if cynomolgus monkeys from different geographic regions are 
mingled in the same toxicity study or used for different studies 
of a single test article. Toxicity studies to test targeted bio-
molecules may require pre-study expression analysis to ensure 
that animals enrolled in the study will respond properly to the 
test article. For example, safety testing of alemtuzumab is not 
feasible in many NHPs because widespread expression of its 
molecular target (CD52) on RBCs would cause massive acute 
hemolysis leading to death, but toxicity studies can be under-
taken with NHPs that are shown to be CD52-negative.342 Other 
hematological and clinical chemistry differences in different 
cynomolgus monkey strains have been described, illustrating 
the general principle that care is needed to ensure that in the 
toxicology program for a given test article that the individuals 
from a given species should share a common background.79
Age differences in conventional toxicity studies may impact 
clinical pathology endpoints over the course of the assay. 
Many studies are initiated using immature animals (e.g., 
6- to 8-week-old rats, 4- to 6-month-old Beagle dogs, 2- to 
3-year-old cynomolgus monkeys) that are still growing rapidly.

Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
Values for many clinical pathology analytes evolve over time. 
Depending on the species and strain/breed, common trends in 
most species include increasing erythrocyte maturity (seen as 
higher RBC numbers, Hct, and Hgb in conjunction with lower 
Retic counts and MCVs), immune system maturation (shown 
by higher absolute neutrophil counts and serum globulin [Glob] 
concentrations together with decreased lymphocyte numbers), 
and slowing bone remodeling (indicated by decreased serum 
ALP activity and inorganic phosphorus concentration). The 
existence of subtle age-related differences may prevent recog-
nition of test article–related effects if comparisons are being 
made to pre-study baseline data obtained more than 2 weeks 
earlier.150 Age-related changes also may be observed in mature 
animals, especially aged rodents (12 or more months old) due 
to the presence of comorbid conditions such as chronic degen-
erative, inflammatory, and neoplastic diseases. These differ-
ences underscore the importance of properly matched controls 
to serve as a comparator.
Sex differences in the variability of clinical pathology data 
have been reported besides the expected divergence in circulat-
ing levels of male and female sex hormones. For example, male 
Sprague-Dawley rats have significantly higher erythrocytic 
indices (RBC counts, Hct, Hgb, MCV, mean cell hemoglobin 
[MCH], and red cell distribution width [RDW]), leukocyte 
numbers (total white blood cells [WBC], neutrophils, lym-
phocytes, monocytes), and serum activities of ALT and AST 
relative to females.158 Sex-related variations in hematologic 
analytes have been reported in several NHP species, but in 
general, such effects are subtle.298 Nonetheless, the potential 
for such changes indicates that clinical pathology data should 
be segregated by sex for analysis and interpretation until a suf-
ficient database exists to show that reference intervals for both 
sexes are sufficiently similar enough to permit their consolida-
tion for analysis.
Individual-specific behavioral and psychological states such 
as excitement, fear, and stress can have pronounced effects 
on clinical pathology data.116 In general, excitement/fear and 
stress are overlapping events in toxicity studies. Excitement 
and fear are associated with the acute systemic release of 
catecholamines while stress is mediated by exposure to 
elevated systemic levels of endogenous glucocorticoids. 
Catecholamine-induced effects are immediate but transient 
(usually less than 30 minutes) while glucocorticoid-driven 
effects rise and recede gradually and thus tend to last for a 
longer time. The most obvious changes in excited or frightened 
animals are increased circulating numbers of erythrocytes and 
leukocytes (chiefly lymphocytes in rodents, segmented neutro-
phils in dogs, and both lymphocytes and neutrophils in NHPs) 
as well as increased serum glucose concentration. In chroni-
cally stressed animals, typical hematologic findings include an 
increase in segmented neutrophil counts and often decreased 
lymphocyte and eosinophil counts. These behavioral/psycho-
logical responses may be minimized in animals enrolled in 
toxicity studies by acclimatization for at least 1 week following 
shipping or surgical procedures as well as by training to accept 
routine handling practices (including blood sampling).
Diet affects many clinical pathology parameters, though 
the effects may differ depending on the species. For example, 
high-fat (atherogenic) diets tend to induce markedly increased 
serum cholesterol concentrations in hamsters374 and Watanabe 
rabbits4 (a New Zealand White strain with heritable hyper-
lipidemia). Plant-based diets may be associated with modest 
decreases in serum concentrations of total protein, albumin, 
and urea nitrogen. Meat-based diets are more prone to yield 
false-positive signals for fecal occult blood (used as a marker 
of gastrointestinal hemorrhage). Toxicity studies typically use 
standardized diets from commercial vendors, and in some spe-
cies supplements (e.g., fruit for NHPs) acquired from a single 
source, to reduce subtle food-related effects. Clinical pathol-
ogy data from animals fed purified or unusual diets should 
not be compared to HCD compiled from animals fed standard 
diets.
Fasting prior to blood sampling is widely practiced in rou-
tine toxicity testing as a means for reducing inter-individual 
variability in certain clinical pathology analytes (e.g., serum 
glucose and insulin concentrations).12,210,219 All groups need to 
be treated the same since the most relevant comparison for 
test article–treated animals will be to concurrent controls 
rather than to historical reference intervals. Non-rodents (e.g., 
dogs, NHPs) are routinely fasted overnight prior to blood 
sampling. Rodents may be fasted overnight (up to 16 hours) or 
beginning early in the morning on the day of blood sampling 
(4 hours) depending on the facility’s or sponsor’s preferences. 
The rationale for avoiding routine overnight fasting in rodents 
is that they have higher basal metabolic rates and, as noc-
turnal animals, generally consume about two-thirds of their 
daily food and water intake during the night.168,169,237 Fasted 
animals will have low serum glucose and insulin concentra-
tions relative to non-fasted animals. Rodents fasted overnight 
(16 hours) also will have increased serum corticosterone levels; 
decreased concentrations of cholesterol, triglycerides, thyroid 
hormones (T3 and T4), urea nitrogen, bilirubin, and calcium; 
and decreased serum ALT and ALP activities compared 
to animals fasted for 12 hours or less.169,219 Prolonged fast-
ing in rodents will alter many hematologic parameters since 
reduced water consumption leads rapidly to dehydration and 
hemoconcentration. Care must be taken when scheduling food 
withdrawal during large-scale (i.e., lengthy) rodent necrop-
sies to ensure that the period of fasting is similar in control 
and treated animals. In all species, clinical pathology results 
should be interpreted in the context of food consumption data 
to confirm that test article–related effects in treated animals 
represent toxicity and not a procedural effect associated with 
reduced food consumption. If the test article renders the diet 
unpalatable, treated animals will be “fasted” relative to well-
fed (“non-fasting”) control animals.
Timing of sample collection in toxicity studies should be 
devised to minimize the effect of physiological rhythms.89 For 
example, batch sampling where blood is taken from control 
animals in the morning and high-dose animals in the after-
noon will yield samples with divergent serum concentrations 
of many hormones that exhibit circadian cycling.141 Affected 
clinical pathology analytes may vary depending on the spe-
cies, due in part to differences related to the active phase of 
daily life (i.e., diurnal vs. nocturnal). A similar challenge 
may be encountered in measuring hormone concentrations in 
female animals in the absence of knowledge regarding an indi-
vidual’s current estrus status.320

644
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
12.3.5.1.2  Procedural Sources
The sampling site and method as well as the use of anesthe-
sia for restraint during sampling are perhaps the most fre-
quently reported procedural influences on clinical pathology 
results.150 The effects of such variables have been investigated 
extensively in laboratory species, especially in rats,66,184,218,22
0,236,314,325,326,340 but also in mice,235,301 rabbits,152 dogs218,227,359 
minipigs,324 and NHPs.25,218 Differences exist in many fac-
tors, including blood cell counts (which tend to be increased 
in small-caliber plexuses and vessels relative to large-caliber 
arteries and veins), serum glucose concentration (typically 
higher in the aorta since it has yet to be extracted by tissues), 
and serum hormone concentrations (which are substantially 
higher if blood is drawn from vessels near the organ producing 
the molecule). Anesthesia tends to lessen inter-individual vari-
ability for clinical pathology data from NHPs, especially with 
respect to cell counts and serum electrolyte concentrations. 
Variability may be related to technician proficiency rather than 
the sampling site,220 though with suitable training and expe-
rience interpretable data may be obtained from blood drawn 
from any site. To optimize the clinical pathology analysis, a 
single collection technique and anesthesia protocol should be 
used for concurrent control and treated animals for a given 
study (including pre-study, interim, and terminal time points) 
or series of related studies. Where possible, current clinical 
pathology data should be compared only to historical reference 
intervals created from data acquired using demographically 
matched animals (i.e., the same strain, sex, age, and colony), 
an identical sampling approach and means of anesthesia, and 
comparable assay methods.
Sampling frequency also impacts clinical pathology data. 
Intermittent blood collection is relatively simple in non-rodents 
due to their large size and relatively easy access to multiple 
large veins. The frequency of blood collection in rodents, 
especially mice, is limited by their small body size and lim-
ited blood volume. In rodent toxicity studies, these restrictions 
may be avoided by collecting blood as a terminal procedure; in 
this setting, greater blood volumes may be obtained from the 
heart (e.g., cardiocentesis) or large-caliber blood vessels (e.g., 
abdominal aorta, vena cava). In general, frequent blood sam-
pling during toxicity studies is most common for TK profiling, 
in which case multiple samples are taken shortly after dosing 
on the first day of the study and often again near the necropsy 
date. Blood sampling to assess clinical pathology endpoints 
often is performed only at interim and terminal necropsies, 
often with the addition of one or two pre-study samples in non-
rodents (usually collected between 2 and 14 days prior to the 
first test article dose) to establish baseline values.
The order of sample collection and analysis heavily influ-
ences the clinical pathology data set. Timing of sample col-
lection and processing in toxicity studies should be scheduled 
to avoid systematic temporal differences among groups. Key 
time-related events that will impact clinical pathology results 
include batch collections (e.g., drawing blood from controls 
in the morning and high-dose animals in the afternoon) and 
delays in separating serum from clotted blood or analyzing 
urine. These sources of variability may be minimized by ran-
domizing the time at which blood is drawn and using a work-
flow that enables blood collection and sample processing in as 
short a time as possible. An alternative approach to true ran-
domization is a structured (round-robin or stratified) pattern 
of blood collection such that one animal per group is bled in 
succession. Samples should be processed and analyzed in the 
order in which they were collected; rearranging the sequence 
so that all samples from a given group are analyzed in order 
may yield statistically significant false-positive findings as a 
result of analytical drift (e.g., a shift in detector sensitivity over 
time) rather than as a consequence of test article exposure. For 
large-scale studies where samples will be collected over mul-
tiple days, day-to-day variability may be reduced by collecting 
and analyzing samples from all males on 1 day followed by all 
females on the next. On rare occasions, randomization of blood 
sampling may not be feasible, and all animals in a group will 
be sampled before moving to the next group. In such instances, 
the high-dose group should be processed immediately before 
or after the control group (e.g., mid dose, control, high dose, 
low dose) rather than sampling all groups in consecutive order 
(i.e., control, low dose, mid dose, high dose). Waiting until 
all samples have been collected before processing may lead 
to in vitro artifacts that confound interpretation, especially if 
control samples are taken more than 1 hour prior to high-dose 
samples. This delay may yield significant differences between 
control and high-dose group mean values (e.g., the high-dose 
animals will have a higher serum glucose concentration, 
decreased serum potassium and inorganic phosphorus con-
centrations, and diminished serum AST and LDH activities) 
that might be misinterpreted as test article–induced effects.150
12.3.5.1.3  Artifactual Sources
Sample quality may be impacted by collection and processing 
techniques, thereby producing artifactual differences into the 
clinical pathology data set. The two main issues in routine tox-
icity studies are unintended coagulation or hemolysis.
Unwanted coagulation is a problem in samples taken to 
assess hematology or coagulation endpoints. Coagulation (for-
mation of either a clot or fibrin mesh) in a hematology sample 
usually arises from traumatic collection technique (leading 
to activation of the clotting cascade in the needle and/or col-
lection syringe), inadequate mixing of the blood and antico-
agulant, or addition of insufficient blood volume to a standard 
blood tube. Coagulation in hematology samples always leads 
to an artificial decrease in circulating platelet numbers and 
often also produces lower erythrocyte and leukocyte counts. 
An inappropriately small blood volume will yield an excess of 
anticoagulant, the presence of which will impact cell counts 
(due to dilution errors) and volumes, particularly for erythro-
cytes. The choice of anticoagulant may alter several clinical 
pathology analytes. For example, the use of potassium EDTA 
will lead to very high potassium concentrations, very low cal-
cium and magnesium concentrations (due to chelation), and 
very low activities of enzymes that use magnesium as a cofac-
tor (e.g., ALP and CK).80 Excess citrate in samples destined for 
coagulation assays may prolong coagulation times.
Hemolysis may be induced by test articles, but the more 
common cause in routine toxicity studies is traumatic tech-
nique for sample collection, transport, and/or serum/plasma 
separation. Hemolysis produces spuriously increased or 
decreased assay results by two main mechanisms: release of

645
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
erythrocyte intracellular constituents and interference with 
test methodology.80,128,204,241 Alterations that may be found 
when serum from hemolyzed samples is analyzed include 
higher serum AST and LDH activities, increased serum con-
centrations of albumin and electrolytes (sodium, potassium, 
and chloride), and decreases in serum creatinine concentra-
tion and serum ALP activity.
Improper storage of clinical pathology samples may alter 
several analytes. The main storage errors include excessively 
long retention prior to analysis and inappropriate storage tem-
perature. Most analytes are relatively stable if samples are pro-
cessed in a timely manner (ideally within hours on the same 
day, or early the next day).255,334 Hematology samples should 
be retained on ice or placed in the refrigerator (4°C) and ide-
ally processed within 1–4 hours of collection. Serum or plasma 
for which clinical chemistry analysis cannot be completed 
the same day should be either refrigerated for processing the 
next day or frozen (–20°C) for up to 2 weeks depending on 
the analyte of interest, the test species, and the laboratory. If 
coagulation tests cannot be performed on the day of collection, 
plasma samples should be frozen at –20°C and thawed only 
once before analysis. If an extended delay will occur before 
analysis, samples for clinical chemistry (serum or plasma) or 
coagulation (plasma) assays may be frozen at –70°C for up to 
6 months for most standard analytes, although some analytes 
of interest may degrade during this prolonged period of stor-
age. Samples from all animals for a given study time point 
should be handled the same way, and ideally should be evalu-
ated on the same day using the same instrument.
Other sources of variation may impact clinical pathology 
data. However, in toxicity studies, their impact on data inter-
pretation may be minimized by the inclusion of appropriate 
sex- and age-matched concurrent control animals that have 
experienced the same husbandry conditions and experimental 
procedures (except for administration of the test article). One 
often overlooked source of variation is procedures that only 
have relevance when assessed in test article–exposed animals 
(e.g., multiple blood samples to investigate pharmacokinetic 
and/or pharmacodynamic endpoints). To avoid spurious differ-
ences among groups that might confound data interpretation, 
the controls should undergo the same procedures performed 
on treated animals (e.g., repeated blood sampling, sham sur-
geries).74,233,308 The length of time needed to recover from 
repeated sampling depends on the volume of blood collected 
and may be several weeks long if a large quantity is withdrawn 
over a short period of time.233
12.3.5.2  Analytical Variation
Variation among the performance of instruments and proce-
dures also may impact the quality of clinical pathology data. 
Analytical sources of variation are addressed by sufficient 
attention to detail in verifying the accuracy and precision of 
routinely performed assays and newly developed methods. 
Accuracy reflects the degree to which the measured quantity 
of an analyte approaches the true amount, while precision 
reflects the extent to which replicate assays for an analyte from 
a given sample yield an equivalent answer (i.e., reproducibil-
ity). Accuracy and precision are independent qualities; ideally, 
assays are both accurate and precise, but in practice, assays 
may be accurate but imprecise or inaccurate but precise. For 
clinical pathology endpoints, accuracy is determined in a 
manner pre-defined by the contents of commercial assay kits 
and instrument software and is confirmed by means of regu-
lar proficiency testing.293,363 Precision of an assay typically is 
assessed using two complementary approaches: within-run 
precision is measured by performing 20 or more consecutive 
analyses of the same sample, while between-run precision is 
evaluated by analyzing the same sample on several consecu-
tive days. Accuracy and precision must be monitored for every 
assay and every analytical instrument. In most laboratories, 
samples employed for this purpose are commercially available 
reference standards prepared by external proficiency testing 
programs.
In routine toxicity testing, precision is more important 
than accuracy since interpretation of clinical pathology data 
relies on comparison of data from treated animals with com-
parable data from concurrent control animals. Imprecise 
tests, regardless of their accuracy, are less capable of detect-
ing small differences between test article–treated and control 
groups, particularly in non-rodent studies where group sizes 
are small. For example, if the true group mean serum glucose 
concentrations of four treated dogs and four control dogs are 
115 and 100 mg/dL, respectively, the difference between the 
means may be interpreted to be genuine only if the assay pre-
cision is high (i.e., the standard deviation is small because the 
measured values are very similar). Modern automated hema-
tology and clinical chemistry analyzers have high within-
run and between-run precision (indicated by coefficients of 
variation [CVs] of 1%–4% for most assays, and <10% for all 
assays),155,173,339 which is sufficient analytical reproducibility to 
ensure acquisition of a meaningful clinical pathology data set 
for routine toxicity studies.
Clinical pathology laboratories in toxicity testing facilities 
often use instruments optimized for accuracy when utilized 
to analyze human rather than animal samples. It is likely that 
evaluation of animal samples on human-optimized equipment 
will lead to small differences in measured values between 
animal and human samples.150 Hence many clinical pathology 
assays as used in toxicity studies could be thought of as “rela-
tive quantitative” assays. The direction and frequency of these 
minimal differences is consistent (i.e., the value for a given 
assay will be slightly above or slightly below the true value 
[“systematic bias”]), and experience has shown that the high 
precision afforded by using reagent kits, reference standards, 
and calibrators designed for use with human samples is capa-
ble of providing acceptable clinical pathology data from toxic-
ity studies in animals. On occasions, animal species-specific 
reagents may need to be used. For example, measurement of 
albumin in rabbits requires the use of a rabbit-specific calibra-
tor. Hematology analyzers that count and differentiate blood 
cells must be validated specifically to work with samples from 
laboratory animal species because of differences between ani-
mal and human cell morphology and size.
A well-devised QC system is essential for clinical pathol-
ogy laboratories that process samples from toxicity studies. 
Details regarding QC systems for clinical pathology laborato-
ries are outside the scope of this chapter, but this information

646
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
may be found in major reference texts and “best practice” 
papers.14,112,175,284,357 The bare-minimum QC system for a clini-
cal pathology laboratory should cover factors necessary to 
maintaining reliable instrument operation, assay performance, 
and technician proficiency (Table 12.8).
12.4  Anatomic Pathology Endpoints
Samples destined for anatomic pathology may be obtained 
antemortem, generally for diagnostic purposes to identify 
the disease affecting an individual patient. In this context, 
the samples are biopsies collected either using a minimally 
invasive method (e.g., needle biopsy to obtain a thin col-
umn of tissue) or a more invasive procedure (e.g., excisional 
biopsy to remove part or all of a lesion). In principle, such 
antemortem techniques may be performed in research ani-
mals, although in this setting such approaches typically are 
utilized for discovery or early development studies (e.g., effi-
cacy studies with animal models of disease). In contrast, the 
usual approach to the anatomic pathology evaluation during 
animal toxicity studies is to undertake a comprehensive nec-
ropsy (i.e., an animal autopsy) on all subjects. The reason for 
this approach is that a necropsy offers the greatest ability to 
evaluate the potential effects of test articles on major organs 
and systems.
The main objectives of the anatomic pathology examina-
tion are (1) to identify and evaluate all macroscopic (gross) 
lesions and (2) to collect all tissues listed in the study protocol 
as well as all gross lesions. Subsequently, tissues are trimmed 
(sliced in a consistent manner from animal to animal) and pro-
cessed to prepare tissue sections for microscopic evaluation. 
The pathologist then reviews all tissues, generates diagnoses 
for structural abnormalities, and interprets their meaning in a 
pathology report. The rest of this section presents a sequential 
description of the functions that are involved in the anatomic 
pathology evaluation.
12.4.1  Necropsy
Necropsy involves the examination of a body after death. In 
routine toxicity studies, the necropsy often provides the ini-
tial evidence of test article–related effects, including toxicity 
and carcinogenicity in major target organs as well as cause of 
death (COD). In addition, pre-existing disease and/or proce-
dural factors that might complicate study interpretation may 
be evident at necropsy.
The necropsy often is one of the shortest phases of a tox-
icity study. Nonetheless, this phase is critical since such an 
examination can only be performed once. Any tissue that is 
not evaluated and/or collected at this stage will be lost forever. 
An improper or incomplete necropsy may lead to permanent 
gaps in the pathology data set. Therefore, the necropsy must be 
performed by well-trained and experienced prosectors (nec-
ropsy technicians) working under the supervision of a quali-
fied and experienced pathologist.264
12.4.1.1  General Considerations
The necropsy involves a sequence of related tasks, all of which 
are necessary to provide a complete picture of a test article’s 
impact on the living organism. For routine toxicity studies, 
necropsy personnel should be trained in dissection procedures 
as well as the anatomy of the test species. Detailed, well-
illustrated guidelines describing necropsy procedures and 
preferred descriptive terminology for laboratory animals have 
been published134,186,188,222 and should be reviewed as necessary 
prior to and even during the necropsy session.
