Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
Improper or inconsistent necropsy practices may compli-
cate interpretation of pathology data. All elements of the nec-
ropsy may contribute to interpretive difficulties, but one factor 
of special importance is the necropsy order. In general, the 
necropsy order should be rotated (i.e., “round robin,” where 
one animal per group is processed in the same order until all 
animals have been necropsied) or randomized.264 These meth-
ods prevent systematic variation in tissue quality (or “block” 
effects303) that often occur if all control animals are necropsied 
together and all high-dose animals are necropsied as a group. 
Necropsies should not be performed on all animals in a group 
before moving to the next (e.g., all controls first, all high-dose 
individuals last) because this block arrangement will maxi-
mize the likelihood of artifactual differences related to the 
experimental design (e.g., different levels of glycogen accumu-
lation in hepatocytes depending on the length of time food was 
withheld prior to necropsy [Figure 12.2]) being mistaken for 
test article–related effects.
As with other aspects of a toxicity study, the necropsy must 
be planned in detail and needed materials must be organized 
in advance. The necropsy team must have access to the fol-
lowing documents both well before the date of the necropsy as 
well as during the necropsy.
•	 Study protocol (or plan), including amendments
•	 SOPs for necropsy-related tasks
•	 In-life records for each animal
The study protocol defines the study objectives, required sam-
ples and measurements, and study-specific procedures. The 
protocol takes precedence over SOPs and descriptions of study-
specific procedures in terms of dictating how the necropsy 
should be organized and performed.62 Facility SOPs provide the 
necropsy team with information regarding how common nec-
ropsy tasks should be implemented; in general, the procedures 
in SOPs are applied universally to all toxicity studies in a facil-
ity to ensure consistency and to eliminate variables that might 
complicate interpretation of the pathology data. The in-life 
records allow the prosector and any assistants (e.g., technicians 
who weigh organs and/or record macroscopic observations) to 
identify potential changes that might explain any unusual clini-
cal findings.
The individual animal necropsy record (IANR) is a log that 
provides a real-time chronicle of critical information necessary 
for subsequent data interpretation.264 The IANR includes key 
study- and animal-specific details (e.g., study and animal num-
bers, treatment group and dose, euthanasia date and time and 
method), a list of protocol-specified tissues to be assessed and 
sampled), and lines for recording measurements to be taken at 
necropsy (e.g., total body and organ weights) as well as gross 
findings. Signature and date lines for all necropsy team mem-
bers (technicians and pathologists) typically are placed at the 
bottom of the form. The IANR may be a paper form for man-
ual data entry or an electronic portal accommodating direct 
entry into a data collection system, but in most settings, this 
log is generated electronically rather than by manual recording 
on a paper document.
12.4.1.2  Euthanasia Procedures
Euthanasia at necropsy may be implemented using many 
methods. The best choice depends on the study objectives, 
animal species, and age.19,238,253 The euthanasia method must 
be humane, minimizing distress to the animal, should be 
easy to implement consistently, and should produce few tis-
sue artifacts. Generally, inhaled (e.g., anesthetic gases, carbon 
dioxide) and injectable (e.g., sodium pentobarbital) chemical 
agents are preferable to physical means (e.g., decapitation). 
The method of euthanasia should be reviewed and approved 
by the IACUC and stated in the study protocol.
The euthanasia method may impact many anatomic pathol-
ogy characteristics. Some will be influenced regardless of 
the species. For instance, carbon dioxide asphyxiation may 
produce focal to multifocal, usually minimal, acute alveolar 
FIGURE 12.2  Incidental vacuolation of hepatocytes near the central vein (C) due to glycogen accumulation in an ad libitum-fed, adult, control Beagle 
dog. The clear, colorless cytoplasm of vacuolated cells contrasts clearly with the eosinophilic (pink) cytoplasm of unaffected hepatocytes near the 
portal triad (P).

648
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
hemorrhage in the lung (Figure 12.3). This limited change 
should not be confused with a test article–related effect. 
However, other parameters impacted by the euthanasia method 
may be specific to a particular species. For example, barbitu-
rate solutions produce blood retention in the spleen, thereby 
causing marked splenomegaly (i.e., enlargement) in dogs asso-
ciated with congestion of the red pulp. The importance of rec-
ognizing such known tendencies is that the euthanasia method 
may be selected to decrease the likelihood of inducing cer-
tain procedure-related effects. Alternatively, the presence of 
such effects may be taken into consideration when interpreting 
gross observations, organ weights, and microscopic findings.
12.4.1.3  Gross Examination
In toxicity studies, euthanasia generally is either immedi-
ately preceded or immediately followed by blood and urine 
collection (to permit clinical pathology analysis, as described 
above). Next, the body surface is examined, after which prin-
cipal organs are exposed for macroscopic evaluation and 
then removed (Table 12.3). Selected organs are weighed after 
removal (Table 12.3), and then samples are collected from 
most major organs (Table 12.3). For GLP-compliant studies, 
most organs are processed for microscopic analysis, while a 
few organs may be retained in fixative for potential processing 
at a later time. Other tasks completed at necropsy may include 
collection of ancillary measurements (e.g., sizes or volumes) 
or samples (e.g., to assess test article distribution or evaluate 
biochemical or molecular endpoints indicative of test article 
activity), preparation of blood and bone marrow smears, and 
sometimes photography to document any macroscopic find-
ings that will be lost once the carcass is discarded.
During the necropsy, the prosector must handle tissues care-
fully and deliberately. Dissection trauma (i.e., crushing, punc-
turing, or excessive pressure using instruments or digits) and 
flawed tissue preservation (i.e., delayed fixation or insufficient 
fixative volume) may introduce artifacts that distort micro-
scopic features of cells and tissues, thereby obscuring possible 
test article–related effects. Freshly harvested tissues may be 
cleaned or moistened by gentle immersion or rinsing with iso-
tonic (“normal”) saline. This solution possesses a similar sol-
ute composition and pH as other body fluids and thus prevents 
artifactual tissue swelling and cell rupture, which may occur 
if hypotonic (i.e., tap) water is employed. Irreversible errors in 
sampling may be avoided by collecting protocol-specified tis-
sues in a defined order during the necropsy and then recording 
on the IANR that they have been examined grossly and sam-
pled. At this point, some facilities place specimens directly 
into tissue fixative while others may place them into separate 
chambers of multi-compartment tissue trays so that a final 
check may be performed at the end of the necropsy. To prevent 
tissues from drying out (which induces cellular artifacts that 
may make tissue interpretation difficult), the chambers usually 
are filled with isotonic saline.
The necropsy must be performed immediately after eutha-
nasia and completed rapidly, for two main reasons. First, 
prolonged intervals between euthanasia and necropsy may 
substantially impact organ weights and microscopic features.127 
For instance, liver weights (both absolute and relative to body 
weight) and hepatocyte vacuolation in tissue sections may be 
seen with a delay of only 25 minutes between euthanasia and 
the time of necropsy.206 Second, delayed dissection will increase 
the impact of post mortem degeneration (termed “autolysis”) on 
gross and microscopic structure, including organ color and tis-
sue integrity. Autolysis is produced by the continued activity 
of intracellular enzymes in tissues and microbial flora (espe-
cially in the digestive tract). In general, the necropsy should 
begin within 5 minutes of euthanasia, and the dissection should 
not exceed 20 minutes.127 Thus, each prosector (for rodents and 
small non-rodents) or team of prosectors (for large non-rodents) 
should be assigned to a single animal at a time. Organs that 
are affected rapidly by autolysis – gastrointestinal tract, pan-
creas, and bone marrow first, followed quickly by the adrenal 
gland, eye, kidney, liver, and CNS – should be evaluated and 
sampled as soon as possible; connective tissue-rich organs like 
bone, muscle, and skin may be assessed late in the necropsy as 
FIGURE 12.3  Acute hemorrhage (usually multifocal and minimal) lining or filling scattered alveoli often occurs as an incidental finding in lungs of 
animals that have been euthanized by carbon dioxide inhalation. Adult, control Sprague-Dawley rat.

649
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
they are more resistant to autolysis. If necropsy cannot be per-
formed immediately after death, the carcass should be placed 
in a refrigerator as cold temperatures retard autolysis. However, 
carcasses should never be frozen before necropsy because 
freezing and thawing produce ice crystals within cells and tis-
sues that severely distort normal tissue structures and obscure 
many microscopic changes.
Another primary goal of the necropsy is to identify and 
accurately describe macroscopic lesions. This task is essential 
as the gross examination guides the subsequent tissue trim-
ming and microscopic evaluation needed to find and charac-
terize potential test article–related findings and (for animals 
that expire prior to a scheduled necropsy date) the COD. Gross 
observations should be described succinctly using standard 
terms (Table 12.9) to ensure consistency among prosectors 
both within and across studies. In general, abnormal findings 
seen during the gross examination should be described using 
some or all of the following criteria: location, number, distri-
bution, shape, size, color, consistency, and any special charac-
teristics. A key difference between usual diagnostic pathology 
vs. toxicologic pathology terminology is that nomenclature for 
gross findings in the diagnostic setting often communicates an 
interpretation (e.g., “acute hemorrhage” or “congestion”) while 
the toxicologic pathology approach emphasizes the description 
(e.g., “red discoloration”) and leaves interpretation until the 
gross and microscopic observations have been integrated. A 
major advantage in necropsies for toxicity studies is that the 
gross appearance of abnormal tissues may be compared con-
currently to normal organs from control animals (Figure 12.4).
12.4.1.4  Body and Organ Weight Determinations
Body and organ weights may be measured manually or using 
automated scales that send data automatically to an electronic 
database. Scales should be calibrated before each session, 
and for long necropsies should be recalibrated intermittently 
throughout the necropsy session. To reduce the potential for 
weighing errors, expected weight ranges for each animal and 
organ (matched to the test species, strain, sex, and age) should 
be located next to the scales or be triggered by the electronic 
database. Any weight falling outside the normal range should 
be verified before recording.
Body weights are a rapid and simple means of assessing test 
article–related changes to an animal’s health. Body weight 
within a species is influenced by the strain, sex, age, diet (both 
the caloric content and form), and fasting status. For exam-
ple, extended fasting in mice (overnight [18 hours] or longer) 
results in significantly lower body weights relative to unfasted 
animals. This weight loss stems from both a decreased amount 
of gastrointestinal contents (40% of the loss) and reduced body 
mass (60% of the loss) related to lower heart, kidney, and liver 
weights.169 Test article–induced toxicity leading to reduced 
consumption of food and water is a common cause of reduced 
body weights during routine toxicity studies.
Organ weight changes are sensitive indicators of target 
organ toxicity when collected in a consistent manner dur-
ing repeat-dose toxicity studies of durations from 1 week to 
1 year.309 Recommendations for which organ weights to col-
lect overlap to various degrees among regulatory agencies,

international consortia dedicated to harmonized toxicity test-
ing, and professional societies of toxicologic pathology (Table 
12.3). Test facilities often craft SOPs for weight collection that 
combine these recommendations. Additional organs may be 
added on a case-by-case basis to address specific study objec-
tives. Organ weight determinations are not recommended for 
carcinogenicity studies (both 2-year bioassays in wild-type 
rodents and 6-month assays in engineered mice) as changes 
related to aging and comorbid diseases produce greater inter-
individual variability that confounds interpretation. Similarly, 
organ weights generally should not be collected from animals 
that die or are euthanized for humane reasons prior to a sched-
uled necropsy as such individuals experience multiple factors 
that will influence their organ weights in unpredictable ways 
(e.g., terminal illness, poor nutritional status, lack of exsangui-
nation, absence of appropriate control animals). Organ weights 
are considered to be of limited value in single-dose studies and 
dose escalation studies.309
Significant changes in the weights of some organs may be 
detected in the absence of changes to other pathology parame-
ters.21,264 For example, increased liver weights associated with 
test article–related induction of hepatic drug-metabolizing 
enzymes (chiefly cytochromes P450 in the hepatocyte micro-
somal fraction) is a frequent finding in toxicity studies, and 
liver weight increases of up to 20% relative to controls may 
occur in rats with no or minimal (Figure 12.5) microscopic 
evidence of hepatocellular hypertrophy or changes in serum 
activities of hepatocyte leakage enzymes7; a similar capac-
ity for elevated liver weight in the absence of microscopic 
liver changes or elevated serum enzyme activities has been 
observed in NHPs.6 In like manner, modest dose-dependent 
alterations in kidney weight (likely due to induction of cyto-
chromes P450 in proximal tubular epithelium) in the absence 
of microscopic renal changes are a common effect in toxicity 
studies.144 In general, however, organ weight changes seldom 
serve as the sole indicator of target organ toxicity and typically 
should not be interpreted as adverse in and of themselves.309 
Instead, test articles that substantially impact organ weights 
typically induce correlated alterations in gross and/or micro-
scopic features and often clinical pathology analytes.
Organ weights must be obtained in a standardized man-
ner to be useful. As noted above, the necropsy order should 
be rotated (i.e., round robin) or randomized as organ weights 
may decline during the course of an extended necropsy, 
especially in fasted rodents. Furthermore, no single prosec-
tor should be allowed to disproportionately necropsy animals 
from a given dose group as differences in organ removal and 
trimming techniques may artifactually impact weights.309 For 
example, the olfactory bulbs of rodents represent 6%–7% of 
the brain weight,34 so inconsistent inclusion of these structures 
when weighing the brain will significantly affect the group 
means. Similarly, incomplete removal of adhered fat may sub-
stantially alter the weights of abdominal organs such as the 
adrenal glands, kidneys, and spleen. Organ weights are more 
consistent if animals are exsanguinated prior to collecting 
weights. For some organs, weights may be taken after fixation 
rather than at the time of necropsy as a means of minimizing 
microscopic artifacts associated with handling of fresh tis-
sues309; organs for which this practice often is used include 
brain (to avoid induction of ‘dark neuron’ artifact172) and small 
organs of rodents (e.g., adrenal, pituitary, and thyroid glands). 
When comparing organ weights to HCD, the appraisal should 
be made to organs collected in the same manner (i.e., weights 
collected in fresh tissue at necropsy should be matched specifi-
cally to historical weights for unfixed organs).
Interpretation of organ weight findings is improved with 
concurrent evaluation of absolute organ weights and organ-to-
body and/or organ-to-brain weight ratios for both group mean 
values and individual animal values. Organ-to-body weight 
ratios (using the total body weight collected at necropsy)

Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
control for possible variations related to individual-specific 
physiological responses (e.g., diurnal rhythms, fasting, stress) 
and to understand effects on organ weights as affected by test 
article–related body weight changes. Organ-to-brain weight 
ratios are a useful standard (especially for the adrenal gland 
and ovary) since brain weight remains fairly constant and can 
help correct for body weights that are substantially impacted 
by test article exposure. Moreover, brain weights are rarely 
associated with neurotoxicity or systemic toxicity.21,226,309 
Toxicologic pathologists generally report that organ weight 
determinations are most useful for heart, kidney, liver, and 
testis, noting that weights for reproductive organs are most 
valuable when taken in sexually mature animals.226,309
12.4.1.5  Tissue Fixation
Tissue preservation is an essential portion of the necropsy 
design. Delayed fixation leads to an increasing departure for 
normal cell and tissue features.126 Therefore, cell and tissue 
structures must be stabilized as soon as possible after euthana-
sia to ensure that test article–related changes are not obscured 
by autolysis during the microscopic evaluation. In conven-
tional toxicity studies, chemical agents are the usual means for 
tissue preservation.
Many different fixatives and fixation procedures are avail-
able as no one fixative is ideal for all applications.185,202 The 
choice of fixative and fixation procedure is dictated by the 
study objectives. In routine toxicity studies, the most common 
preservation method is immersion in NBF, an aldehyde-based 
aqueous fixative containing ~4% formaldehyde. Commercial 
NBF formulations are inexpensive, widely available (as solu-
tions containing ~1% methanol as a stabilizer to prevent 
inactivating formaldehyde cross-links), and straightforward 
to discard or recycle. Tissue samples should be trimmed at 
necropsy to a thickness of ~0.5 cm or less (to facilitate rapid 
penetration of NBF into dense organ parenchyma) prior to 
immersion in NBF at a fixative-to-tissue volume ratio of at 
least 10:1 for 24–48 hours. Hollow organs may be flushed 
(e.g., the gastrointestinal tract, nasal cavity, and urinary blad-
der) or inflated (e.g., lungs36,42) with NBF to facilitate fixation. 
For special studies, methanol-free 3.7% formaldehyde (desig-
nated colloquially as paraformaldehyde [PFA] since it is made 
from PFA pellets or powder) may be substituted for NBF to 
improve the stability of fragile antigens for IHC and/or pre-
vent the potential formation of artifactual vacuoles in lipid-
rich tissues (due to the ability of alcohols to extract lipids).31 
Specimens with thick layers of surrounding connective tissue 
(e.g., eye, testis, whole rodent embryos) often are immersed in 
composite fixatives like Bouin’s solution, Davidson’s solution, 
or modified Davidson’s solution which contain both aldehydes 
and additional components (acids and/or alcohols).33,36,201 
Glutaraldehyde (GLUT, 2%–2.5%) often is utilized on tis-
sues that are destined for ultrastructural evaluation since it 
cross-links molecules more effectively than NBF or PFA, thus 
optimizing the preservation of fine cytoarchitectural features; 
PFA (2%) often is added to GLUT to provide fast initial pen-
etration. These aldehyde-based fixatives may be delivered 
by intravascular perfusion (whole-body or head-only) when 
rapid delivery of the fixative solution to deep tissues is neces-
sary.31,86 In general, perfusion is only used for special toxicity 
studies used to explore specific questions in a particular sys-
tem (e.g., neurotoxicity assays).31,34,125 Perfusion is not a good 
option during routine toxicity studies since this method may 
alter the morphology and weights of many non-neural organs 
(e.g., lung, spleen). Aldehyde-based fixatives including NBF 
result in measurable tissue shrinkage that may induce artifacts 
that resemble possible test article–related effects (e.g., retinal 
detachment in the eye), and aldehyde-based fixatives in the 
absence of other rapidly penetrating fixatives (e.g., acids and 
alcohols) tend to yield poor preservation of delicate cells in the 
retina and seminiferous tubules.201,202 Common fixatives must 
be used, stored, and discarded with care as their components 
are toxic, corrosive, and in some cases explosive (e.g., picric 
acid in Bouin’s solution).202
FIGURE 12.5  Increased cell size (termed “hypertrophy”) is a common change in liver after exposure to test articles that induce microsomal drug-
metabolizing enzymes. Compared to an age- and sex-matched vehicle-treated animal (A), repeated test article administration leads to hepatocyte 
enlargement – seen here (B) as an increase in cytoplasm around each nucleus in the centrilobular zone surrounding the central vein (C) but not in the 
periportal region around the portal triad (P). Adult Sprague-Dawley rat exposed to an unspecified small molecule test article daily for 28 days.

652
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
Certain experimental questions are approached using frozen 
tissues rather than fixed tissues. In routine toxicity studies, fro-
zen tissues are collected for relatively few studies and typically 
are limited to one or two carefully selected organs. In this con-
text, frozen tissues may be utilized to evaluate target expres-
sion or test article distribution using IHC or EHC methods. 
In such cases, unfixed tissue blocks are obtained, trimmed, 
covered in medium (e.g., OCT compound), and snap-frozen by 
immersion in liquid nitrogen-cooled isopentane. Tissue should 
not be placed directly into liquid nitrogen since the thermal 
differential between the warm tissue and liquid will form a 
vapor barrier that retards freezing. Frozen samples then are 
wrapped and maintained in freezers at –80°C until the time of 
cryotomy. The frozen sections are applied to slides and then 
fixed by brief immersion in an aldehyde solution (commonly 
PFA, pH 7.4) or a coagulating fixative like acetone or alcohol.
12.4.2  Histology
Histology involves the processing of tissue samples to permit 
microscopic (histopathologic) evaluation. The goal of histo-
logic processing is to minimize the artifactual changes that 
occur in tissue samples following removal and in particular 
to maintain consistent processing conditions across animals 
within and between studies. In routine toxicity studies, most 
organs are processed using common conditions, although in 
some instances organ-specific processing conditions may be 
used to provide better preservation of microscopic features.
12.4.2.1  General Considerations
Like necropsy, histology involves a sequence of related tasks that 
are standardized through the use of SOPs. For routine toxicity 
studies, histotechnologists are trained in tissue trimming, tissue 
processing, tissue embedding, microtomy, and tissue staining 
methods. Detailed methods for routine (e.g., hematoxylin and 
eosin [H&E]) and special histochemical stains, molecular pathol-
ogy (e.g., IHC, ISH) techniques, and tissue-type specific proce-
dures have been published elsewhere,60,327 and interested readers 
are referred to these resources for technical details.
As with the necropsy, improper histology practices may 
amplify artifacts in tissue sections and complicate the genera-
tion and interpretation of histopathology data. Variations in 
tissue trimming187,228,292 as well as batch effects (i.e., process-
ing all control tissues in one staining run and all high-dose 
tissues in another run) during tissue processing and stain-
ing303 are the main deficiencies in histological processing that 
complicate the microscopic evaluation. In particular, samples 
from all treatment groups should be mingled in all processing, 
embedding, and staining batches as tissue artifacts associated 
with instrument failure for one batch are less likely to be mis-
interpreted as test article–related effects.224,332
12.4.2.2  Tissue Trimming
In routine toxicity studies, tissue samples typically are 
trimmed after 24 hours to 7 days in NBF. Trimming may be 
performed by necropsy technicians or histology technicians, 
occasionally with oversight by a pathologist when orienting 
complex samples or working with unusual test articles (e.g., 
medical devices). Technicians should be well acquainted with 
gross anatomy for major test species11,59,77,113,145,165,221,269,270,337 
and intricate organs (e.g., brain70,122,129,256,257,266,267,296,333, eye82). 
Prior to trimming, the technician should have access to the 
study plan with its census of protocol-specified tissues to trim, 
the list of gross findings for each animal, and familiarity with 
published guides187,228,292 or the facility SOP showing appropri-
ate planes for trimming (e.g., longitudinal and/or transverse) 
as well as the specific sites to sample and number of pieces 
to obtain when trimming larger organs. Appropriate safety 
precautions (e.g., protective gloves, working in a fume hood) 
are required to protect personnel from inhalational and topical 
exposure to toxic chemicals in fixative.
Trimming is performed with razor blades on a hard surface 
(commonly hard plastic). Blades should be rotated regularly 
(every few tissues) since fixatives are corrosive; failure to 
switch blades may lead to a dull edge, the continued use of 
which will crush rather than cut tissue. Fixed tissues should 
be trimmed to a maximum thickness of 0.3 cm so that the lids 
of tissue cassettes may be closed without compressing the 
specimens. For rodents, multiple samples may be placed in the 
same cassette as long as their consistency (e.g., soft or firm) is 
similar. If necessary, sample orientation may be maintained by 
placing porous sponges in the cassette to prevent tissues from 
flipping during later stages of processing. Small organs may 
be submitted intact; very small organs may need to be placed 
in mini-inserts to prevent their loss through the slotted sides of 
the cassettes. Masses with visible margins should be trimmed 
where possible so that the lesion and adjacent normal tissue 
both will be evident in the tissue section. Multiple sections 
should be taken for large masses, especially if different parts 
exhibit different appearances, so that microscopic variations 
may be cataloged.
Additional processing may be required for some fixed tis-
sue before trimming is possible. For example, bone is too hard 
to trim until it has been decalcified. Different decalcification 
agents may be employed.327 In general, strong (inorganic) acids 
produce rapid decalcification (1–2 days); mixtures of weak 
(organic) acids and their salts, such as a 1:4 ratio of 8N formic 
acid and 1N sodium formate,191 yield modestly slower demin-
eralization (4–10 days); and chelating agents (e.g., EDTA) 
provide very slow decalcification (several weeks to months). 
However, the speed of decalcification is inversely related to 
the digestion of the remaining tissue. In general, acids are used 
for routine toxicity studies while slower methods (typically the 
acid/salt mixture) may be substituted if microscopic evaluation 
of IHC markers is a known or likely endpoint.
12.4.2.3  Tissue Processing
For routine toxicity studies, trimmed tissues borne in plastic 
tissue cassettes are transferred to automated tissue proces-
sors to replace tissue water with paraffin wax by sequential 
immersion in solutions with increasing concentrations of 
organic solvents. This substitution is needed since paraffin (or 
other embedding media) are required to support tissues dur-
ing microtomy. This stage of histological processing involves 
a series of steps,327 which are reviewed briefly here.

653
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
The steps in tissue processing are dehydration, clearing, 
and finally infiltration. Dehydration is necessary because 
common embedding media – mainly paraffin for routine 
toxicity studies – is not miscible with water present in fixed 
cells and tissues. Dehydration involves transferring fixed tis-
sues through an ascending series of graded alcohols (usually 
ethanol), with tissues remaining in each solution at RT for 
10–20 minutes. For routine toxicity studies, the alcohol con-
centrations commonly start at 70% (in water) and then rise 
to 80%, 95% (repeated twice), and 100% (repeated twice), 
but for some lipid-rich tissues, the lowest ethanol concentra-
tion may be reduced to 50% or even 30%. Next, tissues are 
cleared by moving them from the second 100% alcohol bath 
into the first of two organic solvent baths. Clearing reagents 
(usually toluene or xylene) are miscible with both dehydrant 
(alcohol) and paraffin. The clearing steps are completed at 
RT for 10–20 minutes each. Finally, dehydrated and cleared 
tissues are placed in molten paraffin at around 60°C for 
10–20 minutes so that infiltrating paraffin replaces the clear-
ing reagents. For lipid-rich tissues, incubation times in all 
solutions may be increased to 30–45 minutes to optimize the 
extent of paraffin infiltration. Excessive hardening of pro-
cessed tissues may occur if samples are left too long in the 
clearing solutions or the temperature of the molten paraffin 
baths is more than 5°C above the melting point of paraffin 
(56°C–58°C). Modern tissue processors usually apply a vac-
uum for the entire sequence of steps to increase the efficiency 
of water, clearing reagent, and paraffin movement.
In certain instances, samples may be embedded in media 
other than paraffin. Examples of alternative media include 
“soft plastics” like glycol methacrylate (GMA) and “hard plas-
tic” resins (e.g., araldite, epon, or mixtures thereof). Plastic 
media provide more support for tissues, which allows the 
procurement of thinner tissue sections for optimal assess-
ment of very fine cytoarchitectural details. Particular uses for 
these alternative media in toxicity testing include assessment 
of undecalcified bone, high-resolution analysis of nerve cross 
sections, evaluation of tissues containing metal-containing 
medical devices, and TEM. Further details on these special-
ized techniques have been published elsewhere.35,81,290,368
12.4.2.4  Tissue Embedding
In routine toxicity studies, embedding involves encasing paraf-
fin-infiltrated tissues in solid paraffin. Cassettes holding infil-
trated tissue are transferred from the paraffin bath of the tissue 
processer to a bath of liquid paraffin attached to an embedding 
station. Working with a single cassette at a time, the histology 
technician inspects the size of the tissue(s) and then selects a 
metal mold into which the tissue will fit. The mold is placed 
under a nozzle, which dispenses liquid paraffin until the tissue 
is covered. The technician uses one or more metal probes to 
position the tissue in the appropriate orientation, after which 
the labeled cassette is placed on top of the mold and filled with 
more liquid paraffin. The mold then is shifted to a cold plate 
so that the paraffin congeals. Once the paraffin is solidified, 
the paraffin block with attached cassette is detached from the 
mold. A major point with respect to performing an efficient 
microscopic analysis is that the orientation and position of the 
tissue within the block ideally are consistent for all animals 
for a given study.
In general, all tissues in a multi-tissue block should share a 
similar composition in order to facilitate microtomy. Examples 
of this principle include placing multiple sections from a single 
organ (e.g., coronal sections of rodent brain taken at different 
levels34) or mingling organs with firm consistency (e.g., heart, 
kidney, liver, and spleen for rodents) in the same cassette. If 
many tissues are placed in a single block, care must be taken 
to ensure that a sufficient rim of paraffin remains between the 
tissue margins and wall of the mold to support the tissue dur-
ing microtomy.
12.4.2.5  Microtomy
For routine toxicity studies, paraffin blocks with embedded 
tissues are placed on a microtome equipped with a metal 
blade. The block is “faced” (i.e., shaved until all embedded 
tissues have a fully exposed flat surface), after which one or 
more sections are cut. In general, paraffin sections in toxic-
ity studies are cut with thicknesses of 4–6 μm (microns). The 
margins of sequential (i.e., serial) sections adhere to each 
other so that several passes of the microtome yield a long rib-
bon. The technician uses forceps to transfer the ribbon to the 
surface of a warm water bath. The heat and surface tension 
allow the sections to spread, thereby smoothing any folds 
or wrinkles that developed when the block was cut. A pre-
labeled glass slide is positioned under the ribbon and raised to 
allow one or more floating sections to adhere to the surface. 
The slides are then moved aside to dry. For some purposes 
(e.g., IHC), slides may be coated in advance with a matrix 
(e.g., albumin, poly-L-lysine) to promote stronger bonding of 
the section to the slide.
12.4.2.6  Tissue Staining
Modern histology laboratories perform complex staining pro-
cedures using automated stainers. Staining of paraffin sec-
tions represents the reverse of the original tissue processing 
steps needed to remove water and permit paraffin infiltrations. 
Slide-mounted paraffin sections are treated with an organic 
solvent (e.g., xylene) to remove the paraffin wax, passed 
through a descending series of graded alcohols, and then thor-
oughly rinsed with water to rehydrate the tissue. The sections 
are now ready for treatment with aqueous staining solutions.
Numerous stains (i.e., histochemical dyes) may be applied 
to tissue sections to demonstrate general cell and tissue char-
acteristics (e.g., H&E), structural features (e.g., periodic acid–
Schiff [PAS] to reveal basement membranes and glycogen, 
Masson’s trichrome to highlight collagen), and pathogens (e.g., 
Gram stain for bacteria, PAS for certain fungi and protozoa). In 
contrast to histochemical stains, IHC and ISH methods actu-
ally lead to labeling of structural elements through binding of 
a primary reagent (an antibody for IHC, nucleic acid for ISH). 
A complete listing of histochemical and molecular techniques 
is outside the scope of this chapter, but uses and protocols for 
many stains and molecular methods may be found in standard 
histotechnology references.45,47,67,167,200,278,327,343,364 However, a 
brief description of H&E staining will be given here since this

654
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
method is the mainstay for microscopic evaluation during rou-
tine toxicity studies.
For H&E staining, rehydrated sections undergo a series of steps 
that ultimately results in exposure to both dyes. Hematoxylin is 
applied first. Hematoxylin (a “basophilic” stain) comes in sev-
eral formulations that combine a dye (oxidized hematoxylin) 
and mordant (i.e., binding agent, often an aluminum salt). This 
reagent has a strong affinity for nucleic acids, thereby imparting 
a blue color to cell nuclei. Next, slides are rinsed in water and 
then immersed in a weakly alkaline “bluing” agent (e.g., dilute 
aqueous ammonium hydroxide) that makes the dye–nucleus 
interaction permanent, imparting a medium to dark blue color 
to cell nuclei. A differentiation (de-staining) step is performed 
using a weak acid alcohol next to remove nonspecific back-
ground staining, thereby improving contrast. The sections are 
then passed through the bluing reagent again and rinsed thor-
oughly. Next, eosin (an “acidophilic” stain) is applied as the 
counterstain (i.e., second stain). Eosin is produced in various 
aqueous or alcoholic formulations, all of which impart a pink 
to red color to nonnuclear cell and tissue components. The fully 
stained section is now dehydrated by passage through a series 
of graded alcohols and then cleared by several rinses in xylene. 
For routine toxicity studies, the stained section is then covered 
by a thin glass coverslip anchored by a thin layer of permanent 
(resin-based) mounting medium or a resin-based tape strip. The 
advantage of glass coverslips is that they are less likely to be 
scratched, while the advantage of coverslipping tape is that no 
sticky mounting medium is needed.
12.4.3  Microscopic Evaluation
Microscopic (or histopathologic) evaluation is the study of 
morphological changes in tissues at the light microscopic 
level.64 The microscopic evaluation is a key portion of the 
toxicologic pathology assessment as histopathologic findings 
frequently inform the determination of an NOAEL in toxic-
ity studies. The STP has promulgated recommended practices 
for toxicologic histopathology64 describing appropriate tech-
niques for maximizing diagnostic accuracy and sensitivity 
while minimizing observer bias.
12.4.3.1  General Considerations
The microscopic evaluation must accurately identify find-
ings, discriminate among various causes (e.g., incidental 
background changes vs. procedure-related changes vs. test 
article–induced changes), and clearly and concisely communi-
cate their implications to other pathologists as well as toxicolo-
gists, regulators, and sometimes other parties. Histopathology 
data sets generated for toxicity studies emphasize diagnoses 
applied across many subjects using a set of standard terms that 
lend themselves to tabular summarization. This approach con-
trasts markedly with the usual practice in diagnostic pathology 
practice where morphological changes are described at length 
for a single animal.
Microscopic evaluation is as much art as science, and pro-
ficiency in this role is influenced by both training and experi-
ence. In this respect, histopathologic diagnosis is intrinsically 
subjective to some degree (i.e., framed by the pathologist’s 
education and experience), but it is not inherently biased (i.e., 
performed to favor a particular conclusion). Instead, histo-
pathologic diagnosis in toxicologic pathology (and in fact in 
all pathology settings) is an iterative process in which initial 
findings are refined when necessary by further re-assessment 
to establish final diagnoses. This step-wise process provides 
the most challenging aspect of microscopic evaluation for 
toxicity studies in that diagnostic consistency may shift over 
time for a given pathologist within a single study and also 
among pathologists across many studies. International efforts 
to establish standard terms for common findings have substan-
tially increased diagnostic uniformity in recent years,143,323 but 
even, so the nomenclature employed by different pathologists 
is seldom identical.64 Nonetheless, qualified and experienced 
toxicologic pathologists commonly are capable of identifying 
the same test article–related findings and using them to set an 
NOAEL.127
In most circumstances, the best (most accurate and sensi-
tive) histopathology data set is produced when all tissues from 
a toxicity study are evaluated (“read”) by a single patholo-
gist.64 On occasion, two or more pathologists may be assigned 
to the same project, such as for very large studies or critical 
studies with extremely short timelines. In such instances, the 
pathologists are assigned particular subsets of animals or tis-
sues (e.g., one assesses all males while the other evaluates all 
females, or one reads all neural tissues and the other examines 
all non-neural tissues).
12.4.3.2  Procedures for Microscopic Evaluation
The design and conduct of a microscopic evaluation depend on 
many factors. Some of these are determined in the study proto-
col (e.g., use of an informed [non-blinded] vs. coded [blinded] 
analytical approach), while others are defined by the patholo-
gist (e.g., diagnostic nomenclature and thresholds, grading 
criteria). This section briefly reviews some of the major con-
siderations that influence the histopathologic evaluation for 
routine toxicity studies.
12.4.3.2.1  Design of Analysis
In routine toxicity studies, the evaluation typically is con-
fined to conventional bright-field (i.e., transmitted white light) 
microscopy of H&E-stained sections. Such sections are suit-
able for describing key cell and tissue changes that represent 
an alteration from the expected appearance of normal organs 
in test species.
In many instances, such changes reflect incidental back-
ground findings (i.e., spontaneous variations included in the 
range of “within normal limits” (WNL) or “no visible lesions” 
(NVL) for a given structure) and/or may be linked to a particu-
lar molecular or cellular mechanism of toxicity. If warranted, 
additional sections of some tissues may be processed using 
special procedures (usually IHC rather than ISH methods) to 
demonstrate cell type- or tissue-specific features (Table 12.10). 
Such auxiliary methods are used to provide information 
regarding a test article–related tissue response that might not 
be evident in H&E-stained sections (e.g., IHC techniques to 
highlight reactive glial cells associated with injury to nearby 
neurons) and/or to improve recognition of cell changes

655
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
indicative of a particular cellular effect (e.g., IHC techniques 
to detect apoptotic or dividing cells). Special procedures usu-
ally are included in the study protocol (if the procedure is to 
be applied systematically to a particular tissue from selected or 
all treatment groups) or may be performed on a specific slide 
at the discretion of the study pathologist to better define the 
nature of a tissue change.
The choice of microscope objectives appropriate to the his-
topathologic analysis is made by the study pathologist. In rou-
tine toxicity studies, the microscopic evaluation is performed 
using low-magnification objectives (usually 4× or 5× but occa-
sionally 2× or less, where “×” indicates the fold magnification 
of the lens). This “low power” assessment provides an efficient 
and rapid screen for visible tissue changes. Where unusual cell-
level changes are observed, a tissue may be evaluated using 
high-magnification objectives (10×, 20×, or occasionally 40×) 
to better examine cytoarchitectural details. This “high-power” 
assessment is especially helpful if counts of altered cells are 
needed to characterize a finding. An example of this approach 
is estimating proliferation in a tumor by determining the 
number of mitotic figures per high-power field (hpf, typically 
calculated by counting the number of affected cells at 5–10 
sites when viewed through a 40× objective). The choice of 
microscope objectives appropriate to the microscopic analysis

12.4.3.2.2  Quality Control
An essential element of the microscopic evaluation is QC. This 
task generally is performed by the study pathologist during 
the course of the microscopic evaluation to detect any tissue 
changes. Section quality is determined by a series of factors, 
the most important of which are mentioned here.
The first question is whether or not a protocol-specified tis-
sue is actually present on the slide. This issue generally is a 
consideration for small organs (e.g., endocrine glands, nerves). 
In many cases, such small tissues are embedded in fat, and 
the sections may consist mostly or entirely of this surrounding 
tissue. The absence of an organ in a block may require acquisi-
tion of additional sections or retrieving the fixed (“wet”) tis-
sues from the archive in a second attempt to find and trim the 
correct organ for histological processing.
The second question is cell preservation. Adequate fixation 
(in terms of both quick initiation and acceptable duration) is 
indicated by crisp cell and tissue features. Delayed fixation 
may lead to recognizable autolysis (post mortem tissue degen-
eration due to the actions of endogenous cell and/or micro-
bial nucleases and proteases). If autolytic changes are mild, 
the study pathologist typically can continue the evaluation. 
However, severe autolysis (Figure 12.6) may preclude the eval-
uation of some or all of a tissue section.
The third question is section quality. This factor addresses 
both section integrity and staining consistency. Unlike the rel-
atively pristine images depicted in published papers and study 
reports, histological preparations for routine toxicity studies 
often exhibit minor flaws in the section (e.g., folds, fragmenta-
tion, or tears), staining (e.g., small decolorized areas), or slide 
(e.g., air bubbles or dust superimposed over the tissue section). 
In general, the study pathologist will be able to evaluate the 
section regardless of such minor defects. If the tissue is too 
distorted or obscured, a new section may be produced or the 
coverslip may be removed and reapplied.
The final question is the section size. Sections must be of 
sufficient size to permit analysis of the organ parenchyma. 
Tangential sections of organs may exhibit little tissue for eval-
uation. For example, hastily trimmed sternum samples from 
rodents may yield sections with bone but little bone marrow, 
thereby preventing a credible assessment of the latter tissue. 
This fault generally is addressed by taking more sections to 
expose the missing tissue.
12.4.3.2.3  Order of Analysis
The order of microscopic evaluation typically varies with the 
study pathologist’s preference. On occasion, a certain order is 
specified in the study protocol to address a particular objec-
tive. Details regarding the order of analysis do not need to be 
communicated in the study protocol or pathology report.
For some studies, especially those with small group sizes, 
all tissues from all control animals, or all animals of a given 
sex, may be evaluated to establish the spectrum of expected 
background findings (i.e., within normal limits [WNL]). 
Thereafter, tissues from high-dose animals (and intermediate-
dose animals, as required by the study protocol) are examined. 
Alternatively, the microscopic evaluation may proceed by the 
alternating examination of animals (or subsets of animals) 
from the control and high-dose groups. This latter approach is 
common for studies with very large group sizes.
For most toxicity studies, the microscopic evaluation will 
proceed by evaluating all tissues from each animal before 
moving to the next animal. This approach is used since it per-
mits a more comprehensive assessment of global health. Less 
frequently, a single tissue (e.g., liver) is evaluated from all ani-
mals across all groups before moving to the next tissue. The 
latter organ-by-organ approach affords a more focused exami-
nation of a particular organ’s structure and may provide more 
consistent severity grading when evaluating subtle findings.
FIGURE 12.6  Border zone in the liver between histologically intact parenchyma (indicated by visible hepatic cords and intact nuclei [upper layer]) 
and a region exhibiting severe autolysis (post mortem tissue degeneration due to delayed tissue fixation [bottom layer]). Cells at the boundary between 
the two layers are beginning to degenerate as shown by nuclei with chromatin margination (arrow). Adult control mongrel dog.

Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
12.4.3.2.4  Diagnostic Nomenclature
The output of a microscopic evaluation is a list of microscopic 
diagnoses, which serves as the foundation for assembling the 
pathology data set. Since the objective of a microscopic evalu-
ation is to identify test article–related effects, microscopic 
diagnoses must be used consistently among all control and 
test article–exposed animals for a given study, and ideally 
across all studies for a given test article in a given test spe-
cies. This consistency is essential when attempting to identify 
test article–related effects using tabulated data summaries. 
Recognized standards for harmonized diagnostic nomencla-
ture have been established in recent years through interna-
tional consortia of expert toxicologic pathologists. The most 
recent and far-reaching of these efforts is the International 
Harmonization of Nomenclature and Diagnostic Criteria for 
Lesions (INHAND) initiative. In the past decade, this project 
has produced lexicons for major organs of rats and mice as 
well as companion terminology for non-rodent species includ-
ing dogs, minipigs, and NHPs.143,323 These lexicons have been 
incorporated by the FDA into SEND (Standard for Exchange 
of Nonclinical Data) terminology used in product registration 
packages since 2016 to help streamline the regulatory review 
process for new biomedical products.54
Microscopic diagnoses represent descriptions of morphologi-
cal features. Each diagnosis is assembled (and typically reported) 
as a string of hierarchical terms that specify major features 
about the location and nature of the finding. Key components in 
the hierarchy generally include the organ, pathological process, 
and a series of one or more qualifiers to better define the nature 
of the change. Common qualifiers communicate the affected 
subsite(s) within an organ or the distribution, duration, charac-
ter, and severity of the lesion.127 In general, the study patholo-
gist chooses which qualifiers to employ for a given diagnosis as 
a means of discriminating which changes might be related to 
test article exposure and thus be of toxicological significance.
The hierarchical approach to microscopic diagnosis is highly 
flexible and allows for a large universe of potential unique find-
ings. With the exception of the lesion severity, the combination 
of topography, process, and qualifier terms define distinct find-
ings. However, care must be taken not to employ an excessive 
number of diagnoses for the same change since the presence of 
many diagnoses for a single finding will affect the incidence in 
summary data tables and will complicate the interpretation. For 
example, study pathologists who are independently evaluating 
the same tissue sections may construct diagnoses for hepatocyte 
death in several ways, all of which define a common lesion for 
the same primary pathological process:
•	 Liver – Necrosis, coagulative, centrilobular, diffuse, 
acute, moderate
•	 Liver – Necrosis, coagulative, centrilobular, acute, 
moderate
•	 Liver – Necrosis, centrilobular, acute, moderate
•	 Liver – Necrosis, acute, moderate
•	 Liver – Necrosis, moderate
All of these diagnostic choices are acceptable as long as they 
are applied consistently by the study pathologist throughout 
the study. However, as constructed these diagnoses will be 
listed separately in the summary data tables, thus necessitat-
ing additional post-analytical review to consolidate the equiv-
alent diagnoses prior to interpretation. Separate diagnostic 
terms for a morphologically similar finding are warranted if 
their appearance is considered to be indicative of a different 
pathological process. For example, acute coagulative necrosis 
of the liver with a subcapsular rather than centrilobular distri-
bution is thought to reflect secondary compression of periph-
eral (subcapsular) hepatocytes due to global hepatic swelling 
rather than primary ischemia of cells in oxygen-depleted cen-
trilobular zones.7 The pathologist is responsible for assigning 
diagnoses that appropriately group lesions of similar location, 
morphology, and pathogenesis so that their relationship to test 
article exposure may be ascertained. This same issue of sub-
dividing findings with too many modifiers into a multitude of 
macroscopic lesions at necropsy, which is otherwise very simi-
lar, can lead to similar interpretive quandaries for the macro-
scopic findings.
Microscopic diagnoses for all organs associated with test 
article exposure usually are divided into two categories. The 
first is non-proliferative findings, which typically appear as 
cell injury (e.g., degeneration), loss (e.g., necrosis or apoptosis), 
or adaptation (e.g., atrophy or hypertrophy). In certain cases, 
complex lesions consisting of multiple morphologic features 
are given a single diagnosis rather than a series of diagnoses 
for each of the component parts (Figure 12.7). A key example 
of this approach is CPN, which combines such features as 
renal tubular degeneration and regeneration, basement mem-
brane thickening, and interstitial fibrosis and inflammation. 
The combined diagnosis is preferred to a series of separate 
diagnoses for the component parts since these related findings 
usually must be evaluated together to provide a meaningful 
interpretation.
The other common diagnostic category addresses prolif-
erative findings. These changes usually are considered to 
comprise a continuum ranging from hyperplasia to atypical 
(pre-neoplastic) hyperplasia to benign neoplasia to malignant 
neoplasia.111,370 The choice of proliferative diagnosis is based 
on morphologic features associated with a predicted biologi-
cal behavior. For example, benign tumors (Figure 12.8A) 
typically are well differentiated and expansive but not inva-
sive while malignant tumors (Figure 12.8B) generally are less 
differentiated to undifferentiated and invasive either locally or 
systemically (i.e., metastasis). However, these diagnoses are 
operational terms based on morphologic features observed at 
one point in time; the future progression of the lesion cannot 
be predicted with certainty unless metastasis is already evi-
dent. Lesions occurring secondary to the main process, such 
as inflammation, are generally not recorded (especially for 
neoplasms) as these secondary changes seldom provide useful 
information.
The most critical task for study pathologists is to dis-
criminate findings that exhibit superficial similarities in 
morphology but that have significantly different implica-
tions in terms of the risk posed by a test article. A classic 
example is separating leukocyte infiltration from inflamma-
tion within tissues. Infiltration (Figure 12.9A) occurs as one 
or more leukocyte clusters (typically mononuclear cells like

658
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
lymphocytes, histiocytes, and occasionally plasma cells) that 
are present within an organ but that do not induce any dam-
age to the adjacent parenchyma. Such infiltrates are frequent 
incidental findings in many organs (e.g., choroid plexus of 
the brain, kidney, liver) of healthy animals of most species, 
and their numbers may increase in other tissues following 
exposure to immunogenic test articles (e.g., meningeal infil-
trates following intrathecal administration of biomolecules). 
In contrast, inflammation (Figure 12.9B) reflects accumula-
tion of leukocytes that are associated with damage to nearby 
parenchyma. The pattern of inflammation seen in animals 
enrolled in toxicity studies varies with several experimental 
variables. Young animals used in shorter term studies gen-
erally have little or no inflammation as a background find-
ing but may develop clinically significant inflammation due 
to infections (e.g., contaminated catheters) or trauma (e.g., 
fight wounds, gavage errors). Older animals (especially for 
chronic studies [12 months or more] in rodents) may exhibit a 
substantial degree of inflammation affecting multiple organs, 
and the severity – as well as the degree of damage to paren-
chymal tissue – generally increases with age. The distinctly 
different mechanisms (or “pathogeneses”) and impacts of 
infiltration and inflammation necessitate that these findings 
be given different diagnoses.
FIGURE 12.7  Microscopic data tables are influenced by the choice of diagnostic terminology for simple vs. complex findings thought to represent 
different stages of a common disease process. (A) Cytoplasmic basophilia of renal tubules in the absence of other changes is the earliest microscopic 
finding in rats with chronic progressive nephropathy (CPN); the dotted circle encompasses a small number of infiltrating lymphocytes. (B) In a 
slightly more advanced stage of CPN, the complex focus is characterized by prominent mononuclear cell (mainly lymphocytic) inflammation sur-

(arrow). Depending on the pathologist, the two findings might receive different diagnoses (“basophilia” and “CPN”) or be lumped as a single diagnosis 
(“CPN”). Adult, male, control Sprague-Dawley rat.

tumors like this pulmonary adenoma usually are expansive but not invasive masses composed of well differentiated cells of a recognizable cell 
lineage (in this case bronchiolar epithelium). Mature (12-month-old) C57BL/6 mouse. (B) Malignant tumors like this glioma tend to invade (shown 
here [arrow] by dense plaques of cells spreading in the meninges) and consist of variably differentiated (and often poorly differentiated) cells. Aged 
(2-year-old) Wistar rat.

Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
12.4.3.2.5  Severity Grading
The last qualifier in a microscopic diagnosis generally is 
the severity grade. Such grades are semi-quantitative scores 
assigned to distinguish distinct levels of a finding, primarily 
for non-proliferative changes.299,310 Lesion grading is espe-
cially useful for discriminating test article–related effects that 
present as divergent severities rather than differing incidences 
between the control and treated animals. For instance, some 
test articles act in a dose-dependent manner to exacerbate the 
severity of CPN rather than increasing the incidence. When 
applied consistently using discrete criteria,139,225 severity 
grades provide a rapid and technically simple means for sort-
ing lesions. Semi-quantitative and quantitative methods often 
produce similar histopathologic data when used to evaluate the 
same tissue sections.53,310 In routine animal toxicity studies, 
semi-quantitative grading typically is utilized since it offers 
high throughput with good accuracy and no need for develop-
ing a digital imaging algorithm.
Severity grades should be definable, reproducible, and 
meaningful.127 Generally, severity grades should communicate 
the extent of the change within a tissue section, where “extent” 
is dictated by the amount of a finding based on its complex-
ity, distribution, and/or size. Grades should be assigned solely 
based on the microscopic appearance of the tissue and not 
with regard to any ancillary pathology data (e.g., gross obser-
vations, altered organ weights) or putative relationship to test 
article exposure, imputed biological significance, or supposed 
relevance to predicting possible human responses.299
As noted above for histopathologic diagnosis in general, 
severity grading is partially subjective (i.e., framed by the 
pathologist’s education and experience). Different patholo-
gists may devise distinct grading schemes based on similar or 
even identical microscopic features (Table 12.11). Therefore, 
the severity grades assigned by two or more pathologists who 
evaluate the same study, or even for a single pathologist assess-
ing the same study at different time points, will vary slightly 
(typically by one or occasionally two severity grades) but will 
exhibit the same general trend. Given this intrinsic variabil-
ity, the key to generating accurate and sensitive histopathol-
ogy data for routine toxicity studies is that the pathologist 
uses well-defined diagnostic criteria in a consistent manner 
across the entire study. For this reason, preferred practice for 
a toxicity study should be to have a single pathologist for the 
entire project. If necessary to speed the analysis (e.g., a 400-rat 
lifetime carcinogenicity study with a tight timeline for report 
finalization), multiple pathologists may be assigned to a single 
project, but each should assess a complete set of tissues. For 
example, one pathologist should assess all males and the other 
all females, or one pathologist should evaluate a particular sub-
set of functionally related organs from both sexes (e.g., endo-
crine, neural, sensory, and muscular organs) while the other 
examines a different subset of organs from both sexes (e.g., 
gastrointestinal, hematopoietic, lymphoid, and respiratory). In 
such instances, the consistency of severity grades across the 
entire study typically should be evaluated by a peer review 
pathologist, who usually will assess all tissues from a subset 
of high-dose animals of both sexes. As long as severity grades 
are consistently applied across the entire study, a one-grade 
difference of opinion between the study pathologist and a peer 
review pathologist is generally considered to be acceptable, 
and the study pathologist is responsible for assigning the final 
severity grade. Severity grades commonly are not applied to 
findings such as congenital anomalies, cysts, and neoplasms. 
Instead, such lesions are recorded as “Present.”
12.4.3.2.6  Diagnostic Thresholding
Pathologists typically employ diagnostic thresholds to dis-
tinguish expected spontaneous findings from possible test 
FIGURE 12.9  Superficially similar morphologic changes often have different implications for risk assessment. (A) Infiltration, evident here as a 
densely clustered sheath of lymphocytes surrounding a blood vessel in the superficial brain, is a leukocyte accumulation that does not damage nearby 
parenchyma. (B) Inflammation, seen here as a loosely packed sheath of lymphocytes surrounding a blood vessel in the deep brain with widespread 
migration of inflammatory cells into the adjacent tissue, is a leukocyte accumulation that injures the adjacent parenchyma. Neural injury is demon-
strated here by the presence of degenerating and apoptotic (arrow) cells. Leukocyte dispersion in the inflamed tissue is confirmed by comparing cell 
density near the ensheathed blood vessels with the much lower density in normal parenchyma (shown within the dotted box). Young adult cynomolgus 
monkey (A) and young adult Beagle dog (B).

Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
article–related findings. Thresholding is the practice of deter-
mining which variations in normal tissue morphology will be 
recorded and which variations will be assessed as falling below a 
threshold and thus will not be recorded. For example, one or two 
small clusters of lymphocytes are observed frequently around 
small radiating arteries in the kidneys of mature control animals 
of both sexes and all species; this finding will be observed but 
usually will not be recorded in most toxicity studies unless the 
test article may act to mobilize leukocytes or induce an immu-
nogenic or inflammatory response. Thresholding prevents over-
diagnosis, thereby removing irrelevant entries for normal tissue 
variations. This deliberate choice will both declutter the sum-
mary data tables and simplify the identification of relevant test 
article–related findings.127 That said, pathologists are careful in 
establishing thresholds as potential test article–related effects 
on the incidence and severity of subtle lesions may be hard to 
distinguish if normal findings are not recorded. Pathologists 
sometimes may develop the impression during the course of a 
study that a previously unrecorded change is being impacted 
by exposure to the test article. In such cases, the pathologist 
will adjust the threshold and then apply the updated version to 
re-evaluate previously viewed tissues as well as evaluate the 
remaining tissues for that study.
12.4.3.2.7  Diagnostic Drift
Inconsistent application of diagnostic nomenclature and/or 
severity grading schemes may occur during a single large, long-
term study (for a single study group or across several groups), 
or more often across several related studies performed over 
an extended period. This diagnostic drift typically is gradual 
and usually is evident as many diagnostic terms or a single 
term with several strings of qualifiers all used to identify the 
same fundamental pathological process. Such drift commonly 
reflects the growing appreciation by the study pathologist of the 
full spectrum of test article–related effects. Diagnostic drift is 
a source of variation that, if sufficiently marked, may falsely 
produce or hide test article–related changes. This source of 
diagnostic inconsistency typically is controlled by viewing sub-
sets of animals taken from across all treatment groups (e.g., five 
control, five high-dose, five low-dose, and five mid-dose ani-
mals), repeating such replicates until the entire study has been 
completed. If diagnostic drift is demonstrated for one or more 
tissues in a given study, this tissue (from all animals) should be 
re-evaluated over a short time frame to correct the data set.
12.4.3.2.8  Non-Blinded vs. Blinded 
Microscopic Evaluation
A decades-long exchange between pathologists and non-pathol-
ogists has debated the appropriate approach to designing the 
microscopic evaluation. Toxicologic pathologists generally 
advocate for a non-blinded (or informed) approach to histopath-
ologic analysis since this design maximizes the sensitivity for 
identifying and characterizing potential hazards.32,64,69,75,234,372 
This stance arises from the primary objective of routine tox-
icity studies as observational (descriptive) screens rather than 
formal tests of a scientific hypothesis. Accordingly, preferred 
practice is that the study pathologists will evaluate tissue sec-
tions for each animal with foreknowledge of the treatment

(control vs. test article), dose, and ancillary pathology data (e.g., 
gross observations, organ weights, and clinical pathology data). 
Existing GLP guidance and STP “best practice” recommenda-
tions acknowledge the importance of access by the pathologist 
to other study data during the microscopic evaluation for toxic-
ity studies.64,107,117 Additional data such as in-life clinical signs 
for the individual animal as well as TK and biodistribution find-
ings also are helpful to the pathologist as they may provide clues 
regarding other possible target organs.
On occasion, a blinded approach to microscopic evaluation 
(alternatively called a coded or masked or targeted evaluation) 
may prove useful when analyzing samples from toxicity stud-
ies. The most common use for blinded microscopic evalua-
tion is to verify preliminary diagnoses made during the initial 
non-blinded histopathologic analysis.32 For this purpose, the 
study pathologist assembles all sections of selected tissues 
(i.e., potential target organs) and then evaluates them without 
referring to the identifying information on the slide labels. 
This informal post hoc selective re-evaluation is utilized regu-
larly by toxicologic pathologists as a tool for establishing final 
microscopic diagnoses and threshold values (e.g., NOEL or 
NOAEL). Informal blinded re-evaluation is performed at the 
discretion of the study pathologist and usually is not docu-
mented in the pathology report.
Much less frequently, the initial microscopic evaluation 
may use a blinded approach. For toxicity studies, a blinded 
approach to microscopic analysis usually is selected to investi-
gate a specific hypothesis (e.g., to define whether a test article 
acts by a certain mechanism) or to address recommendations 
given in existing regulatory guidance (e.g., studies performed 
according to the FDA Animal Rule120) or particular questions 
raised by regulatory reviewers. In such cases, the choice to 
use a blinded approach for the initial microscopic evaluation 
typically is documented in the study protocol. Toxicity stud-
ies that incorporate a blinded approach for the initial micro-
scopic evaluation tend to have complicated data sets (due to 
both diagnostic drift and the inability to establish diagnostic 
thresholds to set a baseline for excluding normal variations); 
for studies using an initial blinded evaluation, it is of para-
mount importance that the findings to be assessed are well 
defined and evaluated using a pre-set grading scale. Moreover, 
such studies will be more expensive and take longer due to 
increased time needed for administrative tasks (e.g., slide cod-
ing and data decoding) as well as interpretation and reporting 
of complex data sets. If a blinded approach to initial micro-
scopic evaluation is selected for a toxicity study, toxicologic 
pathologists generally affirm that the only information with-
held from the study pathologist is the treatment and dose since 
cell and tissue alterations are best interpreted when integrated 
with ancillary pathology data.
12.5  Interpretation and Communication 
of Toxicologic Pathology Data
As with other data categories generated during toxicity studies, 
interpretation of pathology data is concerned primarily with 
discrimination of test article–related effects and determin-
ing if these findings are adverse. Where feasible, additional 
questions addressed by the pathology evaluation are to define 
whether the test article–related effects are direct or indirect 
results of exposure as well as to ascertain their implications 
at biologically relevant doses. In general, pathology reports 
often indicate whether specific findings are considered adverse 
or non-adverse. Interpretations with respect to adversity are 
made by the pathologist so that the study director, in consulta-
tion with the pathologist and other contributing scientists, can 
make the best decision on setting threshold levels (e.g., NOEL 
or NOAEL) in the study report.183
12.5.1  General Considerations
The interpretation and reporting of pathology data requires an 
integrated assessment of both anatomic pathology and clinical 
pathology changes.275 This task may be performed separately, 
by assembling individual pathology reports for anatomic 
pathology (i.e., gross observations, organ weights, and micro-
scopic findings) and clinical pathology (i.e., hematology, 
­coagulation, clinical chemistry, and urinalysis) endpoints, 
with subsequent integration of these parameters either by col-
laboration and cross referencing of reports or by doing this 
within the full study report by the study director. Alternatively, 
a single pathology report is generated that covers both ana-
tomic ­pathology and clinical pathology parameters; this com-
bined report may be written by a single pathologist or by a 
team of anatomic and clinical pathologists. The choice of 
which approach to use depends on institutional preferences. 
Regardless of the format, pathology findings must be consid-
ered in the context of other experimental factors (Table 12.5) 
that might influence cell and tissue responses including the 
study design (dose, duration, route of exposure, length of 
post-exposure recovery, mode of death), test system (species, 
stock/strain/breed, sex, age), and husbandry (diet, environ-
mental conditions, light cycle, population density).367 Care 
also must be taken to ensure the consistency of histopatho-
logic diagnoses, which fosters data accuracy and a meaningful 
interpretation.
12.5.2  Statistical Analysis
Statistics may be used to compare group means for certain 
pathology data generated during routine toxicity studies. Many 
different tests may be used depending on the kind of data,133,289 
a review of which is beyond the scope of this chapter but is 
available elsewhere in this book (see Chapter 9). The data from 
routine toxicity studies that are amenable to statistical analy-
sis include organ weights and (only in carcinogenicity studies) 
tumor incidences for the anatomic pathology results, and most 
of the clinical pathology parameters.
Statistical analysis of clinical pathology data is performed 
in toxicity studies with sufficient statistical power (typically 
non-routine studies to investigate specific questions rather than 
routine studies) and often identifies one or several statistically 
significant differences between control and treated groups. In 
contrast, statistical evaluation of anatomic pathology data for 
routine toxicity tests may be limited to organ weights and inci-
dences of rare neoplasms. Even if statistics are performed, the 
presence or absence of a statistically significant result should

662
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
be tempered by the pathologist’s assessment regarding the 
biological relevance of a finding. For example, small differences 
(µm) in placement of anchor points when acquiring linear mor-
phometric measurements may yield statistically significant but 
biologically inconsequential differences between group mean 
values.136 In contrast, inconsistent induction of an effect by a test 
article may present as a lack of statistical significance due to the 
combination of severely affected, mildly affected, and unaffected 
animals in a single group. In such cases, the biological impact 
of the test article may be best explored by evaluating individual 
animal data rather than by statistical analysis. This emphasis on 
individual animal data is especially important in short-term tox-
icity studies, which often have low statistical power due to small 
group sizes, and for endpoints whose measurements exhibit 
some degree of intrinsic variation. As the group size rises (e.g., 
rat studies with 15 or more animals per group), statistically sig-
nificant differences of very small magnitude that lack toxicologic 
relevance are detected more frequently.150
12.5.3  Implications of Pathology Findings
Interpretation of a pathology data set often presents a com-
plex challenge to the pathologist. Key aspects that should be 
considered include the relationship of test article exposure to 
the induction of pathological effects, whether such effects rep-
resent direct (primary) vs. indirect (secondary) responses or 
extreme manifestations of toxicity (i.e., seen following acute, 
high-dose experimental exposures in animals but unlikely 
during chronic, low-dose exposures anticipated for humans), 
which of the various pathological effects is responsible for 
death, and the ultimate biological significance of the tissue 
response (adverse or non-adverse). Answers to these questions 
are the main purpose for performing a comprehensive pathol-
ogy evaluation during a toxicity study.
12.5.3.1  Cause–Effect Relationships
Many factors suggest that a pathology finding may be related 
to test article exposure (Table 12.12).205 Major parameters to 
consider in making this interpretation include the presence (or 
absence) of a dose–response, the degree to which an effect for 
the dose group reflects an outlying response (from the group 
mean value as well as concurrent and historical control values) 
in one or two individuals, and the lack of biological plausibil-
ity (e.g., the effect is not consistent with the known effects or 
mechanism of action expected for that test article or chemi-
cally related agents). Other factors suggesting that an apparent 
effect might be a false-positive signal include intrinsic incon-
sistency of the analytical method (e.g., severity grading) and 
existence of the putative test article–related effect within the 
normal range of biological variation encountered during past 
studies. In general, these factors must be considered together 
rather than in isolation, and the impression gained from a 
given study’s data set must be integrated with the outcomes 
observed for the same or related test articles during prior stud-
ies (i.e., a “weight-of-evidence” approach).
Extreme values (or “outliers”) for one or a few animals of 
a treatment group may be viewed as instances of individual-
specific biological variation unrelated to test article exposure. 
This interpretation is especially likely when assessing clinical 
pathology data sets where the presence of one or two outlier 
values is a fairly frequent occurrence; this interpretation may 
be moderated if the outlier value falls within the bounds of 
the reference interval. Outliers that can be shown to arise as a 
technical error or as an outcome linked to a pathological event 
unrelated to test article exposure should be removed from the 
analysis. However, the relevance of statistical outliers should 
be approached with care to ensure that they do not represent 
low-incidence occurrences of test article–related effects. For 
this reason, a “weight-of-evidence” approach (e.g., match-
ing an outlier clinical pathology response to the microscopic 
appearance of the corresponding tissue section) is essential 
when deciding whether or not to include or exclude outliers in 
interpreting the integrated data.16,64
Cause–effect relationships are best probed by comparison 
to findings observed in concurrent control animals. However, 
in some cases (e.g., for rare neoplasms90 or where concurrent 
control data are widely dispersed), a sufficiently powered ref-
erence interval or robust set of HCD may provide a superior 
benchmark against which the putative test article–related 
effect may be judged. HCD should not be used to discount 
unwanted or difficult findings.205
