12.5.3.2  Direct vs. Indirect Effects
Findings associated with test article exposure may be direct 
or indirect effects. Direct (or primary) effects represent target 
organ toxicity that results from specific alterations (biochemi-
cal, molecular, or sometimes mechanical) induced by a test 
article in a target cell population. Indirect effects reflect sys-
temic toxicity where direct injury to cells in a target organ 
cause physiological (biochemical and/or molecular) alterations 
in other cells at distant sites. Discriminating between direct 
and indirect effects may have important implications for haz-
ard classification and risk assessment.
Direct and indirect effects may be observed grossly or micro-
scopically. For example, direct toxicity in key organs that pro-
duce malaise in a test animal may reduce food consumption, 
thus leading indirectly to decreases in total body weight as well 
as absolute and often relative organ weights. Failure to consider 
the relationship between body weight and organ weights may 
lead to misinterpretation of organ weight alterations. A frequent 
setting in which such considerations are paramount is defin-
ing the meaning of relative organ weights. Absolute weights 
of certain organs in adults (e.g., brain and testes) are relatively 
unaffected by modest reductions in body weight, while absolute 
weights of others (e.g., liver and thyroid gland) fluctuate to some 
degree with changes in body weight.309 Therefore, relative organ 
weights (i.e., organ weight as a percentage of total body weight) 
will respond differently in the face of body weight changes. 
Organs with fairly constant absolute organ weights will appear 
to have increased relative weights as body weight falls, while 
organs with malleable absolute organ weights tend to main-
tain unaffected organ-to-body weight ratios as body weight 
declines. In the absence of other evidence indicating direct 
injury to organs exhibiting altered relative weights, altered rela-
tive weight alone should not be interpreted as confirmation of 
primary toxicity in that organ. However, an increase in relative 
liver weight in association with decreasing body weight may 
indicate a primary effect on liver (i.e., hepatocytes [as the main 
cell population contributing to liver mass]).21,254
Many microscopic changes are indicative of indirect effects 
(systemic toxicity) rather than direct effects, and their differ-
entiation from actual primary toxicity may confound inter-
pretation of test article–related changes. The nature of these 
indirect effects depends on the affected organ. For example, 
atrophy of female reproductive organs is a common indirect 
microscopic finding of nonspecific systemic toxicity (e.g., 
reduced food intake, stress).291 Similarly, systemic stress seen 
at higher doses of test articles may enhance circulating levels 
of glucocorticoids nonspecifically, which subsequently will 
cause lymphocyte death and lower weights in lymphoid organs 
(especially thymus). Differentiation of primary immunomodu-
lation from a generalized stress response may be challenging, 
but the dose–response profile may be helpful in clarifying the 
relationship. For example, immunosuppressive agents often 
produce dose-related direct effects in lymphoid organs with 
no substantial changes in other organs. In contrast, immuno-
suppression as an indirect effect of systemic stress typically 
induces effects in lymphoid organs chiefly at higher doses, and 
often in association with other signs of systemic toxicity such 
as weight loss or failure to gain weight.144,291
Careful consideration of integrated physiological processes 
may be helpful in distinguishing direct (primary) from indi-
rect effects. For example, renal tubular epithelial necrosis as 
a direct toxic effect will alter renal excretion of calcium in 
the urine. The resulting indirect increase in circulating cal-
cium levels may drive soft tissue calcification (or “metastatic 
mineralization”) in normal tissues at other sites (e.g., gastric 
mucosa). This secondary effect may lead to destruction of 
mucosal epithelial cells, which might induce an additional 
tertiary indirect effect since loss of mucosal mass can alter 
gastric acid release and food digestion. The primary effect on 
the kidney epithelium would be the key finding with respect to 
risk assessment for this test article.
12.5.3.3  Extrapolation of Extreme Effects
Some microscopic changes, including neoplasia, develop only 
following exposure to high doses of test article. The biologi-
cal explanation for such findings is that normal physiological 
defense mechanisms have been overwhelmed (e.g., insufficient 
DNA repair following exposure to mutagenic chemicals) or are 
exuberant (e.g., chronic increase in cell proliferation to regener-
ate tissue after severe and sustained cytotoxicity). In mechanis-
tic terms, underlying changes related to extreme toxicity often 
manifest as direct (primary) degeneration and necrosis followed 
by a secondary response that either drives cell turnover (e.g., pro-
longed regenerative hyperplasia in liver71,162) or causes further 
tissue damage (e.g., chronic inflammation in the lung related to 
inhaled particulates352). The presence of such secondary effects 
permits the test article as well as other exogenous and endoge-
nous toxicants (e.g., reactive oxygen species) to maintain or even 
magnify the severity of many high-dose effects.
Extrapolation of extreme pathological effects for hazard 
identification and risk assessment requires care in interpre-
tation. For example, proliferative lesions such as hyperplasia 
or neoplasia have different implications if they arise as direct 
test article–induced toxicity or as an indirect (but appropri-
ate) regenerative response to cell degeneration or necrosis. 
If hyperplasia can be linked unmistakably to direct toxicity, 
then exposures that do not induce the primary toxic event are 
unlikely to cause tumors in the affected tissue.38 Similarly, 
extreme effects observed at high doses may be harmful (i.e., 
adverse) to a test animal, but their relevance for hazard identifi-
cation is uncertain as real-world exposures typically will occur 
at lower doses. Thus, a major element of the pathologist’s inter-
pretation is to characterize the spectrum of findings across all 
dose levels so that extreme effects may be positioned properly 
with respect to more relevant test article–related changes.
12.5.3.4  Assessing Cause of Death
Defining the COD in individual animals is a principal consid-
eration in the pathology evaluation for toxicity studies. The 
pathologist is responsible for determining the COD in ani-
mals that die prior to the scheduled necropsy, whether they 
are observed to be in moribund condition (requiring eutha-
nasia for humane reasons) or are found dead. The pathologist 
also will assess overall mortality for all groups to identify

664
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
possible differences in mortality related to test article expo-
sure. Assigned CODs and their relationship to exposure should 
be incorporated in the pathology report.208
Assigning a COD requires comprehensive knowledge regard-
ing general biology (especially physiology and physiological 
chemistry) as well as familiarity with species-specific biological 
attributes that contribute to health and disease. In addition, pro-
fessional judgment tempered by ample experience is necessary 
to make COD decisions. In assessing parameters responsible for 
death and moribundity, the pathologist will need access to all 
study data for the individual animal including clinical observa-
tions, gross findings, organ weights, clinical pathology values, 
and microscopic changes. In many studies, the pathologist over-
seeing the necropsy (where gross observations are made) may 
be different from the study pathologist performing the micro-
scopic evaluation and ultimate pathology data interpretation, 
so use of standard diagnostic terminology for gross findings is 
necessary to permit integration of the final pathology data set. 
In the end, the decision regarding the COD and/or moribundity 
boils down to determining the single primary fatal condition 
responsible for the animal’s unscheduled demise; the primary 
mechanism may be an incidental background disease (e.g., CPN 
in male rats or pituitary adenomas in rats of both sexes110,153) or 
a consequence of test article exposure. If several potential CODs 
are evident, the one deemed to be the most likely precipitating 
event should be selected.
In many studies, one or several animals will experience 
unscheduled deaths in the absence of a clear COD. This phe-
nomenon results from COD that do not produce structural 
changes that can be seen during the macroscopic and micro-
scopic evaluations. For example, sudden seizures may be fatal 
due to electrical and neurochemical disturbances in the brain, 
but the anatomic changes that might serve to demonstrate 
occurrence of a lethal seizure generally take 6 hours or more 
to begin and several days to mature.135 If a COD cannot be 
assigned from the information available for analysis, the COD 
should be listed as “undetermined.” Even though the COD for 
some animals may be considered “undetermined,” the overall 
incidence of mortality in a study could still cause the patholo-
gist to attribute them to the test article even if the mechanism 
remains undetermined.
12.5.3.5  Adversity Determinations
Another key consideration when interpreting pathology data is 
whether or not changes are adverse or non-adverse. Many defi-
nitions of adversity have been presented. The definition in the 
STP “best practice” recommendations for determining, com-
municating, and using adverse effect data from nonclinical 
studies is “[a]dversity is a term indicating harm to the test ani-
mal.”183 In formal terms, the European Society of Toxicologic 
Pathology (ESTP) has devised a working definition based on 
biological attributes where 
an adverse effect is a test [article]-related change in 
the morphology, physiology, growth, development, 
reproduction or life span of the animal model that 
likely results in an impairment of functional capac-
ity to maintain homeostasis and/or an impairment of 
the capacity to respond to an additional challenge.258 
Any test article–related changes that cause no harm are non-
adverse by definition.
Many factors will be considered in making adversity deci-
sions (Table 12.13). In general, these factors should be weighed 
together rather than considered in isolation. While adversity 
decisions generally are made on a case-by-case basis, several 
general features may be understood when considering the impli-
cations of a pathology finding.183 Adversity as stated in a non-
clinical study report is applicable only to the test species and 
specifically for the conditions used for that study. Adversity 
decisions with respect to test article–related effects should be 
made on the basis of actual observations or measurements in 
the test species and not on speculation about potential biological 
processes and their possible relevance as predictors of toxicity 
TABLE 12.13
Discriminating Factors for Assessing Adversity
Interpretationa
Consideration
Adverse
The effect (functional and/or structural) is substantial
The effect is an increased incidence of tumors 
The effect cannot be repaired or resolved (e.g., neuronal necrosis)
Non-adverse (usually)
The effect does not alter the function of the affected organ or the affected animal
The effect is not a precursor lesion (i.e., an early stage of a continuum of changes known to progress with time to an 
established adverse effect)
The effect is transient (i.e., recedes during the course of test article exposure)
The effect is reversible (i.e., resolves upon cessation of test article exposure)
The effect is isolated (no other test article–related findings are present) 
The effect is independent (i.e., not associated with changes in other parameters that often occur in combination with the 
effect in question) 
The effect is an indirect (secondary) response to direct (primary) toxicity at another site
The effect is not a consequence of test article exposure (e.g., findings induced by stress associated with the experimental 
procedure or that occur as incidental background findings)
Adaptive
The effect is an expected appropriate response that permits the affected cells to sustain relatively normal function in the 
presence of ongoing test article exposure
The effect is modest
a	 Parameters were adapted in part from several sources.10,11,14

665
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
in other species. The presence of abnormal, test article–related 
clinical pathology values in isolation typically are not adverse, 
regardless of their magnitude276; instead clinical pathology 
changes generally are part of a constellation of findings that 
collectively are defined as adverse (e.g., increased serum ALT 
and AST activities in combination with hepatocyte necrosis). 
The need to extrapolate adversity decisions regarding pathol-
ogy findings in a given test species for risk assessment in other 
species (e.g., humans) is irrelevant in deciding whether or not a 
finding observed in a toxicity study will be judged as adverse. 
A few clinical pathology changes may be considered adverse 
despite having no other correlates, such as severe decreases in 
platelet counts where the biological outcome is expected to be a 
predisposition to catastrophic bleeding accidents.
Certain features of pathology findings are more likely to 
support a non-adverse designation, even if their presence is 
related to test article exposure. For instance, an altered value 
for a given parameter that falls within the normal limits of 
biological variation (as shown by robust HCD) often are indic-
ative of a non-adverse change. Similarly test article–related 
adaptive responses (i.e., appropriate changes in function and/
or structure that permit an organism’s cells to sustain relatively 
normal function in the presence of ongoing test article expo-
sure181,183) often are seen as non-adverse. A classic example 
of this phenomenon is test article–related induction of cyto-
chromes P450 drug–metabolizing enzymes, which leads to 
progressive cell hypertrophy of centrilobular hepatocytes 
(Figure 12.5) and an increased capacity for systemic clear-
ance of many small molecules. Over time, increasing hyper-
trophy may constrict blood flow through the affected hepatic 
lobules, thus leading to minimal cell death of isolated cells. 
Both the initial modest hypertrophy and the later occurrence 
of scattered cell death typically are deemed to be non-adverse. 
However, continued hypertrophy resulting in exuberant cell 
death – usually manifesting as multiple, variably sized foci of 
necrosis – generally would be judged to be adverse under the 
conditions of the study.
12.5.4  Reporting Pathology Findings
The pathology data and their interpretation must be stated in 
the study report. The coverage should be accurate, focused, 
and complete. The pathology data may be communicated in 
several ways. One choice is for the pathologist to prepare a 
stand-alone pathology report in which all findings are inte-
grated using an interpretive narrative and relevant summary 
and individual animal data tables. Some institutions produce 
a single report in which the clinical pathology and anatomic 
pathology findings are discussed together, while other orga-
nizations generate separate clinical pathology and anatomic 
pathology reports. Key messages in such stand-alone pathology 
reports will be inserted in the narrative of the full study report, 
and the complete pathology report(s) signed by the pathologist 
will be attached as an appendix to the final study report. Other 
institutions may elect to prepare a comprehensive study report 
in which the pathology data may be included as a part of the 
general presentation. In the latter case, the final study report 
may be signed by both the study director and the pathologist. 
All these options are acceptable as long as the pathology find-
ings are integrated with the remainder of the study data and 
the interpretation of the pathology data as supplied by the 
pathologist is not changed as it is introduced into the full study 
report.229 In the authors’ experience, preparation of stand-alone 
pathology reports that are appended to the final study report 
is utilized more often. Some recommended best practices for 
pathology report generation are given in Table 12.14.
TABLE 12.14
Best Practices for Pathology Report Generation in Animal Toxicity Studies
Taska

The study pathologist(s) should contribute to generating the study protocol
The study pathologist(s) should have access to the study protocol and all protocol amendments
Individual responsibility of the study pathologist(s) should be defined in the study protocol

The study pathologist(s) should have access to all study data including
•	 Group assignmentsb (i.e., control or test article–treated [with dose]) 
•	 The intended effect of the test article (e.g., pharmacologic target and mechanism of action)
•	 The tissue distribution of the pharmacologic target (from the current and/or prior studies)
•	 Kinetic data related to test article distribution and clearance (from the current and/or prior studies)
•	 In-life study data
•	 Gross observations
•	 Organ weight data
•	 Clinical pathology data

interpretation and report preparation

ancillary information (e.g., molecular pathology and ultrastructural findings)

Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
Well-written pathology reports are both descriptive and 
interpretive and will include a number of essential details.17,64 
First, the tabular data will supply a comprehensive list of all 
pathology findings including mortality incidence, macro-
scopic changes, organ weights, clinical pathology values, and 
microscopic diagnoses (with severity grades). In general, the 
summary tables will show group mean values for all con-
trol and treated groups in a side-by-side format to facilitate 
identification of potential test article–related effects. The 
individual animal data tables usually will show all pathol-
ogy data and (for unscheduled deaths) the assigned COD; 
with some reporting systems, the individual clinical obser-
vations may be included as well. The narrative will briefly 
review the methods relevant to the pathology endpoints and 
then describe the main pathology findings and the patholo-
gist’s conclusions regarding their meaning. Important points 
to address in the narrative include potential target organs and 
major test article–related changes for the study, the dose–
response relationship, and whether the test article–related 
changes are judged to be adverse.
12.5.5  QA and Pathology Peer Review
Pathology data sets for most toxicity studies include a mixture 
of completely objective measurements (e.g., clinical pathol-
ogy values, organ weights) and partially subjective observa-
tions (e.g., gross changes, microscopic diagnoses and severity 
grades). Because pathology data are essential in making regu-
latory decisions, the global pathology community has devel-
oped a number of procedures for ensuring that pathology data 
are accurate and their interpretation is clear. These quality 
review procedures are performed for many but not all stud-
ies and are applicable to both GLP-compliant and non-GLP 
experiments. The procedures discussed below describe the 
processes employed for GLP-compliant toxicity studies.
12.5.5.1  Pathology Data Review
This review step is undertaken to ensure the quality of the 
materials and procedures used to generate pathology data, 
especially microscopic data. The pathology data review is 
conducted most often by a QA unit. Pathology materials 
subject to review include IANRs, histology laboratory work-
sheets, archived fixed tissues, blocks, and glass slides. The 
pathology materials subject to review vary depending on 
the institution. At the U.S. National Toxicology Program, a 
complete (100%) inventory of wet-tissue containers (bags or 
sometimes buckets), blocks, and slides is undertaken to show 
that all materials are properly labeled and that all slides can 
be matched to their blocks; this extensive review typically 
is not performed for routine toxicity studies performed by 
contract research organizations (CROs). A randomly chosen 
subset of slides may be examined macroscopically and micro-
scopically to evaluate slide quality, including coverslipping, 
tissue placement and orientation, stain, and the presence or 
absence of artifacts in tissue sections. A randomly selected 
subset of archived wet tissues should also be examined to 
verify the animal identification number, which usually will 
be present as a metal tag, microchip, or tattoo. Residual wet 
tissue may be examined by a pathologist to ensure that no 
other gross lesions are present that were either not identified 
at necropsy or not trimmed. Items that may be discovered 
in the data review include untrimmed lesions, incorrectly 
identified animals, and slides that do not correspond to their 
respective blocks.212
12.5.5.2  Pathology Peer Review
This process involves review by a second pathologist of a 
subset of tissues or pathology findings generated during the 
initial pathology evaluation by the original pathologist. In 
this context, the original pathologist is known as the Study 
Pathologist, and the second pathologist is termed the Peer 
Review Pathologist.87,212 Global GLP regulations do not 
require pathology peer review,107,117,243 but properly imple-
mented peer reviews are appreciated by regulatory agencies 
around the world.124 The study and peer review pathologists 
perform distinct roles in toxicity studies. The study patholo-
gist generates a set of preliminary histopathologic diagnoses, 
while the peer review pathologist cross-checks their accuracy 
and consistency. Importantly, the peer review pathologist does 
not generate pathology raw data by their review.117
The primary purpose of pathology peer review is to sub-
stantiate the accuracy of diagnoses, severity grades, and 
­interpretations for key microscopic findings. Such peer reviews 
verify the integrity of the pathology evaluation and promote 
clear communication through the consistent application of 
diagnostic terminology and grading criteria. Documented 
pathology peer reviews also increase the confidence of regu-
latory agencies in the pathology portion of study reports.351 
Pathology peer review is recommended when critical busi-
ness and/or risk assessment decisions may hinge on pathology 
interpretations in toxicity studies, especially GLP-compliant 
toxicity and carcinogenicity bioassays. Pathology peer review 
also may be beneficial for non-GLP studies that inform test 
article development decisions such as selecting among lead 
candidates, dose ranges, and routes of administration.230
The pathology peer review may be informal (undocumented 
consultations) or formal, and a formal peer review may be 
prospective (“contemporaneous”) or retrospective. Informal 
peer reviews usually involve a pathologist showing a slide 
(or image) to a colleague to confirm a diagnosis and/or its 
potential implications. The formal prospective peer review 
is undertaken prior to finalization of the study. The prospec-
tive peer review is established as a distinct sub-section in the 
original study protocol (or by later protocol amendment), and 
the results of the peer review are documented in the final 
pathology report (signed by the study pathologist) and usually 
a pathology peer review certificate or memorandum (signed 
by the peer review pathologist). The formal retrospective peer 
review generally occurs after the study was finalized, so the 
findings by the peer review pathologist will be documented in 
a separate report and/or the pathology report may be amended 
to reflect the consensus.212
Formal pathology peer reviews typically employ several 
organizational principles.230 First, the peer review pathologist 
will work independently from the study pathologist during the 
course of the review. Second, the peer review pathologist is

667
Pathology Principles and Practices for Toxicity Studies
responsible for establishing the scope of the review; impor-
tantly, the peer review is not intended to corroborate every 
microscopic finding but instead is focused on the examina-
tion of a portion of the tissue sections as well as the pathol-
ogy data tables and narrative. Third, peer reviews generally 
should be done by pathologists with complete access to the 
original pathology data (i.e., a non-blinded review). Fourth, 
reviews typically begin with analysis of tissues from subsets 
(often 30% of rodents and 50% or more of non-rodents of 
both sexes) of the high-dose and control groups; a higher per-
centage of non-rodents are reviewed since group sizes are 
small. Animals selected for this initial portion of the review 
usually are chosen at random, while the later stages of the 
review are deliberately focused on key findings. All puta-
tive test article–related target organs identified by the study 
pathologist are reviewed for as many high- and intermedi-
ate-dose groups as are necessary to confirm the NOAEL for 
that finding. In addition, all neoplasms are reviewed. Finally, 
at the end of a prospective peer review, the final diagnosis 
and interpretation listed in the pathology report should rep-
resent the consensus of the study pathologist and the peer 
review pathologist. The certificate describing the scope of 
the pathology peer review should document key study details 
(e.g., title, study number, test article designation), date(s) of 
the peer review, the tissues and study documentation avail-
able for analysis, the methods employed in the review, and 
any actions taken to resolve differences of opinion between 
the two pathologists. Any interim notes prepared by the peer 
review pathologist during the course of the review need not 
be retained as the pathology raw data are the consensus diag-
noses included in the final signed pathology report.
12.5.5.3  Pathology Working Groups (PWG)
A PWG is a panel of expert pathologists who assemble to 
review a specific question raised in or about a toxicity study. 
These panels typically are conducted for finalized studies but 
may be convened to assess pivotal or controversial findings for 
ongoing studies. In general, PWGs address issues of concern 
to regulatory agencies such as harmonizing outdated diag-
nostic terms with recently revised nomenclature, deliberating 
the implications of newly recognized lesions, or comparing 
the results reported for a test article as compiled from many 
studies (often performed at several laboratories and evaluated 
by multiple pathologists). A PWG also may be assembled to 
resolve intractable differences between a study pathologist and 
peer review pathologist with respect to diagnostic criteria, ter-
minology, and/or interpretation.
As with pathology peer reviews by a single peer review 
pathologist, a PWG is organized by several fundamental prin-
ciples.213 A PWG reviews a subset of tissue sections and not 
the entire study. The materials for PWG review are chosen by 
the PWG chair, an expert pathologist who also will serve as 
the principal author of the PWG report. The PWG members 
may evaluate coded slides (i.e., a blinded review with regard 
to the prior diagnoses and possibly to treatment group as well) 
and then arrive at a final diagnosis for each finding by major-
ity vote. A PWG also generally provides nuanced discussion 
regarding the interpretation of the final diagnoses.
12.5.5.4  Histopathology Data Collection 
and the Audit Trail
Formal definitions of raw data vary among different regula-
tory jurisdictions.107,117,243 With respect to routine toxicity 
studies, data resulting from the microscopic evaluation rep-
resent original histopathologic observations of findings in tis-
sue sections mounted on glass slides. The task of generating 
histopathologic diagnoses is an iterative (step-wise) process 
whereby a study pathologist may refine an initial diagnosis 
through further evaluation until satisfied with the final result. 
Furthermore, histopathologic observations generally are based 
on the evaluation of tissue sections (specimens) that are per-
manently mounted on durable slides. Because of this perma-
nency, the slides can be re-evaluated at any future date by a 
pathologist or team of pathologists to confirm the interpreta-
tion. Therefore, the histopathology raw data for a given study is 
represented by the interpretation in the final, signed and dated 
anatomic pathology report, and is supported by the final set of 
diagnoses present in that pathology report.117 All prior work-
sheets and notations used in reaching the final diagnoses are 
interim notes that may be discarded once the report is signed 
as such notes are not needed to reconstruct the final report 
since (as noted previously) the slides can be re-evaluated at any 
time by additional experts.117
Concerns by regulators regarding the potential for “undue 
influence” from sponsors with respect to the generation of 
histopathology data has influenced the timing of data locking 
and initiation of the histopathology audit trail. Data may be 
locked prior to sponsor peer review (i.e., a typical practice in 
Japan), or pathology peer review may be completed to achieve 
diagnostic consensus before the data are locked (i.e., the usual 
approach in Europe and the United States for prospective peer 
reviews). Modern GLP-compliant, validated systems to sup-
port electronic collection of histopathology data have security 
measures that protect data integrity during both the data entry 
and reporting processes. As interim notes (i.e., pre-locking 
initial microscopic observations) are not raw data necessary 
for study reconstruction, diagnostic changes made prior to 
data locking will not be tracked. The audit trail is activated 
once the data are locked, and any subsequent changes will be 
tracked fully with respect to who, what, when, and why an 
adjustment was made.338
12.6  Conclusions
Pathology data are core components produced in conventional 
toxicity studies. In many cases, one or more adverse pathology 
findings is instrumental in establishing threshold values (e.g., 
NOEL or NOAEL) used to position toxicity data sets for risk 
assessment. The current chapter has endeavored to provide 
broad introduction to the discipline of toxicologic pathology, 
the relevant tools and data categories, and the implications of 
many key pathology findings. In addition, the contents of this 
chapter should aid toxicologists and allied scientists in gaining 
a better appreciation for how pathology data may be integrated 
with other kinds of toxicity data. Finally, the explanations 
for pathology data interpretation and communication should

668
Hayes’ Principles and Methods of Toxicology
be helpful in understanding the key messages contained in 
pathology reports.
12.7  Questions
	 11.1  The most appropriate time for collecting and evalu-
ating clinical pathology samples (e.g., hematology 
and clinical chemistry endpoints) in single-dose 
studies is:
	
a.	 12–24 hour post-dose
	
b.	 48–72 hour post-dose
	
c.	 5–7 day post-dose
	
d.	 14-day post-dose
	
Answer: b
	 11.2  Tissue destined for histopathologic evaluation 
should be prepared as follows to provide suitable 
cellular preservation:
	
a.	 Tissues except eyes and testes should be 1 cm 
or less in thickness to aid fixative penetration 
(based on 24-hour entry of neutral buffered 
10% formalin)
	
b.	 The ratio of fixative-to-tissue volumes should 
be at least 10:1
	
c.	 Eyes and testes should be fixed intact in Bouin’s, 
Davidson’s, or modified Davidson’s solution
	
d.	 All of the above
	
Answer: 	d
	 11.3  Historical control data for histopathologic (micro-
scopic) raw data from toxicity studies:
	
a.	 are deemed to be more reliable if they are com-
piled from studies that underwent pathology 
peer review
	
b.	 should only be developed for neoplasms
	
c.	 typically do not contribute to the weight of evi-
dence for data interpretation
	
d.	 may be the main reason for interpreting differ-
ences between concurrent control and test arti-
cle–treated animals as genuine and/or adverse 
effects
	
Answer: a
